Trying to Time the Market
Market timing is one of the all-time great myths of investing. There is no
strategy that consistently tells you when to be in the market and when to be
out of it, and anyone who says otherwise usually has a market-timing service
to sell you.
Consider an interesting study in the February Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú±issue of Financial An-
alysts Journal, which looked at the difference between buy-and-hold and mar-
ket-timing strategies from Ôú±ÔúπÔú≤Ôú∂through Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔúπusing a very elegant method.
The authors essentially mapped all of the possible market-timing variations
between Ôú±ÔúπÔú≤Ôú∂and Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔúπwith different switching frequencies.Ôú¥
They assumed that for any given month, an investor could be either in
T-bills or in stocks and then calculated the returns that would have resulted
from all of the possible combinations of those switches. (For the curious,
there are Ôú≤Ôú±Ôú≤‚Äîor Ôú¥,Ôú∞ÔúπÔú∂‚Äîpossible combinations between two assets over 
Ôú±Ôú≤months.) Then they compared the results of a buy-and-hold strategy
with all of the possible market-timing strategies to see what percentage 
of the timing combinations produced a return greater than simply buying
and holding.
The answer? About one-third of the possible monthly market-timing
combinations beat the buy-and-hold strategy. You may be thinking, ‚ÄúI have a
Ôú≥Ôú≥percent chance of beating the market if I try to time it. I‚Äôll take those
odds!‚Äù But before you run out and subscribe to some timing service, consider
three issues:
Ôú±. The results in the paper cited previously overstate the benefits of timing
because they looked at each year as a discrete period‚Äîwhich means they
ignore the benefits of compounding (as long as you assume that the mar-
ket will generally rise over long periods of time, that is).
Ôú≤. Stock market returns are highly skewed‚Äîthat is, the bulk of the returns
(positive and negative) from any given year comes from relatively few
days in that year. This means that the risk of not being in the market is
high for anyone looking to build wealth over a long period of time.
Ôú¥Richard J. Bauer Jr. and Julie R. Dahlquist, ‚ÄúMarket Timing and Roulette Wheels,‚Äù Financial
Analysts Journal, ÔúµÔú∑(Ôú±), pp. Ôú≤Ôú∏‚ÄíÔú¥Ôú∞.
Ôú±Ôú∏
Ôù≥Ôù•Ôù∂Ôù•ÔùÆ Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù´Ôù•Ôù≥ Ôù¥ÔùØ Ôù°Ôù∂ÔùØÔù©Ôù§
Ôú≥. Not a single one of the thousands of funds Morningstar has tracked over
the past two decades has been able to consistently time the market. Sure,
some funds have made the occasional great call, but none have posted any
kind of superior track record by jumping frequently in and out of the
market based on the signals generated by a quantitative model.
That‚Äôs pretty powerful evidence that market timing is not a viable strategy
because running a mutual fund is a very profitable business‚Äîif someone had
figured out a way to reliably time the market, you can bet your life they‚Äôd
have started a fund to do so.
Ignoring Valuation
This one came back to haunt many people over the past few years. Although
it‚Äôs certainly possible that another investor will pay you ÔúµÔú∞times earnings
down the road for the company you just bought for Ôú≥Ôú∞times earnings, that‚Äôs
a very risky bet to make. Sure, you could have made a ton of money in
CMGI or Yahoo! during the Internet bubble, but only if you had gotten out
in time. Can you honestly say to yourself that you would have?
The only reason you should ever buy a stock is that you think the business
is worth more than it‚Äôs selling for‚Äînot because you think a greater fool will
pay more for the shares a few months down the road.
We‚Äôll talk a lot more about the concept of intrinsic value and valuation
more in Chapters Ôúπand Ôú±Ôú∞. For now, just remember that the best way to mit-
igate your investing risk is to pay careful attention to valuation. If the mar-
ket‚Äôs expectations are low, there‚Äôs a much greater chance that the company
you purchase will exceed them. Buying a stock on the expectation of positive
news flow or strong relative strength is asking for trouble.
Relying on Earnings for the Whole Story
At the end of the day, cash flow is what matters, not earnings. For a host of
reasons, accounting-based earnings per share can be made to say just about
whatever a company‚Äôs management wants them to, but cash flow is much
harder to fiddle with. The statement of cash flows can yield a ton of insight
into the true health of a business, and you can spot a lot of blowups before
they happen by simply watching the trend of operating cash flow relative to
Ôù©ÔùÆÔù∂Ôù•Ôù≥Ôù¥ÔùØÔù≤‚ÄôÔù≥ Ôù£Ôù®Ôù•Ôù£Ôù´Ôù¨Ôù©Ôù≥Ôù¥
Ôú±Ôúπ
earnings. One hint: If operating cash flows stagnate or shrink even as earn-
ings grow, it‚Äôs likely that something is rotten.
We‚Äôll talk more about the importance of analyzing cash flow in Chapters Ôúµ
and Ôú∂and I‚Äôll show you how to use it to detect potential accounting problems
in Chapter Ôú∏.
If you can avoid these common mistakes, you‚Äôll be miles ahead of the av-
erage investor. Now, let‚Äôs move from what you shouldn‚Äôt do to what you
should do. In the next chapter, I‚Äôll show you how to separate great companies
from average ones by analyzing economic moats.
Investor‚Äôs Checklist: Seven Mistakes to Avoid
3 Don‚Äôt try to shoot for big gains by finding the next Microsoft. Instead,
focus on finding solid companies with shares selling at low valuations.
3 Understanding the market‚Äôs history can help you avoid repeated pitfalls.
If people try to convince you that ‚Äúit really is different this time,‚Äù ignore
them.
3 Don‚Äôt fall into the all-too-frequent trap of assuming that a great product
translates into a high-quality company. Before you get swept away by ex-
citing new technology or a nifty product, make sure you‚Äôve checked out
the company‚Äôs business model.
3 Don‚Äôt be afraid to use fear to your advantage. The best time to buy is
when everyone else is running away from a given asset class.
3 Attempting to time the market is a fool‚Äôs game. There‚Äôs ample evidence
that the market can‚Äôt be timed.
3 The best way to reduce your investment risk is to pay careful attention to
valuation. Don‚Äôt make the mistake of hoping that other investors will keep
paying higher prices, even if you‚Äôre buying shares in a great company.
3 Cash flow is the true measure of a company‚Äôs financial performance, not
reported earnings per share.
Ôú≥
Economic Moats
IÔùÆÔù∂Ôù•Ôù≥Ôù¥ÔùØÔù≤Ôù≥ ÔùØÔù¶Ôù¥Ôù•ÔùÆ Ôù™ÔùµÔù§ÔùßÔù•companies by looking at which ones have in-
creased profits the most and assuming the trend will persist in the future. But
more often than not, the firms that look great in the rearview mirror wind up
performing poorly in the future, simply because success attracts competition
as surely as night follows day. And the bigger the profits, the stronger the
competition. That‚Äôs the basic nature of any (reasonably) free market‚Äîcapital
always seeks the areas of highest expected return. Therefore, most highly
profitable firms tend to become less profitable over time as competitors chip
away at their franchises.
You can see this every day in the headlines. Why do generic drug firms em-
ploy armies of lawyers to look for patent loopholes? Because large pharmaceu-
tical firms such as Pfizer and Merck are immensely profitable, and even one
successful patent challenge will pay off in spades. Why were venture capitalists
throwing money at every start-up firm in the networking industry during the
late Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s? Because Cisco was growing at Ôú¥Ôú∞percent per year with operating
margins of Ôú≤Ôúµpercent. If a firm is generating big profits, it will surely attract
competition.
Ôú≤Ôú≤
Ôù•Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔùØÔù≠Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù≠ÔùØÔù°Ôù¥Ôù≥
The concept of economic moats is crucial to the way Morningstar ana-
lyzes stocks because a moat is the characteristic that helps great-performing
companies to stay that way.
We‚Äôve learned much about the subject by studying investment great
Warren Buffett and Harvard professor Michael Porter, who first set down
many of the main principles for analyzing competitive strategy and eco-
nomic moats.
To analyze a company‚Äôs economic moat, follow these four steps:
Ôú±. Evaluate the firm‚Äôs historical profitability. Has the firm been able to gener-
ate a solid return on its assets and on shareholders‚Äô equity? This is the true
litmus test of whether a firm has built an economic moat around itself.
Ôú≤. If the firm has solid returns on capital and consistent profitability, assess
the sources of the firm‚Äôs profits. Why is the company able to keep com-
petitors at bay? What keeps competitors from stealing its profits?
Ôú≥. Estimate how long a firm will be able to hold off competitors, which is the
company‚Äôs competitive advantage period. Some firms can fend off competi-
tors for just a few years, and some firms may be able to do it for decades.
Ôú¥. Analyze the industry‚Äôs competitive structure. How do firms in this indus-
try compete with one another? Is it an attractive industry with many
profitable firms or a hypercompetitive one in which participants struggle
just to stay afloat?
Analyzing economic moats is complicated because there are a nearly infi-
nite number of solutions to the problem of consistently making a buck when
your competition wants to take it away, but the process is interesting for pre-
cisely the same reason.
Evaluating Profitability
The first thing we need to do is look for hard evidence that a firm has an eco-
nomic moat by examining its financial results. (Figuring out whether a com-
pany might have a moat in the future is much tougher, but we‚Äôll give it a shot
at the end of this chapter.)
What we‚Äôre looking for are firms that can earn profits in excess of their
cost of capital‚Äîcompanies that can generate substantial cash relative to the
amount of investments they make. One easy way to do this is by using the
Ôù•Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù∞Ôù≤ÔùØÔù¶Ôù©Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¢Ôù©Ôù¨Ôù©Ôù¥Ôùπ
Ôú≤Ôú≥
metrics in the following questions. Although none of these measures are per-
fect by themselves‚Äîthey‚Äôre really a series of shortcuts‚Äîthey generally do a
good job of identifying which firms have economic moats and which ones
don‚Äôt when they‚Äôre used together. If you‚Äôre confused by the financial measures
in this section, don‚Äôt worry: I‚Äôll discuss them in detail in the next few chapters.
Does the Firm Generate Free Cash Flow? If So, How Much?
First, look at free cash flow‚Äîwhich is simply cash flow from operations
minus capital expenditures. (We‚Äôll go over free cash flow more in Chapter Ôúµ.
For now, just go to a firm‚Äôs statement of cash flows, which you can find in its
quarterly and annual financial filings, look for the line item labeled ‚Äúcash flow
from operations,‚Äù and subtract the line labeled ‚Äúcapital expenditures.‚Äù) Firms
that generate free cash flow essentially have money left over after reinvesting
whatever they need to keep their businesses humming along. In a sense, free
cash flow is money that could be extracted from the firm every year without
damaging the core business.
Next, divide free cash flow by sales (or revenues), which tells you what pro-
portion of each dollar in revenue the firm is able to convert into excess profits.
If a firm‚Äôs free cash flow as a percentage of sales is around Ôúµpercent or better,
you‚Äôve found a cash machine‚Äîas of mid-Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, only one-half of the S&P ÔúµÔú∞Ôú∞
pass this test. Strong free cash flow is an excellent sign that a firm has an eco-
nomic moat.
What Are the Firm‚Äôs Net Margins?
Just as free cash flow measures excess profitability from one perspective, net
margins look at profitability from another angle. Net margin is simply net
income as a percentage of sales, and it tells you how much profit the firm
generates per dollar of sales. (You can find sales and net income on a firm‚Äôs
income statement, which should also be in each of its regular financial fil-
ings.) In general, firms that can post net margins above Ôú±Ôúµpercent are doing
something right.
What Are Returns on Equity?
Return on equity (ROE) is net income as a percentage of shareholders‚Äô equity,
and it measures profits per dollar of the capital shareholders have invested in 
a company. Although ROE does have some flaws‚Äîwhich we discuss in
Ôú≤Ôú¥
Ôù•Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔùØÔù≠Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù≠ÔùØÔù°Ôù¥Ôù≥
Chapter Ôú∂‚Äîit still works well as one tool for assessing overall profitability. As
a rule of thumb, firms that are able to consistently post ROEs above Ôú±Ôúµper-
cent are generating solid returns on shareholders‚Äô money, which means
they‚Äôre likely to have economic moats. We‚Äôll go over ROE in more detail in
Chapter Ôú∂.
What Are Returns on Assets?
Return on assets (ROA) is net income as a percentage of a firm‚Äôs assets, and it
measures how efficient a firm is at translating its assets into profits. Use Ôú∂per-
cent to Ôú∑percent as a rough benchmark‚Äîif a firm is able to consistently post
ROAs above these rates, it may have some competitive advantage over its peers.
When you‚Äôre looking at all four of these metrics, look at more than just one
year. A firm that has consistently cranked out solid ROEs, good free cash flow,
and decent margins over a number of years is much more likely to truly have
an economic moat than a firm with more erratic results. Consistency is impor-
tant when evaluating companies, because it‚Äôs the ability to keep competitors at
bay for an extended period of time‚Äînot just for a year or two‚Äîthat really
makes a firm valuable. Five years is the absolute minimum time period for
evaluation, and I‚Äôd strongly encourage you to go back Ôú±Ôú∞years if you can.
In addition, these benchmarks are rules of thumb, not hard-and-fast cut-
offs. Comparing firms with industry averages is always a good idea, as is ex-
amining the trend in profitability metrics‚Äîare they getting higher or lower?
There‚Äôs also a more sophisticated way of measuring a firm‚Äôs profitability
that involves calculating return on invested capital (ROIC), estimating a
weighted average cost of capital (WACC), and then looking at the difference
between the two. We‚Äôll talk more in Chapter Ôú∂, but don‚Äôt worry if it seems too
complicated for you. Using a combination of free cash flow, ROE, ROA, and
net margins will steer you in the right direction.
Building an Economic Moat
Next, we need to determine why a firm has done such a great job of holding
on to its profits and keeping the competition at arm‚Äôs length. Although being
in an attractive industry can certainly help, the strategy pursued at the com-
pany level is even more important. The mere fact that there are excellent
companies in fundamentally unattractive industries (e.g., Southwest Airlines)
Ôù¢ÔùµÔù©Ôù¨Ôù§Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù°ÔùÆ Ôù•Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔùØÔù≠Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù≠ÔùØÔù°Ôù¥
Ôú≤Ôúµ
tells us intuitively that this must be the case. Academic research suggests that
a firm‚Äôs strategy is roughly twice as important as a firm‚Äôs industry when it‚Äôs
trying to build an economic moat.Ôú±
When you‚Äôre examining the sources of a firm‚Äôs economic moat, the key
thing is to never stop asking, ‚ÄúWhy?‚Äù Why aren‚Äôt competitors stealing the firm‚Äôs
customers? Why can‚Äôt a competitor charge a lower price for a similar product or
service? Why do customers accept annual price increases?
When possible, look at the situation from the customer‚Äôs perspective.
What value does the product or service bring to the customer? How does it
help them run their own business better? Why do they use one firm‚Äôs product
or service instead of a competitor‚Äôs? If you can answer these questions, odds
are good that you‚Äôll have found the source of the company‚Äôs economic moat.
In general, there are five ways that an individual firm can build sustain-
able competitive advantage:
Ôú±. Creating real product differentiation through superior technology or
features
Ôú≤. Creating perceived product differentiation through a trusted brand or
reputation
Ôú≥. Driving costs down and offering a similar product or service at a lower
price
Ôú¥. Locking in customers by creating high switching costs
Ôúµ. Locking out competitors by creating high barriers to entry or high barriers
to success
Real Product Differentiation
This is certainly the most obvious type of economic moat‚Äîafter all, wouldn‚Äôt
customers always pay more for a better product or service? Unfortunately,
simply having better technology or more features is usually not a sustainable
strategy because there are always competitors hoping to build a better mouse-
trap. And because having the best product or service usually means charging
a premium price, firms pursuing this strategy often limit the size of their
Ôú±Anita McGahan and Michael E. Porter, ‚ÄúHow Much Does Industry Matter, Really?‚Äù Strategic
Management Journal, Ôú±Ôú∏, p. Ôú±Ôúµ(Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∑).
Ôú≤Ôú∂
Ôù•Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔùØÔù≠Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù≠ÔùØÔù°Ôù¥Ôù≥
potential market. Many customers will be satisfied with a slightly inferior
product at a significantly lower price.
More important, it‚Äôs just plain difficult to constantly stay one step ahead
of competitors by adding features or improving a product, which is why few
firms are able to use this strategy to create long-term excess profits. This is
especially the case in many parts of the technology sector and in the con-
sumer electronics industry‚Äîtoday‚Äôs vendor of the latest and greatest server,
storage system, or DVD player is likely not going to be tomorrow‚Äôs leading
player. Finally, constant innovation generally sucks up a large amount of cap-
ital in the form of research and development expenses, which can make a
product-differentiation strategy very expensive.
Data-storage manufacturer EMC is a good example. In the mid-Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s,
the firm had leapfrogged IBM in this fast-growing area of the technology sec-
tor and was winning new customers because its products had features that
IBM‚Äôs lacked. For several years, EMC raked in enormous profits by charging
customers substantially higher prices than the competition for its technolog-
ically superior products. (In fact, EMC‚Äôs prices were so high that customers
nicknamed the firm ‚Äúexcess margin corporation.‚Äù)
However, IBM and other competitors didn‚Äôt give up. Eventually, IBM
rolled out products that came pretty close to matching EMC‚Äôs, and IBM
priced them much lower in an effort to win customers back. As a result,
IBM began regaining market share, and EMC‚Äôs business suffered.
The lesson here is that although firms can occasionally generate enor-
mous excess profits‚Äîand enormous stock returns‚Äîby staying one step
ahead of the technological curve, these profits are usually short-lived. Unless
you are familiar enough with the inner workings of an industry to know
when a firm‚Äôs products are being supplanted by better ones, be wary of firms
that rely solely on innovation to sustain their competitive advantage.
Perceived Product Differentiation
Very often, however, a firm with consistently better products or services cre-
ates a brand for itself, and a strong brand can constitute a very wide eco-
nomic moat. The wonderful thing about a brand is that as long as customers
perceive your product or service as better than everyone else‚Äôs, it makes rela-
tively little difference whether it actually is different.
Ôù¢ÔùµÔù©Ôù¨Ôù§Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù°ÔùÆ Ôù•Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔùØÔù≠Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù≠ÔùØÔù°Ôù¥
Ôú≤Ôú∑
Tiffany is a fabulous example of the power of a brand to create excess eco-
nomic returns. The simple fact that a piece of jewelry is packaged inside the
famous little blue box allows Tiffany to charge a significant premium for its
products. This example is fascinating because jewelry has so many objective
standards‚Äîkarats of gold, clarity of diamonds‚Äîthat measure the quality of
an individual piece. The fact that consumers will pay more for a virtually
identical diamond ring from Tiffany than from a local jeweler is what defines
a truly valuable brand: It increases a consumer‚Äôs willingness to pay.
Thinking about brands and reputations in this way‚Äîwhether consumers
of a product or service are truly willing to pay more to buy the good from one
firm instead of another‚Äîhelps separate more valuable brands from less valu-
able ones. What matters is not the existence of the brand, but rather how the
brand is used to create excess profits. In fact, brands aren‚Äôt useful at all in
some industries.
Think about companies such as Sony or Ford, both of which have well-
known brands. But both firms have struggled to generate solid returns on
capital over the past few years because they sell goods that are simply not very
amenable to brand-driven price differentiation. Consumers are unlikely to
pay much more for a Sony stereo relative to a product from Panasonic with
similar features, and they‚Äôre also unlikely to pay more for a Ford truck just be-
cause it has the Ford nameplate.
On the flip side, Abercrombie & Fitch has managed to convince legions
of teenagers to pay Ôú§Ôú≤Ôúµfor a T-shirt just because it has ‚ÄúAbercrombie‚Äù on the
front. How long this brand will remain strong is tough to predict, but
there‚Äôs no question that it‚Äôs enabled Abercrombie to charge more for its
products over the past several years. In fact, the durability of a brand is a
critical component of any brand-based economic moat. Some brands‚Äî
for example, Coke or Disney‚Äîlast for generations, but some are much
more fleeting.
When you‚Äôre evaluating whether a strong brand really does create an eco-
nomic moat, it‚Äôs not enough to look at whether consumers trust the product
or have an emotional connection to the brand. The brand has to justify the
cost of creating it by actually making money for the firm, and sustaining a
powerful brand usually requires a lot of expensive advertising. Therefore, un-
less the brand actually increases consumers‚Äô willingness to pay and those
Ôú≤Ôú∏
Ôù•Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔùØÔù≠Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù≠ÔùØÔù°Ôù¥Ôù≥
looser wallets translate into consistently positive returns on capital, the brand
may not be worth as much as you‚Äôd thought.
Driving Costs Down
Offering a similar product or service at a lower cost can be an extremely pow-
erful source of competitive advantage. It costs Southwest Ôú≤Ôúµpercent less to fly
one passenger one mile than the leanest of the major airlines, and it‚Äôs that ad-
vantage that propelled the firm from a Texas upstart to a big-league player in
Ôú≤Ôúµyears. Low costs have fattened Dell‚Äôs profit margins to such an extent that
the firm has been able to expand its share of the PC market from around Ôú∂
percent in Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∑to more than Ôú±Ôúµpercent by year-end Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤‚Äîa big move in just
six years.
Airlines and PCs are known as commodity industries, in which products are
tough to differentiate. Low-cost strategies work especially well in these types
of markets. Even in noncommodity markets, lower costs can bring large ad-
vantages as long as the cost advantage is sustainable and not temporary.
However, it‚Äôs not enough to just look at a firm‚Äôs profit margins and say that
such-and-such firm has lower costs than its competitors‚Äîyou need to iden-
tify the sources of those cost savings, which can come in a variety of flavors.
In general, firms can create cost advantages by either inventing a better
process or achieving a larger scale. Dell is the classic example of a firm with a
process-based advantage. Building PCs only after they‚Äôre ordered allows the
firm to take advantage of the swift price erosion of PC components‚Äîparts
don‚Äôt sit in inventory losing value while the firm waits for orders to come in.
Over time, Dell has continued to squeeze costs from its supply chain to the
point where you could argue that Dell is no longer a technology firm, but
rather a manufacturing one.
Process advantages can also be subtler. In the asset-management industry,
you might think that Vanguard‚Äôs size is what allows it to underprice its ser-
vices‚Äîbut there are other firms of comparable size with much higher fees.
The key is that Vanguard is structured as a mutual organization that‚Äôs collec-
tively owned by fundholders, rather than a profit-maximizing corporation
owned by shareholders. Because of this structure, Vanguard can plow back ex-
cess profits into cost-reducing activities, whereas traditionally structured asset
management firms will either distribute excess profits to their managers in
Ôù¢ÔùµÔù©Ôù¨Ôù§Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù°ÔùÆ Ôù•Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔùØÔù≠Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù≠ÔùØÔù°Ôù¥
Ôú≤Ôúπ
the form of fat bonuses or allow the cash to pile up on the balance sheet. In
Vanguard‚Äôs case, higher profits allow the firm to push costs down, which at-
tracts more assets, which generates more profits, and so forth. This relatively
unique structure means that it will likely be very difficult for any non-mutual
asset-management firm to ever match Vanguard‚Äôs low costs.
Scale advantages are often very difficult for competitors to match because
they tend to build on themselves: The largest firms continue to drive down
costs and prices, and smaller ones have an increasingly difficult time catching
up. The most basic form of a scale advantage comes from simply leveraging
fixed costs‚Äîin other words, spreading the cost of an asset such as a factory
across an ever-larger sales base. Intel, for example, produces far more micro-
processors in any given year than archrival AMD, which means its per-chip
production cost is a great deal lower than AMD‚Äôs.
Fixed costs don‚Äôt have to be factories. For example, the trucks that make
up the distribution network of package-delivery service UPS represent an
enormous fixed cost that allows the firm to deliver to more locations at a
lower cost than almost any other competitor. Although replicating a delivery
network that serves only large metropolitan centers might not be too daunt-
ing a task for a potential competitor, replicating a network that can deliver to
virtually any address in the United States would be another thing altogether.
Because it costs UPS very little extra money to put an additional couple of
packages on a truck that‚Äôs already serving a particular delivery route, its profit
on those extra couple of packages is high.
Locking In Customers
Customer lock-in, or creating high customer switching costs, is possibly the
subtlest type of competitive advantage. Uncovering it requires a deep under-
standing of a firm‚Äôs operations. Cost advantages, brands, and better products
are all relatively easy to spot from the outside, but knowing exactly what makes
it tough for a customer to switch from one firm to another can be difficult to
find out. However, it can also be very powerful, which is why firms with high
customer switching costs often have wide economic moats.
If you can make it difficult‚Äîin terms of either money or time‚Äîfor a
customer to switch to a competing product, you can charge your customers
more and make more money‚Äîsimple in theory, but difficult in practice.
Ôú≥Ôú∞
Ôù•Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔùØÔù≠Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù≠ÔùØÔù°Ôù¥Ôù≥
Remember, a switching cost does not have to be monetary‚Äîin fact, it
rarely is. Much more frequently, what deters customers from dropping a
product or service in favor of a competing product or service is time. Often,
learning how to use a product or service can require a significant investment
of time, which means the benefits of a competing product have to be very
large to induce a switch. A consumer might switch brands of tomato sauce
because one tastes just a little better than the other, but a word-processing
program would have to carry huge advantages over an incumbent program to
induce a consumer to throw away the accumulated knowledge and spend
time learning the new program.
Medical device firms such as Stryker and Zimmer are perfect examples of
how firms can create high switching costs that help ensure customer reten-
tion. Both of these firms manufacture artificial joints such as hips and knees,
and surgeons have to be trained on how to implant their products‚Äîa Stryker
hip is different enough from a Zimmer hip that a surgeon can‚Äôt just choose
one or the other based on which is on sale that week. This training process is
time-consuming for surgeons, which means surgeons tend to develop prefer-
ences for a particular company‚Äôs products and stick with them. Therefore,
Stryker, Zimmer, and their competitors tend to have relatively stable shares of
the joint-replacement market‚Äîa firm would have to introduce a measurably
better product to induce surgeons to incur the retraining costs needed to use
the new product.
When you‚Äôre looking for evidence of high customer switching costs, these
questions should help:
3 Does the firm‚Äôs product require a significant amount of client training? If
so, customers will be reluctant to switch and incur lost productivity dur-
ing the training period.
3 Is the firm‚Äôs product or service tightly integrated into customers‚Äô busi-
nesses? Firms don‚Äôt change vendors of mission-critical products often be-
cause the costs of a botched switch may far outweigh the benefits of using
the new product or service.
3 Is the firm‚Äôs product or service an industry standard? Customers may feel
pressure from their own clients‚Äîor their peers‚Äîto continue using a
well-known and well-respected product or service.
Ôù¢ÔùµÔù©Ôù¨Ôù§Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù°ÔùÆ Ôù•Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔùØÔù≠Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù≠ÔùØÔù°Ôù¥
Ôú≥Ôú±
3 Is the benefit to be gained from switching small relative to the cost of
switching? Bank customers, for example, often endure slightly higher fees
because the lower fees they might get from moving to a competing bank
are of less value than the potential hassle of moving their account.
3 Does the firm tend to sign long-term contracts with clients? This is often
a sign that the client does not want to frequently switch vendors.
Locking Out Competitors
Locking out competitors is the fifth strategy that firms can use to generate
lasting competitive advantages. If done well, this can result in years of strong
profits. If done too well, it can invite the scrutiny of the federal government
on antitrust grounds‚Äîas Microsoft (and other firms) have discovered.
The most obvious way to lock out competitors is to acquire some kind
of regulatory exclusivity, as many casinos do from state governments. Li-
censes and such are powerful deterrents to competitors‚Äîbut because gov-
ernments make the rules, they can also change them without warning. U.S.
state governments have been known to raise the tax rates on the casinos they
license after the casino facilities are in place and generating profits. Although
the casinos were still protected from competitors, state governments were
able to tax away a larger portion of their excess profits than the casinos ini-
tially expected.
Patents fall into the regulatory category as well because a patent holder is
legally protected from direct competition for a set period of time. And as
large pharmaceutical companies have demonstrated, patents can lead to years
of extremely high profit margins. But although patents may deter competi-
tion, they tend to attract litigation, which can severely hamper the patent-
holder‚Äôs ability to earn excess returns.
A great example is Pfizer, which holds patents on some of the top-selling
drugs in the world. These patents allow the company to charge high prices
for new drugs for years after they hit the market. Competition during the life
of the patent is usually limited, so profits and cash flows are huge. That‚Äôs one
reason Pfizer‚Äôs average return on equity over the past decade has been greater
than Ôú≥Ôú∞percent and its net profit margins are currently north of Ôú≤Ôúµpercent
(compared with around Ôú∂percent for the average S&P ÔúµÔú∞Ôú∞company). That‚Äôs
also why it‚Äôs been a great stock to own over the long haul. In fact, many of the
Ôú≥Ôú≤
Ôù•Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔùØÔù≠Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù≠ÔùØÔù°Ôù¥Ôù≥
major pharmaceutical players have outperformed the market over the past Ôú±Ôú∞
years because of the economic moats inherent in this industry.
However, although patents and licenses can do a great job of keeping
competitors at bay and maintaining high profit margins, they can also be
ephemeral. If you‚Äôre investigating a firm whose economic moat depends solely
on a single patent or other regulatory approval, don‚Äôt forget to investigate the
likelihood of that approval disappearing unexpectedly. This will likely involve
a detailed reading of the legal proceedings section of the firm‚Äôs Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´filing.
(You can find financial filings through most firms‚Äô Web sites, as well as di-
rectly from the SEC‚Äôs Web site.)
A much more durable strategy for locking out competitors is to take ad-
vantage of the network effect. A strong network becomes more valuable as the
number of users increases‚Äîmuch like a telephone network, which wouldn‚Äôt
be worth much if you could call only a dozen people, but which has immense
value given the enormous number of users. Companies that have a network
protecting their competitive positions often have very wide economic moats.
In general, markets tend to have strong network effects‚Äîthe more buyers
and sellers that transact in a particular market, the greater the value of that
market to participants. As a publicly listed auction market, eBay is the purest
example of this idea: The firm has held its own against much larger com-
panies, and it seems unlikely that another competitor will emerge soon.
It should be easy to see why eBay has a near-monopoly in online auctions
in most of the world. Because eBay was the first major firm to connect indi-
vidual buyers and sellers over the Internet in an auction format, the number
of buyers and sellers grew very quickly. As more rare baseball cards and vin-
tage posters came up for auction on eBay, more buyers were attracted to the
site to bid on those items. Those extra bidders attracted still more sellers‚Äî
the sellers wanted to go where the buyers were, and the buyers wanted to go
where the sellers were.
By the time competitors such as Yahoo! and Amazon.com tried to get
into the auction game, it was too late. There were already too many bidders
on eBay for sellers to want to switch to a new, smaller auction service‚Äîthey‚Äôd
probably get a lower price for their wares because there were fewer bidders on
each item. Meanwhile, the bidders weren‚Äôt interested in going anywhere
Ôù®ÔùØÔù∑ Ôù¨ÔùØÔùÆÔùß Ôù∑Ôù©Ôù¨Ôù¨ Ôù©Ôù¥ Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôúø
Ôú≥Ôú≥
either because eBay had by far the most items for sale. Thus, eBay gained crit-
ical mass and, today, a near-monopoly position in the online-auction market.
Another great example is money-transfer concern Western Union, which
is owned by First Data. With more than Ôú±Ôú∑Ôú∞,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞locations worldwide, odds
are pretty good that Western Union can get your money wherever you need
it to go, and the value of the firm‚Äôs agent network only grows larger as it adds
locations. The central characteristic of a classic network effect is a virtuous
circle‚Äîmore users attract more users.
How Long Will It Last?
Our next topic is assessing the longevity of an economic moat, so we know
how long a firm is likely to keep its competitors at bay.
Think about an economic moat in two dimensions. There‚Äôs depth‚Äîhow
much money the firm can make‚Äîand there‚Äôs width‚Äîhow long the firm can
sustain above-average profits. Technology firms often have very deep but very
narrow moats, so they‚Äôre incredibly profitable for a relatively short period of
time until a competitor builds a better product. A niche firm such as WD-Ôú¥Ôú∞
is just the opposite. It‚Äôs never going to make an enormous amount of money
in any one year by selling cans of household lubricant, but it has such a solid
franchise that its excess returns are likely to persist for quite some time.
Estimating how long a moat will last is tough stuff, but you need to at
least give it some thought, even if you can‚Äôt come up with a precise answer.
Just being able to separate firms into three buckets‚Äîa few years, several years,
and many years‚Äîis very useful.
In general, any competitive advantage based on technological superiority‚Äî
real product differentiation‚Äîis likely to be fairly short. Successful software
firms, for example, can generate huge excess returns because they have high
profit margins and they don‚Äôt need to spend much money on fixed costs such as
machinery. However, the duration of those returns is typically very short be-
cause of the rapid pace of technological change. In other words, today‚Äôs leader
can quickly become tomorrow‚Äôs loser because the barriers to entry are so low
and the potential rewards so high.
Cost leadership, brands (perceived product differentiation), customer
lock-ins, and competitor lockouts can each confer competitive advantage
Ôú≥Ôú¥
Ôù•Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔùØÔù≠Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù≠ÔùØÔù°Ôù¥Ôù≥
periods of varying lengths‚Äîthere‚Äôs no good rule of thumb, unfortunately. To
give you some guidance in what separates a wide moat from a narrow moat
and what kinds of companies have no moat at all, I‚Äôve listed Ôú≤Ôú∑well-known
large-cap companies in Figures Ôú≥.Ôú±, Ôú≥.Ôú≤, and Ôú≥.Ôú≥and included an explanation
as to why each firm‚Äôs moat is wide, narrow, or does not exist. Having these ex-
amples will, I hope, help you analyze economic moats when doing your own
Dell
Classic low-cost producer: lean operating structure and direct 
Internet-based sales allow the company to run circles around its rivals.
eBay
Network effect: The more buyers and sellers the network has, the 
more attractive it becomes to prospective users and the tougher it 
becomes for competitors to contend with.
PepsiCo
By far the market share leader in salty snacks and sports drinks, 
the diversified food company boasts a stable full of strong brands, 
innovative new products, and an impressive distribution network.
Comcast
Controls roughly one-third of cable households in the U.S. This gives it 
unparalleled leverage with content providers and equipment suppliers.
Intel
Chipmaker‚Äôs dominant position gives it significant economies of scale. 
Brand name and patents are also significant intangible assets.
H&R Block
Dominates the U.S. tax preparation market. One in every seven tax 
returns filed is prepared by Block.
Wal-Mart
Largest retail company in the world is also the preeminent low-cost 
provider. The firm flexes its muscles with suppliers in negotiating 
prices and passes the savings down to consumers.
United Technologies
Operates in a selection of concentrated industries. Buyers of elevators, 
cooling systems, and helicopters don‚Äôt switch brands often.
Adobe
High customer switching costs: graphic designers are trained early
in their careers to use the company‚Äôs software and can‚Äôt do their job 
without it.
Paychex
Vast sales network provides access to new accounts and scale 
gives it a cost advantage. Plus, it has pricing power over its diverse 
and unorganized client base.

Federal Express
Sure, it practically invented overnight delivery, but behind the 
scenes, FedEx is a cargo airline, and airline margins are thin.
Nokia
Although the Nokia brand is strong, cell phones are 
becoming commodities. 
Kraft
Despite a portfolio of familiar consumer brands, there isn‚Äôt much 
breathing space between Kraft and its competitors, which has made it 
difficult for the firm to exert any pricing power.
Waste Management
Network of 300 landfills creates a wide-moat disposal business, 
but lack of brand loyalty in its collection business‚Äîwhere customers 
flock to the lowest price‚Äîearns this company a narrow moat.
Disney
Owns some of the most valuable intellectual property in the world, 
but increasing competition and an absence of creativity have eroded 
some of the brand's appeal.
ExxonMobil
Enjoys enormous economies of scale, but still operates in a 
commodity industry.
Target
‚ÄúCheap chic‚Äù strategy has helped it carve out a moat and differentiate 
itself from Wal-Mart. However, Target‚Äôs strategy depends partially on 
accurately guessing fashion trends, which is more difficult than merely 
being the low-cost provider of everyday items.
Best Buy
Dominates home-electronics and entertainment-software retailing, 
but operates in a very economically sensitive industry. 
AOL Time Warner
Still has some great media properties, but bungled attempts
at formulating a successful broadband strategy have left the AOL 
business vulnerable.
SBC Communications
Former monopoly over local phone service is facing competition from 
wireless carriers, cable companies, and long-distance firms that can 
buy access to its local network.

Industry Analysis
Our last step is to investigate the industry in which the firm operates. Let‚Äôs
face it: It‚Äôs just plain easier to make money in some industries than it is in
others. (Ask the CEO of any airline company.) Although the attractiveness of
an industry doesn‚Äôt tell the whole story‚Äîafter all, Southwest Airlines has
made plenty of money for shareholders‚Äîit‚Äôs important to have a feel for the
competitive landscape.
First, get a rough sense of the industry so you can classify it. Are sales for
firms in the industry generally increasing or shrinking? Are firms consistently
profitable or does the industry go through periodic cycles when most firms
lose money? Is the industry dominated by a few large players, or is it full of
firms that are roughly the same size? How profitable is the average firm‚Äîare
operating margins fairly high (more than Ôú≤Ôúµpercent) or fairly low (less than
General Motors 
Operates in deeply cyclical industry. Legacy costs and reputation for 
mediocre quality puts it at a competitive disadvantage relative to 
most peers.
Micron
Memory chips are essentially commodities, which leads to product 
price volatility and lots of competition.
Circuit City
Main competitor Best Buy has higher profit margins, better 
inventory turnover, and twice the per-store sales numbers.
Staples
A saturated industry, lack of product differentiation, and intense price 
sensitivity prevent this company from digging a moat.
Maytag
Low-cost Asian producers have forced the company to focus on the 
premium appliance market, where competition is fierce and product 
differentiation is difficult to achieve.
Delta
Not the low-cost provider and doesn‚Äôt offer a differentiated product. 
In a commodity industry where competition revolves largely around 
price, its business model is unsustainable.

Ôù©ÔùÆÔù∂Ôù•Ôù≥Ôù¥ÔùØÔù≤‚ÄôÔù≥ Ôù£Ôù®Ôù•Ôù£Ôù´Ôù¨Ôù©Ôù≥Ôù¥
Ôú≥Ôú∑
Ôú±Ôúµpercent)? (An operating margin is income from operations as a percentage
of sales. I discuss such financial ratios in more detail in Chapter Ôú∂.)
You can answer some of these questions by looking at aggregate statistics‚Äî
average growth rates, average margins, and so on. However, averages can‚Äôt tell
you everything, so be sure that you examine a number of individual com-
panies. An easy way to do this is to look at a list of companies in the industry
you‚Äôre researching sorted by sales or market cap, and examine a dozen or so to
get a feel for the industry.
You don‚Äôt need to do detailed analysis at this point‚Äîjust glance over sales
and earnings growth rates and margins. The most important thing is to look
at a variety of firms over a reasonably long time frame‚Äîat least Ôúµyears and,
preferably, Ôú±Ôú∞.
Investor‚Äôs Checklist: Economic Moats
3 Because success attracts competition as surely as night follows day, the
most highly profitable companies tend to become less profitable over time.
That‚Äôs why economic moats are so important: They help great companies
stay that way.
3 For concrete evidence of an economic moat, look for firms that consis-
tently earn high profits. Focus on free cash flow, net margins, return on
equity, and return on assets.
3 After you‚Äôve looked at these specific measures, try to identify the source of
the company‚Äôs economic moat. Companies generally build sustainable
competitive advantages through product differentiation (real or perceived),
driving costs down, locking in customers with high switching costs, or
locking out competitors through high barriers to entry.
3 Think about economic moats in two dimensions: depth (how much
money the company can make) and width (how long it can sustain
above-average profits). In general, any economic moat based on techno-
logical innovation is likely to be short-lived.
3 Although the attractiveness of an industry doesn‚Äôt tell the whole story, it‚Äôs
important to get a feel for the competitive landscape. Some industries are
just easier to make money in than others.

The Language of Investing
IÔùÆ Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù• ÔùÆÔù•Ôù∏Ôù¥three chapters, my goal is to help you understand how finan-
cial statements fit together and how you can use them to identify solid com-
panies. First, we‚Äôll look at how the three main financial statements fit together
by describing what‚Äôs in them and by following a dollar through a business.
Then, we‚Äôll build a hot dog stand to see how the operations of a very simple
business are represented in financial statements.
In Chapter Ôúµ, we‚Äôll tour all three statements in detail by looking at the
books of a real, complicated company. (Note to readers with some account-
ing background: You can skip to Chapter Ôú∂‚ÄîAnalyzing a Company‚Äîunless
you want a review of the basics.) In Chapter Ôú±Ôú±, we‚Äôll apply our newfound ac-
counting knowledge to the analytical process by looking at two real-world
companies.
The Basics
As an investor, you‚Äôre mainly going to be interested in the balance sheet, the
income statement, and the statement of cash flows. These three tables are
your windows into corporate performance, and they‚Äôre the place to start
when you‚Äôre analyzing a company.
Ôú¥Ôú∞
Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¨Ôù°ÔùÆÔùßÔùµÔù°ÔùßÔù• ÔùØÔù¶ Ôù©ÔùÆÔù∂Ôù•Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß
All three of these statements can be found in the three major types of 
financial filings: an annual report, a Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´filing, and a Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù±filing. You‚Äôre
probably familiar with annual reports‚Äîthose glossy booklets with smiling em-
ployees that firms mail out every year‚Äîbut to really understand a firm, you‚Äôll
need to look up Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´s and Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù±s. These are dense, detailed sets of financial in-
formation that companies file with the Securities and Exchange Commission
(SEC) every year (the Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´) and every quarter (the Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù±). You can access these
reports at http://www.sec.gov/edgar.shtml or http://www.freeedgar.com. The
latter is a bit easier to use.
Think of the three statements like this: The balance sheet is like a com-
pany‚Äôs credit report because it tells you how much the company owns (assets)
relative to what it owes (liabilities) at a specific point in time. It tells you how
strong the framework and foundation of the business is.
The income statement, meanwhile, tells how much the company made
or lost in accounting profits during a year or a quarter. Unlike the balance
sheet, which is a snapshot of the company‚Äôs financial health at a precise mo-
ment, the income statement records revenues and expenses over a set period,
such as a fiscal year.
Finally, there‚Äôs the statement of cash flows, which records all the cash that
comes into a company and all of the cash that goes out. The statement of
cash flows ties the income statement and balance sheet together.
Right now, you‚Äôre probably wondering why we need an income statement
and a cash flow statement‚Äîafter all, if a company makes money, it makes
money, right? The difference lies in a confusing concept called accrual account-
ing. Here‚Äôs how it works. Companies record sales (or revenue) when a service or
a good is provided to the buyer, regardless of when the buyer pays. As long as
the company is reasonably certain that the buyer will eventually pay the bill, the
company can post the sale to its income statement.
The cash flow statement, on the other hand, is concerned only with when
cash is received and when it goes out the door. I‚Äôll go over the cash flow state-
ment in detail later in this chapter, but here‚Äôs a quick example.
Let‚Äôs say that Colgate sells a few cases of toothpaste to Joe‚Äôs Corner Store
for Ôú§Ôú±,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞on February Ôú±Ôúµbut gives Joe Ôú∂Ôú∞days to pay because he‚Äôs a regular
customer and has a good track record of paying his bills on time. March Ôú≥Ôú±rolls
around, Joe hasn‚Äôt paid yet, and Colgate closes its books for the quarter.
Ôù∑Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù• Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù≠ÔùØÔùÆÔù•Ôùπ ÔùßÔùØÔù•Ôù≥
Ôú¥Ôú±
Colgate shows Ôú§Ôú±,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞in sales on its income statement because it shipped the
toothpaste to Joe‚Äîaccording to the income statement, the sale is complete, re-
gardless of whether Colgate has received payment. But because Joe hasn‚Äôt
ponied up the grand yet, Colgate will post an entry on its balance sheet to
show that Joe owes Colgate Ôú§Ôú±,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞. (The entry goes into the accounts receiv-
able line‚Äîmore on this later in the chapter.)
As you can see, a company can show rip-roaring sales growth without re-
ceiving a cent of cash. In fact, if Colgate produces and sells toothpaste faster
than its customers pay for the toothpaste, sales growth would look fantastic
even though cash is flowing out the door‚Äîwhich is why we need a statement
of cash flows.
Where the Money Goes
Figure Ôú¥.Ôú±illustrates how money moves from investors, through the com-
pany, to consumers, and back to the company.
This isn‚Äôt nearly as bad as it looks. Let‚Äôs follow some money through the
company to show you what I mean.
A group of investors and bondholders (Ôú±) provide capital to a firm, either by
buying shares in the company (stockholders) or by buying the company‚Äôs bonds
(bondholders). The company takes the money and buys fixed assets (Ôú≤) such as
machinery and buildings‚Äîand uses those assets to produce inventory (Ôú≥). Some
of the inventory is sold for cash (Ôú¥), and some is sold on credit (Ôúµ).
The credit sales are posted to ‚Äúaccounts receivable‚Äù‚Äîa fancy name for
IOU‚Äîuntil the customer pays the firm (Ôú∂). Once the firm has cash in hand,
it can spend it in all sorts of ways. Some of it goes back into production
(Ôú∑), which means buying replacement raw materials to create more inventory,
and some of it goes into investment (Ôú∏), which means buying more machin-
ery or building another factory. (In accounting lingo, you‚Äôll usually see any
investment that purchases a tangible long-term asset such as a building or
factory called ‚Äúcapital expenditure,‚Äù or simply ‚Äúcapex.‚Äù)
Another chunk of cash goes to Uncle Sam as taxes (Ôúπ), and some may
flow out as dividends to stockholders if the firm pays a dividend or as interest
to bondholders (Ôú±Ôú∞) if the firm has debt.
That‚Äôs really all there is to it. Cash flows in, and (you hope) cash flows
out. Let‚Äôs walk through a simplified company‚ÄîMike‚Äôs Hot Dog Stand‚Äîto
Ôú¥Ôú≤
Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¨Ôù°ÔùÆÔùßÔùµÔù°ÔùßÔù• ÔùØÔù¶ Ôù©ÔùÆÔù∂Ôù•Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß
illustrate how all of this comes together. We‚Äôll use Mike‚Äôs to introduce many
of the specific line items in financial statements that tell us how well a com-
pany is doing its core job, which is investing shareholders‚Äô money to generate
a solid return.

hasn‚Äôt disappeared‚Äîit‚Äôs turned into assets and inventory. As shown in the
chart, the Ôú§Ôú∑Ôú∞he spent on the hot dog stand and the tongs is ‚Äúproperty, plant,
and equipment,‚Äù while the Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∞in buns and such has become ‚Äúinventory,‚Äù and
the Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞in cash is just that. Look at the simplified balance sheet in Figure Ôú¥.Ôú≤
to see how this would look if Mike had to file an annual report. (Don‚Äôt worry
if you see an unfamiliar line item‚Äîwe‚Äôll cover it later in the chapter.)
Mike opens for business while the crowd is gathering for the parade and
sells Ôú≥Ôú∞hot dogs at Ôú§Ôú±apiece. By noon, half the hot dogs, buns, condiments,
and charcoal are gone, so Mike‚Äôs ‚Äúcost of goods sold‚Äù is Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞, or half of the Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∞
he spent to buy supplies. Seven people didn‚Äôt have any cash on them, so Mike
let them buy their dogs on credit‚Äîwhich means we need to record Ôú§Ôú∑in ac-
counts receivable, which is the money that folks owe to Mike.
I. Borrows Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞from First National Bank of Mom

In the middle of the day, Mike runs out of buns, so he has to run over to
the corner grocery store to buy more. But when he arrives, he realizes he left
his money back at the stand, so he promises the grocer he‚Äôll come back with
the money on Monday when the hot dog biz slows down. We post Ôú§Ôúµto ac-
counts payable, which is money that Mike owes to the grocery store for the
buns. (Think of accounts payable like your credit card. If you buy a shirt or a
stereo on your Visa, you can use it right away‚Äîbut you still owe the credit
card issuer some cash.)

Ôù∞Ôù≤Ôù°Ôù£Ôù¥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù°Ôù¨ Ôù¶Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù≥‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≥ Ôù©ÔùÆ ÔùµÔù≥Ôù•

At the end of the second day, Mike realizes that his tongs aren‚Äôt working as
well as they used to, and it‚Äôs taking him longer to grill each hot dog. The ac-
counting name for this wear-and-tear is depreciation, which lets us record the
fact that Mike‚Äôs equipment isn‚Äôt as productive as it used to be. (In the real
world, depreciation isn‚Äôt recorded at the moment something starts wearing out.
It‚Äôs actually a regular charge that assumes that an asset wears out over a set time
period‚Äîas long as forty years for a building, and as short as three years for a
computer.) Depreciation is a cost of doing business, just like buying hot dogs
and buns, because Mike will eventually have to buy another set of tongs if he
wants to stay in business. Because all costs have to be recorded‚Äîaccounting is
funny that way‚Äîwe post Ôú§Ôú±to depreciation.
After a long day of serving up delicious dogs to parade-goers, Mike‚Äôs in-
come statement is shown in Figure Ôú¥.Ôú≥.
And his balance sheet is shown in Figure Ôú¥.Ôú¥.
The eagle-eyed reader will notice that although Mike‚Äôs net profit was Ôú§Ôú±Ôúπ,
his cash account went up by Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú≥‚Äîfrom Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞to Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú≥‚Äîon his personal balance
sheet. Why the difference? Let‚Äôs find out by developing a statement of cash
flows from the income statement and balance sheet information that we
have available. By following this example, you‚Äôll see into the heart and soul
of accounting.
To understand how much cash Mike‚Äôs little business generated, we start
with his Ôú§Ôú±Ôúπin net profits, which is the difference between what he paid for

Ôù∞Ôù≤Ôù°Ôù£Ôù¥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù°Ôù¨ Ôù¶Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù≥‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≥ Ôù©ÔùÆ ÔùµÔù≥Ôù•
Ôú¥Ôú∑
set of grilling tongs) as closely as possible, whereas cash profits measure only
the actual dollar bills flowing into and out of a business.
Next, we need to take into account the fact that Mike used up half his
original inventory of hot dogs and buns, as well as the fact that he went out
and bought an additional Ôú§Ôúµworth of buns. His inventory went from Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∞, to
Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞, and back to Ôú§Ôú±Ôúµ. This net decrease in inventory from Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∞to Ôú§Ôú±Ôúµis a source
of cash. In other words, Mike had Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∞of capital tied up in inventory at the
start of the weekend, but now he has only Ôú§Ôú±Ôúµof capital invested in inventory.
As a result, he converted Ôú§Ôúµin inventory to Ôú§Ôúµin cash.
However, Mike also is owed Ôú§Ôú∑by hot dog eaters who haven‚Äôt paid him yet.
Because Mike had to pay to produce the dogs they ate and they haven‚Äôt yet
given him any cash, he‚Äôs used up some money by letting those folks nosh on his
tasty dogs on credit. In other words, Mike paid out cash to get the ingredients
he needed to make the hot dogs, but he hasn‚Äôt yet received any cash in return,
so his decision to extend credit used up Ôú§Ôú∑in cash.
Finally, let‚Äôs not forget that Mike himself is the beneficiary of credit because
he still owes the grocer Ôú§Ôúµfor those extra buns he bought. Because Mike received
something without paying out cash for it, his cash account increases by Ôú§Ôúµ.
We can follow the trail from Mike‚Äôs Ôú§Ôú±Ôúπin net profits to his Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú≥in cash flow
with this simple table:
Net profits = Ôú§Ôú±Ôúπ
+ Ôú§Ôú±depreciation
+ Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞inventory (hot dogs sold)
‚àíÔú§Ôúµinventory (extra buns purchased)
‚àíÔú§Ôú∑accounts receivable (money owed to Mike)
+ Ôú§Ôúµaccounts payable (money owed by Mike)
= Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú≥in operating cash flow
As you can see, the Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú≥in operating cash flow differs from the Ôú§Ôú±Ôúπin net
income because of the choices Mike made in running his little business. For
example, if Mike hadn‚Äôt let anyone buy on credit, but had the same amount
of sales, his cash flow would have been Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞(Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú≥plus Ôú§Ôú∑). Conversely, if the
grocer had forced Mike to pay cash for those extra buns, Mike‚Äôs cash flow
would have been Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∏(Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú≥minus Ôú§Ôúµ.) In both cases, however, Mike‚Äôs net prof-
its would have remained Ôú§Ôú±Ôúπ.
Ôú¥Ôú∏
Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¨Ôù°ÔùÆÔùßÔùµÔù°ÔùßÔù• ÔùØÔù¶ Ôù©ÔùÆÔù∂Ôù•Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß
The key takeaway here is that the income statement and cash flow state-
ment can tell different stories about a business because they‚Äôre constructed
using different sets of rules. The income statement strives to match revenues
and expenses as closely as possible‚Äîthat‚Äôs why we had to deduct the Ôú§Ôú±in de-
preciation from Mike‚Äôs profits, and that‚Äôs why Mike gets to record the Ôú§Ôú∑in
sales that he made on credit. But the cash flow statement cares only about the
dollar bills that go in and out the door, regardless of the timing of the actions
that generated those dollar bills.
If you look only at the income statement without checking to see how
much cash a company is creating, you won‚Äôt be getting the whole story by a
long shot. This simple concept‚Äîthe difference between accounting profits
and cash profits‚Äîis the key to understanding almost everything there is to
know about how a business works, as well as how to separate great businesses
from poor ones. In subsequent chapters, we‚Äôll move from our simple hot dog
stand to real-world companies to learn how to analyze all three of the finan-
cial statements in detail.
Investor‚Äôs Checklist: The Language of Investing
3 The balance sheet is like a company‚Äôs credit report because it tells you
how much the company owns (assets) relative to what it owes (liabilities).
3 The income statement shows how much the company made or lost in ac-
counting profits during a year or a quarter. Unlike the balance sheet,
which is a snapshot of the company‚Äôs financial health at a precise moment,
the income statement records revenues and expenses during a set period,
such as a fiscal year.
3 The third key financial statement‚Äîthe statement of cash flows‚Äîrecords
all the cash that comes into a company and all of the cash that goes out.
3 Accrual accounting is a key concept for understanding financial state-
ments. The income statement matches sales with the corresponding ex-
penses when a service or a good is provided to the buyer, but the cash flow
statement is concerned only with when cash is received and when it goes
out the door.
3 The income statement and cash flow statement can tell different stories
about a business because they‚Äôre constructed using different sets of rules.
To get the most complete picture, be sure to look at both.

Financial Statements Explained
NÔùØÔù∑ Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù°Ôù¥ ÔùπÔùØÔùµhave a good idea of how businesses generate cash and how
profits are recorded on financial statements, let‚Äôs look at each of the three
main financial statements in detail. Unfortunately, not all businesses are as
simple as a hot dog stand, so we need to introduce some additional complex-
ity if we want to analyze real companies. But fear not‚Äîas we walk through
the balance sheet, income statement, and cash flow statement, we‚Äôll look at a
few real-world companies to see what their financial statements can tell us
about how their businesses are functioning.
Wherever possible, I‚Äôll refer to excerpts from the financial statements of
Dell and Hewlett-Packard (H-P), taken from the two firms‚Äô Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´filings with
the SEC. (The Dell excerpts contain data through January Ôú≥Ôú±, Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, and the
H-P excerpts contain data through October Ôú≥Ôú±, Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤.) We‚Äôll start with the
balance sheet, move on to the income statement, and finish with the state-
ment of cash flows. At the start of each section, you‚Äôll see a financial state-
ment from the originally named (and fictitious) ‚ÄúAcme Corporation‚Äù that
will show you how each statement is organized.
Warning: This chapter may be tough going in parts, but it‚Äôs possibly the
most important chapter of the entire book, since reading financial statements

is the foundation for analyzing companies. If you find yourself confused

no rush‚Äîit‚Äôll be here when you get back!
The Balance Sheet
The balance sheet (see Figure Ôúµ.Ôú±)‚Äîsometimes called a ‚Äústatement of finan-
cial position‚Äù‚Äîtells you how much a company owns (its assets), how much it
owes (its liabilities), and the difference between the two (its equity). Equity
represents the value of the money that shareholders have invested in the firm,
and if that sounds odd, think of it just like your mortgage‚Äîyour equity in
your home is the home‚Äôs value minus the mortgage. Stockholders‚Äô equity in a
firm is the value of the firm‚Äôs assets minus its liabilities.

also increases. This makes sense because the value of shareholders‚Äô investment
in the firm rises if that firm starts making a ton of money. Let‚Äôs go through the
balance sheet bit by bit to get a better grasp on how it‚Äôs put together.
Asset Accounts: Current Assets
The accounting gods define current assets as those likely to be used up or con-
verted into cash within one business cycle, usually defined as one year. The
major portions of this category are cash and equivalents, short-term invest-
ments, accounts receivable, and inventories.
Cash and Equivalents and Short-Term Investments: These line items don‚Äôt nec-
essarily refer to actual greenbacks sitting around in a vault but to money in
low-risk, fairly liquid investments. ‚ÄúCash and equivalents‚Äù usually contains
money market funds or anything that can be liquidated quickly and with min-
imal price risk, whereas ‚Äúshort-term investments‚Äù is similar to cash‚Äîusually,
bonds that have less than a year to maturity and earn a higher rate of return
than cash but would take a bit of effort to sell. In most cases, you can men-
tally lump this in with cash when considering how much a firm has on hand
to meet an immediate need.
Accounts Receivable: As we saw in the hot dog example, accounts receivable
are bills that the company hasn‚Äôt yet collected but for which it expects to re-
ceive payment soon. Watch how this account changes relative to the com-
pany‚Äôs sales‚Äîif accounts receivable are rising much faster than sales, the firm
is booking a large amount of revenue for which it has not yet received pay-
ment. This can be a sign of trouble because it may mean that the firm is of-
fering looser credit terms to increase sales‚Äîremember, a firm can record a
sale as soon as it has shipped the product‚Äîbut has less likelihood of ever re-
ceiving the cash it‚Äôs owed.
In Figure Ôúµ.Ôú≤, Dell looks like it was in fine shape on this front. Dell‚Äôs ac-
counts receivable rose about Ôú±Ôú¥percent, but sales increased by the same
amount, as we‚Äôll see later when we look at the company‚Äôs income statement.
However, H-P‚Äôs accounts receivable (see Figure Ôúµ.Ôú≥) took a huge jump from
Ôú§Ôú¥.Ôú¥Ôú∏Ôú∏billion to Ôú§Ôú∏.Ôú¥ÔúµÔú∂billion, which was a ÔúπÔú∞percent increase, whereas sales
Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù• Ôù≥Ôù®Ôù•Ôù•Ôù¥
ÔúµÔú≥
rose only about Ôú≤Ôúµpercent. (Remember, comparing the growth rate of ac-
counts receivable with the growth rate of sales is a good way to judge whether
a company is doing a good job collecting the money that it‚Äôs owed by cus-
tomers.) Although H-P‚Äôs results were somewhat distorted by its acquisition of
Compaq during this time period, that‚Äôs still a big discrepancy. At the time,
H-P investors would have wanted to keep an eye on accounts receivable to
make sure that the firm collected the cash that it was owed by customers.
You‚Äôll often see an ‚Äúallowance for doubtful accounts‚Äù just after accounts
receivable on the balance sheet. This is the company‚Äôs estimate of how much
money it‚Äôs owed by deadbeat customers, and which it‚Äôs consequently unlikely
to collect. For example, on H-P‚Äôs balance sheet, you can see that the firm was
assuming that it wouldn‚Äôt collect Ôú§Ôú¥ÔúπÔúµmillion of the money that customers
owed it as of October Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤.
Inventories: There are several types of inventories, including raw materials
that have not yet been made into a finished product, partially finished prod-
ucts, and finished products that have not yet been sold. Inventories are espe-
cially important to watch in manufacturing and retail firms, and their value
on the balance sheet should be taken with a grain of salt. Because of the way
inventories are accounted for, their liquidation value may very well be a far
cry from their value on the balance sheet. Use your common sense when

judging this: Although a construction firm could probably get a decent price

More importantly, inventories soak up capital‚Äîcash that‚Äôs been converted
into inventory sitting in a warehouse can‚Äôt be used for anything else. The
speed at which a company turns over its inventory can have a huge impact on
profitability because the less time cash is tied up in inventory, the more time
it‚Äôs available for use elsewhere. You can calculate a metric called inventory
turnover by dividing a company‚Äôs cost of goods sold by its inventory level.

of Ôú§Ôúµ.Ôú∏billion for Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤, yielding much lower inventory turns of just Ôú∂. As you

Asset Accounts: Noncurrent Assets
Noncurrent assets are assets that are not expected to be converted into cash or
used up within the reporting period. The big parts of this section are prop-
erty, plant, and equipment (PP&E); investments; and intangible assets.
Property, Plant, and Equipment: These are long-term assets that form the in-
frastructure of the company: land, buildings, factories, furniture, equipment,
and so forth. Dell, for example, had about Ôú§ÔúπÔú±Ôú≥million in PP&E at the end
of Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤, whereas H-P had Ôú§Ôú∂.Ôúπbillion.

market than the amount shown on the balance sheet. You‚Äôll need to dig
around in the notes to the financial statements to see what exactly is in this ac-
count and with how much skepticism you should view its value. You can see
in Figures Ôúµ.Ôú∂and Ôúµ.Ôú∑that H-P has no long-term investments, but Dell has a
pretty sizeable amount‚Äîabout Ôú§Ôúµ.Ôú≥billion‚Äîsitting on its balance sheet. Be-
cause that‚Äôs almost a third of Dell‚Äôs total assets, it‚Äôs definitely something you‚Äôd
want to dig into. (Turns out that Dell‚Äôs investments were mainly bonds, so
you wouldn‚Äôt need to worry too much about them‚Äîif they were equities or
venture capital, you‚Äôd want to find out more about that Ôú§Ôúµ.Ôú≥billion value.)
Intangible Assets: The most common form of intangible assets is goodwill,
which arises when one company acquires another. Goodwill is the differ-
ence between the price the acquiring company pays and tangible value‚Äîor
equity‚Äîof the target company. Essentially, goodwill represents the value of
all of the other stuff that one company gets when it acquires another. For
example, the majority of Coca-Cola‚Äôs value is not in the firm‚Äôs buildings and
equipment; it‚Äôs in the powerful brand that Coke has built up over the past
several decades. If some firm were to buy Coke, it would have to pay far
more than the book value of Coke‚Äôs equity, and that extra amount is called
goodwill.
You should view this account with extreme skepticism because most
companies tend to overpay for their targets, which means the value of good-
will that shows up on the balance sheet is very often far more than the asset
is actually worth. (In Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, for example, AOL Time Warner reduced the
value of the goodwill on its balance sheet by an amazing Ôú¥Ôú∞percent with the
stroke of a pen, essentially admitting that it had far overpaid for AOL when
the two companies merged. If you‚Äôd been counting on this goodwill ac-
count as an asset that the company had at its disposal, you‚Äôd have been
sorely disappointed.)
As you can see in Figure Ôúµ.Ôú∑, H-P had more than Ôú§Ôú±Ôúµbillion‚Äîor
almost Ôú≤Ôú∞percent of its total assets of Ôú§Ôú∑Ôú±billion‚Äîin its goodwill account for
Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤. Was Compaq really worth Ôú§Ôú±Ôúµbillion more than the value of its cash, in-
ventory, fixed assets, customer lists, and patents? Maybe it was‚Äîbut if it‚Äôs not,
H-P will have to reduce the value of this account at some point in the future,
which means the value of the firm‚Äôs total assets will also drop.
ÔúµÔú∏
Ôù¶Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨ Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≥ Ôù•Ôù∏Ôù∞Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù©ÔùÆÔù•Ôù§
Liability Accounts: Current Liabilities
Now that we know what the company owns, we can look at the other side of
the coin‚Äîwhat it owes.
Current liabilities are the flip side of current assets: money the company
expects to pay out within a year. You should focus on accounts payable and
short-term borrowings or payables.
Accounts Payable: These are bills the company owes to somebody else and are
due to be paid within a year, like Mike‚Äôs IOU to the grocer for the extra hot
dog buns. Large companies that have a lot of leverage over their suppliers can
push out some of their payables, which means they hold on to the cash
longer‚Äîand that‚Äôs good for cash flow.
Short-Term Borrowings: This refers to money the company has borrowed for
a term of less than a year, usually to meet short-term needs. It‚Äôs often a line of
bank credit that the firm has temporarily drawn down, though it might also
be a portion of long-term debt that‚Äôs due within the next year. This line item
becomes especially important for companies in financial distress because the
entire amount often has to be paid back quickly. H-P‚Äôs notes payable and
short-term borrowings (see Figure Ôúµ.Ôú∏) were about Ôú§Ôú±.Ôú∏billion, which is fairly
small relative to the size of the firm‚Äôs assets, so no worries there.
Liability Accounts: Noncurrent Liabilities
Noncurrent liabilities are the flip side of noncurrent assets. They represent
money the company owes one year or more in the future. Though you‚Äôll
sometimes see a variety of line items under this heading, the most important

Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù©ÔùÆÔù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù• Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥
ÔúµÔúπ
one by far is long-term debt. This represents money the company has 
borrowed‚Äîusually by issuing bonds, though sometimes from a bank‚Äîthat
doesn‚Äôt need to be paid back for a few years.
Stockholders‚Äô Equity
Remember, shareholders‚Äô equity is equal to total assets minus total liabilities,
and it represents the part of the company owned by shareholders. This can be
the most confusing section of any firm‚Äôs financial statements, because it‚Äôs
filled with many anachronistic line items that have little practical relevance.
The only account worth looking at is retained earnings, which basically
records the amount of capital a company has generated over its lifetime‚Äî
minus dividends and stock buybacks, which represent funds that have already
been returned to shareholders. Retained earnings is a cumulative account;
therefore, each year that the company makes a profit and doesn‚Äôt pay it all out
as dividends, retained earnings increase. Likewise, if a company has lost money
over time, retained earnings can turn negative and is often renamed ‚Äúaccumu-
lated deficit‚Äù on the balance sheet. Think of this account as a company‚Äôs long-
term track record at generating profits.
The Income Statement
Now that we know how much a company owns and how much it owes, we
can move on to the good stuff‚Äîhow much money it‚Äôs making (or losing.)
In a Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´, you‚Äôll usually see the income statement labeled as the ‚Äúconsoli-
dated statement of income‚Äù or the ‚Äúconsolidated statement of earnings‚Äù (see
Figure Ôúµ.Ôúπ).
Revenue
Sometimes labeled ‚Äúsales,‚Äù this is simply how much money the company has
brought in during a quarter or a year. Larger companies sometimes break
down revenues on the income statement according to business sector, geo-
graphic region, or products versus services.
Be sure to check the ‚Äúrevenue recognition policies‚Äù buried in the financial
statements so you know what you‚Äôre looking at‚Äîcompanies can record rev-
enue at different times depending on the business that they‚Äôre in. A software
firm, for example, might record a big chunk of revenue when a product is

shipped to a customer, whereas a service firm might record revenue smoothly
over the life of the service contract.

Also known as cost of goods sold, this number represents the expenses most di-
rectly involved in creating revenue, such as labor costs, raw materials (for man-
ufacturers), or the wholesale price of goods (for retailers). Large companies

Gross Profit
This doesn‚Äôt appear on all income statements, but it‚Äôs simply revenue minus
cost of sales. Once you have gross profit, you can calculate a gross margin,
which is gross profit as a percentage of revenue. Essentially, this tells you how
much a company is able to mark up its goods. As you can see in Figure Ôúµ.Ôú±Ôú∞,
Dell has a gross margin of only Ôú±Ôú∑.Ôúπpercent (Ôú§Ôú∂.Ôú≥billion in gross profit di-
vided by Ôú§Ôú≥Ôúµ.Ôú¥billion in sales) because it sells commodity products. It‚Äôs tough
for Dell to charge much of a premium for the computers it sells.
H-P, meanwhile, sells higher end computer gear for which its customers
are more willing to pay up. If you look at Figure Ôúµ.Ôú±Ôú±, you can see this in its
gross margin: Take product revenue of Ôú§Ôú¥Ôúµ.Ôúπbillion, subtract product costs
of Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú¥.Ôú∂billion, and we have a gross profit of Ôú§Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú¥billion. Divide that back
into revenue, and we‚Äôre left with a gross margin of Ôú≤Ôú¥.Ôú∏percent. As you can
see, the more differentiated a company‚Äôs products are, the more it can mark
up its goods over what it costs to manufacture them. H-P also bundles pro-
prietary software with a lot of the computers it sells, and software has very
high gross margins.
Selling, General, and Administrative Expenses (SG&A)
This number, also known as operating expenses, includes items such as mar-
keting, administrative salaries, and, sometimes, research and development.

(Research and development is usually broken out as a separate line item, as is
marketing for firms that spend large amounts on advertising.) You‚Äôll often see
a relationship between SG&A and gross margin‚Äîfirms that are able to
charge more for their goods (e.g., H-P) have to spend more on salespeople
and marketing. You can get a feel for how efficient a firm is by looking at
SG&A as a percentage of revenues‚Äîa lower percentage of operating expenses
relative to sales generally means a tighter, more cost-effective firm.
H-P, for example (see Figure Ôúµ.Ôú±Ôú≤), spent about Ôú±Ôú∂percent‚Äîor Ôú§Ôúπbillion‚Äî
of its Ôú§ÔúµÔú∂.Ôúµbillion in revenue on SG&A, whereas Dell spent only Ôú∏.Ôúπpercent of
revenue on these types of costs.
Some of the difference results from the fact that Dell sells direct to cus-
tomers and H-P has to pay salespeople to sell big-ticket computers to large
corporations, but it still looks as though Dell runs a leaner shop than H-P.
Unfortunately, it‚Äôs tough to give any hard-and-fast rules as to just how
much a company should be spending on SG&A. Your best bet is to compare a
company with its closest competitors to see which is able to do more with less
and to look at SG&A as a percentage of sales over time. (If it‚Äôs rising fast, watch
out‚Äîthe firm is spending more on overhead without reaping the benefit of
higher sales.)

Depreciation and Amortization
When a company buys an asset intended to last a long time, such as a new
building or a piece of machinery, it charges off a portion of the cost of that
asset on its income statement over a series of years. (Think back to Mike‚Äôs
grilling tongs.) This number is occasionally broken out separately on the in-
come statement, but it‚Äôs usually rolled into operating expenses. It‚Äôs always in-
cluded in the cash flow statement, though, so you can look there to see how
much a company‚Äôs net income was affected by noncash charges such as
depreciation.
Nonrecurring Charges/Gains
This is the catch-all area where companies put all the one-time charges or
gains that aren‚Äôt part of their regular, ongoing operations, such as the cost of
closing a factory or the gain from selling a division. Ideally, you‚Äôd want to see
this area of the income statement blank most of the time.
You should view one-time expenses with a great deal of skepticism. Com-
panies have gotten into the habit of rolling many costs that really are part of
doing business into charges, and firms that are serial chargers‚Äîmeaning that
they seem to take some kind of hit every year‚Äîare much more difficult to

Ôù¶Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨ Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≥ Ôù•Ôù∏Ôù∞Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù©ÔùÆÔù•Ôù§
analyze because all kinds of expenses can be buried in one-time charges. The
reasons behind these charges are always detailed in the notes section of a
firm‚Äôs financial statements, which you should certainly read to understand
what caused the charges.
As you can see in Figure Ôúµ.Ôú±Ôú≥, H-P took a Ôú§Ôú±.Ôú∏billion charge in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤for re-

and another Ôú§Ôú∑Ôú∞Ôú∞million in other assorted charges related to its merger with
Compaq. Although a merger of this size doesn‚Äôt occur every day, which
means we can cut H-P some slack, we can also see that H-P had a good-sized
restructuring charge of Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∏Ôú¥million in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú±. If I owned H-P, I‚Äôd want to look
very carefully at all of those charges to make sure they really were nonrecur-
ring because serial charging is a sign of bad faith on management‚Äôs part.
Operating Income
This number is equal to revenues minus cost of sales and all operating ex-
penses. Theoretically, it represents the profit the company made from its actual
operations, as opposed to interest income, one-time gains, and so forth. In
practice, companies often include nonrecurring expenses (such as write-offs) in
figuring operating income, and you have to add back one-time charges (or
subtract one-time gains) yourself.
Operating income is as close to a solid bottom-line number as you‚Äôre
going to get for most firms. Because it excludes most one-time items, as well
as income from nonoperational sources such as investments, you can use it to
calculate an operating margin, which is fairly comparable across firms and
across industries.
