Ôú∂Ôúµ
Interest Income/Expense
Sometimes interest income and interest expense are listed separately, and
sometimes they are combined into net interest income (or expense, as the case
may be). In either case, this number represents interest the company has paid
on bonds it has issued or received on bonds or cash that it owns. You can get
some insight into the financial health of a firm by looking at its earnings before
interest and taxes relative to its interest expense, which is called an interest cov-
erage ratio. This tells you the extent to which a firm‚Äôs profits can cover needed
interest payments. I talk more about such financial-health measures in the next
chapter.
Taxes
Uncle Sam has to get paid, and tax information is usually the last expense listed
before net income. Unfortunately, corporate taxation is an extremely complex
topic because companies submit a completely separate set of financial state-
ments to the IRS for tax purposes than the ones you and I see filed with the
SEC. (There are a whole host of reasons for this‚Äîthe biggest cause is different
depreciation schedules‚Äîbut it‚Äôs not something you need to worry about.)
In general, the tax rate for U.S. corporations is around Ôú≥Ôúµpercent. If the
tax rate for a company you‚Äôre analyzing is much lower than this, find out why,
and find out whether that tax advantage is likely to be permanent or tempo-
rary. Some firms get tax breaks because they‚Äôre located outside the United
States, even if the bulk of their sales are made here in the States.
In addition, look at the tax rate of the firm you‚Äôre analyzing over time. If it
bounces around from year to year, the firm may be generating earnings by
playing with tax loopholes rather than selling more goods or services. Tax ad-
vantages are nice to have, but politicians have a bad habit of taking them away
at inopportune times, so it‚Äôs not money you want to necessarily count on.
Net Income
This number represents (at least theoretically) the company‚Äôs profit after all
expenses have been paid, and it‚Äôs the number most companies highlight in
their quarterly earnings releases. As we saw in the Mike‚Äôs hot dog example,
net income may or may not be a good representation of the amount of cash
the company has generated. For that, we‚Äôll need to look at the statement of
Ôú∂Ôú∂
Ôù¶Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨ Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≥ Ôù•Ôù∏Ôù∞Ôù¨Ôù°Ôù©ÔùÆÔù•Ôù§
cash flows. Although net income is the number you‚Äôll most often see com-
panies tout in their press releases, don‚Äôt forget that it can be wildly distorted
by one-time charges and/or investment income.
Number of Shares (Basic and Diluted)
This figure represents the number of shares used in calculating earnings per
share; it represents the average number of shares outstanding during the re-
porting period (a quarter or a year). Basic shares include only actual shares of
stock, and you should pretty much ignore it‚Äîthe fact that it‚Äôs still recorded
in financial statements is more of a historical legacy than anything else.
Diluted shares, however, include securities that could potentially be con-
verted into shares of stock, such as stock options and convertible bonds.
Given the amount of egregious granting of stock options that has occurred
over the past several years, it‚Äôs the diluted number that you‚Äôll want to look at,
because you want to know the degree to which your stake in the firm could
potentially be shrunk (or diluted) if all those option-holders convert their op-
tions into shares.
You can see in Figure Ôúµ.Ôú±Ôú¥that H-P‚Äôs basic and diluted shares are the same
but that Dell has about Ôú≤percent more diluted shares. Because diluted shares
can be as much as Ôúµpercent or more higher than basic shares, that‚Äôs not a
huge amount of dilution.
Earnings per Share (Basic and Diluted)
This number, which represents net income divided by number of shares,
usually gets the most attention when a company reports its quarterly or
annual results. It‚Äôs not the end-all, be-all of corporate financial performance,
though‚Äîin fact, without looking at cash flow and many other factors, it‚Äôs
mostly meaningless. So when you read in the paper that a firm ‚Äúbeat‚Äù or
‚Äúmissed‚Äù earnings per share estimates, don‚Äôt get excited. Find out why instead.
The Statement of Cash Flows
This statement is the true touchstone for corporate value creation because it
shows how much cash a company is generating from year to year‚Äîand cash
is what counts (see Figure Ôúµ.Ôú±Ôúµ). In fact, I would almost recommend that you
look at the statement of cash flows first when evaluating a company to see
how much cash it‚Äôs throwing off, then look at the balance sheet to test the
Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù≥Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥ ÔùØÔù¶ Ôù£Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù® Ôù¶Ôù¨ÔùØÔù∑Ôù≥
Ôú∂Ôú∑
firmness of its financial foundation, and only then look at the income state-
ment to check out margins and such.
The cash flow statement strips away all the abstract, noncash items such
as depreciation that you see on the income statement and tells you how much
actual cash the company has generated. Many of the items on this statement
are also found on either the income statement or the balance sheet, but here
they‚Äôre rearranged to highlight the cash generated and how it relates to re-
ported earnings. The cash flow statement is divided into three parts: cash
flows from operating activities, from investing activities, and from financing
activities.
The ‚Äúcash flows from operating activities‚Äù section comes first and it tells
you how much cash the company generated from its business. This is the area
to focus most of your attention on because it‚Äôs the cash-generating power of
the business that we‚Äôre most interested in. Dell‚Äôs statement of cash flows is on
page Ôú∑Ôú∞so you can follow along (see Figure Ôúµ.Ôú±Ôú∂).

Depreciation and Amortization
This is not a cash charge‚Äîremember, Mike didn‚Äôt have to pay anyone just
because his grilling tongs started to wear out‚Äîso we need to add it back to
net income. In Dell‚Äôs case, we add back Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú±Ôú±million.
Tax Benefit from Employee Stock Plans
When an employee exercises stock options, the employer gets to deduct the
gain received by the employee against its corporate income. (Employee com-
pensation is generally tax deductible.) Because the result is a lower tax bill, we
need to add back the tax benefit to the already-taxed net income. Be wary of
this line item‚Äîif it‚Äôs large relative to total operating cash flow and the com-
pany‚Äôs stock has been zooming upwards, you shouldn‚Äôt count on this cash
being around in the future. When the shares sink, fewer employees will exer-
cise their options, and the company will receive a smaller cash tax benefit. As
you can see, Dell‚Äôs tax benefit from stock option exercises dropped in half be-
tween Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú±and Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤and sunk again in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥. Over the same time period,
Dell‚Äôs stock wasn‚Äôt such a hot performer‚Äîand that‚Äôs not a coincidence.

Changes in Working Capital
Remember when Mike let some folks buy hot dogs on credit and owed
money to the grocer for those extra buns? Both of those actions affected
working capital, and they‚Äôd be accounted for here. If a company is owed
more money by customers this year than it was last year, accounts receivable
increase and cash flow decreases; if it owes more money to suppliers, accounts
payable increase and so does cash flow. Finally, if a firm pumps more money
into inventory that doesn‚Äôt sell, cash flow decreases. Remember, inventory
ties up capital.
In Dell‚Äôs case, we need to go back to the balance sheet to see where the
Ôú§Ôú±.Ôú≤billion entry for the line item came from (see Figure Ôúµ.Ôú±Ôú∑).

Dell‚Äôs pocket (see Figure Ôúµ.Ôú±Ôú∏).

Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∏million change in inventory, which gives us Ôú§ÔúµÔú∂Ôúπmillion in cash flow.
Add in the Ôú§ÔúµÔú∞Ôú∞million increase in ‚Äúaccrued and other‚Äù‚Äîmainly warranties
and employee bonuses that Dell may have to pay out in the future‚Äîas well
as some other odds and ends detailed on the bowels of the financial filings,
and you wind up with the Ôú§Ôú±.Ôú≤Ôú≤Ôú∞billion in ‚Äúchanges in operating working
capital‚Äù that you see on Dell‚Äôs cash flow statement.
You don‚Äôt need to go through all of this every time you look at a statement
of cash flows because everything gets neatly netted out for you in the ‚Äúnet cash
provided by operating activities‚Äù line. But because the ‚Äúchanges in working
capital‚Äù entry is often the biggest cause of differences between net income and
operating cash flow, this is an area that you‚Äôll want to pay attention to‚Äîhence
our detailed analysis.
One-Time Charges
Remember these? Dell didn‚Äôt have any, but H-P did (see Figure Ôúµ.Ôú±Ôúπ). Because
most of these charges were noncash charges‚Äîthat is, H-P didn‚Äôt write a check
made out to someone named Restructuring‚Äîthey need to be added back
when figuring cash flow (similar to depreciation, which is also noncash).

This is your holy grail for figuring out whether a company is generating cash.
Also known as operating cash flow, it‚Äôs the result of adding or subtracting the

Now we arrive at the second portion of the statement of cash flows, the
‚Äúcash flow from investing activities‚Äù section. These are activities that involve
acquiring or disposing of PP&E, corporate acquisitions, and any sales or pur-
chases of investments.
Capital Expenditures
This figure represents money spent on items that last a long time, such as
PP&E‚Äîbasically, anything needed to keep the business running and grow-
ing at its current rate. Operating cash flow minus capital expenditures equals
free cash flow, or the amount of cash the company generates after investing in
its business. We can see in Figure Ôúµ.Ôú≤Ôú∞that Dell spent Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞Ôúµmillion on capex.
Investment Proceeds
Companies often take some of their excess cash and invest it in bonds or
stocks in an effort to get a better return than they could in a basic savings ac-
count. This number tells us how much money the company has made (or
lost) on such investments. As you can see in Figure Ôúµ.Ôú≤Ôú∞, Dell invested Ôú§Ôú∏.Ôú∑
billion of its cash in securities of one sort or another (purchases) and received

Ôú§Ôú∑.Ôú∑billion from previous investments that either reached maturity or were

The final portion of the statement of cash flows is the ‚Äúcash flow from fi-
nancing activities‚Äù section. Financing activities include any transactions with
the company‚Äôs owners or creditors. Items that typically show up in this sec-
tion are briefly described next.
Dividends Paid
Unlike many line items, this one is just what it sounds like. You can see that
H-P paid a total of Ôú§Ôú∏Ôú∞Ôú±million in dividends in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤(see Figure Ôúµ.Ôú≤Ôú±).
Issuance/Purchase of Common Stock
This is an important number to look at because it indicates how a company is
financing its activities. Rapidly growing companies often issue large amounts
of new stock, which can dilute the value of existing shares but which also give
the company cash for expansion. Slower growing companies that generate a
lot of free cash flow tend to buy back significant amounts of their own stock,
though companies that issue many stock options to their employees also buy
back stock to minimize dilution.
You can see this kind of buyback activity in Figure Ôúµ.Ôú≤Ôú≤of Dell‚Äôs financial
statements printed to the right. Under ‚Äúpurchase of common stock,‚Äù note that
Dell spent Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôú≥billion repurchasing its own stock, and on the income state-
ment, you can see that the number of diluted shares outstanding fell about
Ôú≥percent, from Ôú≤.Ôú∑Ôú≤Ôú∂billion to Ôú≤.Ôú∂Ôú¥Ôú¥billion. Although share repurchases are
generally a shareholder-friendly use of excess cash‚Äîafter all, the fewer shares
outstanding, the larger the piece of the company that each shareholder owns‚Äî

you have to view share buybacks with caution when they come from firms

essentially selling shares to their employees at low prices and buying it back on
the open market at much higher prices, which is not the best use of capital.

Conclusion
Painful though it may have been, you‚Äôve just gained something very valuable:
You now know enough about financial statements to meaningfully analyze a
company. Although you can (and should) spend much more time learning
the nooks and crannies of financial reporting, understanding how a company
generates cash is now within your grasp, and that‚Äôs what really matters. The
details aren‚Äôt nearly as important as the basic concepts that we‚Äôve just walked
through. Now that you‚Äôve made it through most of this chapter, you‚Äôre al-
ready ahead of the pack.
To wrap things up, let‚Äôs follow a dollar through Dell to see what happens
to it. A customer‚Äîlet‚Äôs call him Steven, just for kicks‚Äîbuys a computer for
Ôú§Ôú±,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞from Dell. Dell turns around and uses Ôú§Ôú∏Ôú≤Ôú±to pay manufacturing em-
ployees and parts suppliers (cost of goods sold). How do I know this? Look at
Dell‚Äôs income statement (Figure Ôúµ.Ôú≤Ôú¥): The firm spent Ôú§Ôú≤Ôúπ,Ôú∞ÔúµÔúµ‚ÅÑÔú§Ôú≥Ôúµ,Ôú¥Ôú∞Ôú¥, or Ôú∏Ôú≤.Ôú±
percent, of every dollar in sales paying for the cost of manufacturing the com-
puter, and Ôú∏Ôú≤.Ôú±percent of Ôú§Ôú±,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞is Ôú§Ôú∏Ôú≤Ôú±. So. Dell has Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∑Ôúπof the Ôú§Ôú±,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞it re-
ceived for the computer left over after paying for the cost of making the
computer.
Now, look at the rest of the income statement (Figure Ôúµ.Ôú≤Ôúµ) to see what
happens to the remaining Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∑Ôúπ. About Ôú§Ôú∏Ôú∂is spent on television ads and cor-
porate overhead (selling, general, and administrative), Ôú§Ôú±Ôú≥goes to research and
development, and another Ôú§Ôú≤Ôúµ.ÔúµÔú∞goes to Uncle Sam. After adding back a little

vested in the business, used to buy back stock, or simply kept in a bank ac-

one of these numbers yourself right from Dell‚Äôs income statement‚Äîjust divide
the line item by total sales, and multiply the resulting percentage by Ôú§Ôú±,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞.
What do we now know about Dell‚Äôs business? For one thing, we know
that the profit margins are pretty low‚Äîwith only about Ôú§Ôú∂Ôú∞in net profits of
every Ôú§Ôú±,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞in sales, we can see that most of Dell‚Äôs revenue goes right back
out the door to the folks who build the computers, supply the parts, and run
the company.
We also know that Dell doesn‚Äôt spend much on R&D or marketing,
which makes sense because the company is striving to be the low-cost pro-
ducer in a commodity market. If Dell were a pharmaceutical company and

spent only Ôú±.Ôúµpercent of revenue on research, we‚Äôd get mighty worried. But
R&D isn‚Äôt all that important to Dell, so we‚Äôre not too concerned about it.
There‚Äôs much more you can learn about a company than this, and that‚Äôs
exactly what we do in the next chapter‚Äîwe take what we‚Äôve learned about fi-
nancial statements and use it to generate analytical insights into how a busi-
ness is functioning.
But don‚Äôt overlook the power of this simple test: If you can‚Äôt understand
how a dollar flows from a company‚Äôs customers back through to shareholders,
something‚Äôs amiss. Either the company‚Äôs business model is too confusing or
you need to do more digging before committing any of your money.
Investor‚Äôs Checklist: Financial Statements Explained
3 The balance sheet tells you how much a company owns, how much it
owes, and the difference between the two, which represents the value of the
money that shareholders have invested in the firm. Shareholders‚Äô equity in
a firm is the value of the firm‚Äôs assets minus its liabilities.
3 Because the balance sheet must balance at all times, any change in assets
or liabilities will cause a corresponding change in equity. If a firm gener-
ates huge profits that drive an increase in assets, equity will also increase.
3 Keep an eye on the trend in accounts receivable compared with sales. If
the firm is booking a large amount of revenue that hasn‚Äôt yet been paid
for, this can be a sign of trouble.
3 When you‚Äôre evaluating a company‚Äôs liabilities, remember that debt is a
fixed cost. A big chunk of long-term debt can be risky for a company be-
cause the interest has to be paid no matter how business is doing.
3 Be wary of companies that report ‚Äúnonrecurring‚Äù charges, particularly if
they make a habit of it. All kinds of expenses can be buried in ‚Äúone-time‚Äù
charges.
3 The statement of cash flows is the true touchstone for corporate value cre-
ation because it shows how much cash a company is generating from year
to year‚Äîand cash is what counts. Look at the cash flow statement first.
3 When you‚Äôre analyzing a company, make sure you can understand how a
dollar flows through the business. If you can‚Äôt do this, you probably don‚Äôt
understand the company well enough to buy the stock.
Ôú∂
Analyzing a Company‚Äî
The Basics
NÔùØÔù∑ Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù°Ôù¥ Ôù∑Ôù•have the basic tools of financial statement analysis in hand,
we can start tearing into companies. Because this can be a daunting task, I
suggest that you break down the process into five areas:
Ôú±. Growth: How fast has the company grown, what are the sources of its
growth, and how sustainable is that growth likely to be?
Ôú≤. Profitability: What kind of a return does the company generate on the
capital it invests?
Ôú≥. Financial health: How solid is the firm‚Äôs financial footing?
Ôú¥. Risks/bear case: What are the risks to your investment case? There are ex-
cellent reasons not to invest in even the best-looking firms. Make sure you
look at the full story and investigate the negatives as well as the positives.
Ôúµ. Management: Who‚Äôs running the show? Are they running the company
for the benefit of shareholders or themselves? This is such a critical topic
that I‚Äôve devoted an entire chapter (Chapter Ôú∑) to it.
Ôú∏Ôú∞
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
One word of caution: In this chapter and the next, we‚Äôll be concerned
only with evaluating the quality of the company. However, this is only half
the story because even the best companies are poor investments if pur-
chased at too high a price. We‚Äôll cover how to evaluate stocks in Chapters Ôúπ
and Ôú±Ôú∞, where I‚Äôll show you how to estimate the right price to pay for a
company‚Äôs shares.
Growth
The allure of strong growth has probably led more investors into temptation
than anything else. High growth rates are heady stuff‚Äîa company that man-
ages to increase its earnings at Ôú±Ôúµpercent for five years will double its profits,
and who wouldn‚Äôt want to do that? Unfortunately, a slew of academic re-
search shows that strong earnings growth is not very persistent over a series of
years; in other words, a track record of high earnings growth does not neces-
sarily lead to high earnings growth in the future.
Why is this? Because the total economic pie is growing only so fast‚Äîafter
all, the long-run aggregate growth of corporate earnings has historically been
slightly slower than the growth of the economy‚Äîstrong and rapidly growing
profits attract intense competition. Companies that are growing fast and pil-
ing up profits soon find other companies trying to get a piece of the action
for themselves.
You can‚Äôt just look at a series of past growth rates and assume that they‚Äôll
predict the future‚Äîif investing were that easy, money managers would be
paid much less, and this book would be much shorter. It‚Äôs critical to investi-
gate the sources of a company‚Äôs growth rate and assess the quality of the
growth. High-quality growth that comes from selling more goods and enter-
ing new markets is more sustainable than low-quality growth that‚Äôs generated
by cost-cutting or accounting tricks.
The Four Sources
In the long run, sales growth drives earnings growth. Although profit growth
can outpace sales growth for a while if a company is able to do an excellent
job cutting costs or fiddling with the financial statements, this kind of situa-
tion simply isn‚Äôt sustainable over the long haul‚Äîthere‚Äôs a limit to how much
costs can be cut, and there are only so many financial tricks that companies
ÔùßÔù≤ÔùØÔù∑Ôù¥Ôù®
Ôú∏Ôú±
can use to boost the bottom line. In general, sales growth stems from one of
four areas:
Ôú±. Selling more goods or services
Ôú≤. Raising prices
Ôú≥. Selling new goods or services
Ôú¥. Buying another company
The easiest way to grow is to do whatever you‚Äôre doing better than your
competitors, sell more products than they do, and steal market share from
them. Mobile-phone giant Nokia, for example, increased its share of the
global mobile phone market from around Ôú±Ôúµpercent in the mid-Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s to
around Ôú≥Ôúµpercent today by simply doing a better job selling phones.
Raising prices can also be a great way for companies to boost their top
lines, although it takes a strong brand or a captive market to be able to do it
successfully for very long. Anheuser-Busch, for example, has historically been
able to raise prices by Ôú±percent to Ôú≥percent per year because it has a strong
portfolio of popular brands such as Budweiser, Bud Lite, and Michelob.
Cable companies have also used pricing power to increase their top lines,
though it‚Äôs due more to monopoly power than strong brands. Throughout
the Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s, most markets had only one cable provider for any given consumer,
so cable firms were able to push through annual price increases of Ôú∂percent
to Ôú∑percent.
If there‚Äôs not much more market share to be taken or your customers are
very price-sensitive, you can do what Wal-Mart did and expand your market
by selling products that you hadn‚Äôt sold before. In the mid-Ôú±ÔúπÔú∏Ôú∞s, founder and
then-CEO Sam Walton saw that the firm‚Äôs growth was likely to hit a wall at
some point in the ensuing decade, so he began investigating new markets.
After a trip to Europe, where European retailers had begun to build hypermar-
kets that sold everything from clothes to toys to ketchup under one roof, Wal-
ton figured that groceries could be Wal-Mart‚Äôs next big market. Fifteen years
later, Wal-Mart is the largest seller of groceries in the United States, and super-
centers that include food sales account for an ever-larger percentage of its sales.
Remember, the goal of this type of analysis is simply to know why a com-
pany is growing. In Anheuser-Busch‚Äôs case, you‚Äôd want to know how much
Ôú∏Ôú≤
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
growth is coming from price increases (more expensive beer), how much is
coming from volume increases (more beer drinkers), and how much is com-
ing from market share growth (more Budweiser drinkers). Once you‚Äôre able
to segment a firm‚Äôs growth rate into its components, you‚Äôll have a much bet-
ter handle on where that growth is likely to come from in the future‚Äîand
when it may tap out.
The fourth source of sales growth‚Äîacquisitions‚Äîdeserves special atten-
tion. Acquisitive firms are often darlings of Wall Street because they‚Äôre major
consumers of investment banking services. They‚Äôre often looking to either
raise capital for a new acquisition or find a new target, which means they
constantly have folks in suits lining up at their door trying to pitch them on
deals. It shouldn‚Äôt come as any surprise, then, that the analysts that follow
acquisition-hungry firms usually have nice things to say.
Unfortunately, the historical track record for acquisitions is mixed.
Most acquisitions fail to produce positive gains for shareholders of the ac-
quiring firm, and one study showed that even acquisitions of small, related
businesses‚Äîwhich you‚Äôd think would have a good chance of working out
well‚Äîsucceeded only about half the time.Ôú±
There are a host of reasons for this. For one, acquisitive firms have to
keep buying bigger and bigger firms to keep growing at the same rate‚Äîand
the bigger a target firm is, the harder it is to check out thoroughly, which in-
creases the risk of buying a pig in a poke. Even Warren Buffett found this out
when Berkshire Hathaway bought GenRe, a huge reinsurance firm, in Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∏.
Buffett knows more about insurance than almost anyone alive, but the
GenRe purchase turned out to be less than stellar. Buffett himself said some
time after the deal that GenRe was in worse shape than he‚Äôd thought when he
bought it. Not even the savviest of CEOs can know all the skeletons that may
be lurking in a huge target firm.
Another reason to be skeptical of acquisitive firms is simply that buying
other companies takes time and money. Targets have to be investigated, in-
vestment bankers have to be paid, and acquired firms have to be integrated
into the new owner, all of which steals resources away from running the core
business. If executives are spending all of their energy looking for ways to
Ôú±Mark L. Sirower, The Synergy Trap (New York: Free Press, Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∑).
ÔùßÔù≤ÔùØÔù∑Ôù¥Ôù®
Ôú∏Ôú≥
make the firm bigger, rather than better, the wheels are sure to come off the
cart eventually.
From the investor‚Äôs perspective, however, the biggest reason to be leery of
a growth-by-acquisition strategy is even simpler: It makes the company more
difficult to understand. Acquisitive firms usually post many merger-related
charges and often wind up restating their financial results, which means the
results of the acquiring company can be obscured in all the merger-related
confusion. As a result, two things can happen:
Ôú±. An unscrupulous management team can use the fog created by constant
acquisitions to artificially juice results, and this financial tinkering can
take a long time to come to light because it‚Äôs buried in the necessary fi-
nancial rejiggering that comes with any sizeable acquisition.
Ôú≤. The true growth rate of the underlying business may be impossible to fig-
ure out, especially if management is evasive about giving out information
on the firm‚Äôs organic growth rate.
Bottom line: If you don‚Äôt know how fast the company would have grown
without acquisitions, don‚Äôt buy the shares‚Äîbecause you never know when
the acquisitions will stop. Remember, the goal of a successful investor is to
buy great businesses, not successful merger and acquisition machines.
Questioning Quality
As you might have guessed, I generally view acquisitions as a very low-quality
way of generating growth. Unfortunately, there are plenty of other ways of
making growth look better than it really is, especially when we turn our at-
tention to earnings growth rather than sales growth. (Sales growth is much
more difficult to fake.) Although the list of tricks that companies can use to
boost earnings growth even as sales growth falters is a long one, there are a
few basic areas to watch out for: Changing tax rates, changing share counts,
pension gains, one-time gains (often from selling off businesses), and ram-
pant cost cutting are among the most common. (I‚Äôll show you the full spec-
trum of earnings-management tricks in Chapter  Ôú∏.)
In general, any time that earnings growth outstrips sales growth over a
long period‚Äîfor example, Ôúµto Ôú±Ôú∞years‚Äîyou need to dig into the numbers to
Ôú∏Ôú¥
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
see how the company keeps squeezing out more profits from stagnant sales. A
big difference between the growth rate of net income and operating income or
cash flow from operations can also hint at something unsustainable.
IBM is a classic example of what I call ‚Äúmanufactured growth,‚Äù because it
used almost all of the previously mentioned techniques to pump up its bot-
tom line during the Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s. As shown in Figure Ôú∂.Ôú±, Big Blue‚Äôs earnings per
share growth looks pretty good since the Lou Gerstner-led turnaround began
in the early Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s‚Äîclose to double digits most years, which is not bad for a
company of this size.
But when we look at operating income, it looks as though the company
was growing much slower, while sales growth was stuck around Ôúµpercent on
average. As a double-check, take a quick look at cash flow from operations‚Äî
unfortunately, this also looks pretty stagnant from Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔúµthrough the end of
the decade (see Figure Ôú∂.Ôú≤).
So what was driving those great earnings-per-share results that Gerst-
ner‚Äôs IBM kept turning like clockwork? A whole host of items: For one, the
firm‚Äôs tax rate plunged from Ôú¥Ôú∂percent in Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔúµto around Ôú≥Ôú∞percent by the
end of the decade. For another, the firm cut overhead spending substan-
tially during the Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s‚Äîa laudable accomplishment, given the bureaucratic
nature of the firm.
Finally, IBM bought back about a quarter of its shares during the latter
half of the Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s‚Äîfewer shares outstanding meant more earnings per share‚Äî
and benefited from an overfunded pension plan that boosted earnings. As
you can see, a simple comparison of IBM‚Äôs earnings per share relative to its

operating income and cash flow raised many red flags‚Äîenough that any in-
vestor looking at the company in the late Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s could have been skeptical
about the quality of the firm‚Äôs earnings growth. (Note: Low-quality growth
doesn‚Äôt imply that a company is cooking the books, merely that the growth
rate isn‚Äôt likely to be sustained over the long haul.)
You should also keep an eye out for one-time gains and losses that can
distort a historical growth rate. A big gain from the sale of a division, for ex-
ample, can make growth look better than it really was. Large losses can also
affect growth rates‚Äîif a company‚Äôs earnings were depressed in the first year
of a three- or five-year period, the firm‚Äôs growth rate will be overstated be-
cause growth will be calculated from a depressed base. So don‚Äôt take the trail-
ing three-year and five-year growth rates that you see as gospel‚Äîalways check
to see what‚Äôs behind the numbers.
In general, any time you can‚Äôt pinpoint the sources of a company‚Äôs growth
rate‚Äîor the reasons for a sharp divergence between the top and bottom
lines, as was the case with Big Blue‚Äîyou should be wary of the quality of
that growth rate. Paying less taxes and buying back shares are good things for
shareholders, no question, but they‚Äôre short-term fixes rather than long-term
sources of earnings growth.
A word on cost cutting: All things equal, I‚Äôd rather own a more efficient
firm‚Äîone with lower overhead costs‚Äîthan a less efficient firm. However,
cost cuts are not a sustainable long-term source of earnings growth, and if
you‚Äôre looking at a firm that‚Äôs been slashing costs, you should be aware that at
some point there won‚Äôt be any more costs to cut. Earnings growth will even-
tually slow unless sales growth speeds up.
At some point, a cost-cutting firm is going to find that it has become as
efficient as possible, and sales and earnings growth will converge unless the
firm manages to boost revenue growth. So when you see a firm with earnings
gains being driven by cost cutting, make sure you think about the sustain-
ability of those cost cuts because they won‚Äôt be around forever.
Profitability
Now we come to the second‚Äîand, in many ways, most crucial‚Äîpart of the
analysis process: How much profit is the company generating relative to the
amount of money invested in the business? This is the real key to separating
Ôú∏Ôú∂
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
great companies from average ones, because the job of any company is to take
money from outside investors and invest it to generate a return. The higher
that return, the more attractive the business.
I‚Äôve already briefly discussed profit margins and the importance of under-
standing whether cost cuts or price hikes are driving an increase in profit mar-
gins. Comparing cash flow from operations to reported earnings per share is
another good way to get a rough idea of a firm‚Äôs profitability because cash flow
from operations represents real profits.
But neither net margin nor cash flow from operations accounts for the
amount of capital that‚Äôs tied up in the business, and that‚Äôs something we can‚Äôt
ignore. We need to know how much economic profit a firm is able to gener-
ate per dollar of capital employed because it will have more excess profits to
reinvest, which will give it an advantage over less-efficient competitors.
Think about it this way‚Äîa company‚Äôs management is similar to the
manager of a mutual fund. A mutual fund manager takes investors‚Äô money
and earns a return on it by investing in stocks and bonds. Wouldn‚Äôt you
rather put your money with an equity manager who has consistently gener-
ated returns of Ôú±Ôú≤percent per year than one who has returned an average of
only Ôúπpercent per year?
Companies aren‚Äôt much different. They take shareholders‚Äô money and in-
vest it in their own businesses to create wealth. By measuring the return that
a company‚Äôs management has achieved through this investment process, we
know how good they are at efficiently transforming capital into profits. Just
like a mutual fund, a company whose management is investing well enough
to generate returns on capital of Ôú±Ôú≤percent is usually a more attractive invest-
ment than a company that returns only Ôúπpercent on its capital.
Our two tools for assessing corporate profitability are return on capital
and free cash flow. I start with return on assets (ROA) and return on equity
(ROE), which we first saw in Chapter Ôú≥when we evaluated economic moats.
Then I‚Äôll show you how to compare free cash flow to ROE and I‚Äôll finish up
with a quick discussion of a sophisticated measure of profitability called re-
turn on invested capital (ROIC).
Return on Assets (ROA)
You already know the first component of ROA. It‚Äôs simply net margin, or
net income divided by sales, and it tells us how much of each dollar of sales
Ôù∞Ôù≤ÔùØÔù¶Ôù©Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¢Ôù©Ôù¨Ôù©Ôù¥Ôùπ
Ôú∏Ôú∑
a company keeps as earnings after paying all the costs of doing business.
The second component is asset turnover, or sales divided by assets, which
tells us roughly how efficient a firm is at generating revenue from each dol-
lar of assets. Multiply these two together, and you have return on assets,
which is simply the amount of profits that a company is able to generate per
dollar of assets.
Think of ROA as a measure of efficiency. Companies with high ROAs
are better at translating assets into profits. We can see this easily when we
compare a top-notch retailer such as Best Buy with a firm like Circuit City,
which was struggling in the late Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s and early Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞s. Since Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∏, Circuit
City‚Äôs returns on assets have been around Ôú¥percent to Ôúµpercent, whereas
Best Buy‚Äôs improved from Ôúµpercent to almost Ôú±Ôú∞percent (see Figures Ôú∂.Ôú≥
and Ôú∂.Ôú¥.)
Higher profit margins‚Äîalmost Ôú≥percent for Best Buy and below Ôú≤per-
cent for Circuit City‚Äîare part of the picture, but higher asset turnover is a
bigger differentiator between the two. In Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤, for example, each dollar that
Circuit City had invested in property and inventory (the two biggest assets
for most retailers) generated about Ôú§Ôú≤.ÔúµÔú∞in sales, while the same dollar in-
vested by Best Buy generated Ôú§Ôú≥.Ôú≤Ôú∞in sales. Clearly, Best Buy was running a
more efficient shop than Circuit City and was much better at transforming
its assets into profits.

ROA helps us understand that there are two routes to excellent opera-
tional profitability: You can charge high prices for your products (high mar-
gins), or you can turn over your assets quickly. Often, you‚Äôll see companies
with lower profit margins, such as grocery stores and discount retailers, em-
phasize high asset turnover as a way to achieve solid ROAs. For any business
that can‚Äôt charge a big premium for its goods, tight inventory management is
critical because it keeps down the amount of capital tied up in assets, which
helps pump up return on assets. On the flip side, companies that can mark
up their goods in a big way‚Äîa luxury retailer such as Tiffany, for example‚Äî
can afford to have more capital tied up in their assets because they make up
for low asset turnover with high profit margins.
Just using ROA would be fine if all companies were big piles of assets, but
many firms are at least partially financed with debt, which gives their returns
a leverage component that we need to take into account. Our next measure
of returns on capital, return on equity, lets us do this.
Return on Equity (ROE)
Return on equity is a great overall measure of a company‚Äôs profitability
because it measures the efficiency with which a company uses shareholders‚Äô
equity‚Äîin other words, it measures how good the company is at earning a
decent return on the shareholder‚Äôs money. Think of it as measuring profits
per dollar of shareholders‚Äô capital.
To calculate ROE, multiply ROA by a firm‚Äôs financial leverage ratio:

Ôù∞Ôù≤ÔùØÔù¶Ôù©Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¢Ôù©Ôù¨Ôù©Ôù¥Ôùπ
Ôú∏Ôúπ
Because Return on assets = Net margin √ó Asset turnover, ROE in all its
glory equals:
Net margin √ó Asset turnover √ó Financial leverage
You‚Äôll notice that we‚Äôve introduced a new metric‚Äîfinancial leverage,
which is essentially a measure of how much debt a company carries, relative
to shareholders‚Äô equity. Unlike net margin and asset turnover‚Äîfor which
higher ratios are almost unequivocally better‚Äîfinancial leverage is some-
thing you need to watch carefully. As with any kind of debt, a judicious
amount can boost returns, but too much can lead to disaster.
Look at the kind of business the firm is in. If it‚Äôs fairly steady, a company
can probably take on large amounts of debt without too much risk because
there‚Äôs only a small chance of the business falling off a cliff and the company
being caught short when bondholders demand their interest payments. On
the flip side, be very wary of a high financial leverage ratio if a company‚Äôs
business is cyclical or volatile. Because interest payments are fixed, the com-
pany has to pay them whether business is good or bad.
Therefore, we have three levers that can boost ROE‚Äînet margins, asset
turnover, and financial leverage. For example, a firm could have only so-so
margins and modest levels of financial leverage, but it could do a great job
with asset turnover (e.g., a well-run discount retailer such as Wal-Mart).
Companies with high asset turnover are extremely efficient at extracting
more dollars of revenue for each dollar they have invested in hard assets. A
firm could also excel at convincing customers to pay up for its products‚Äî
asset turns might be just middling, and the firm might not have much lever-
age, but it would have great profit margins (e.g., a luxury goods company
such as Coach). Finally, a firm can boost its ROE to respectable territory by
taking on good-size amounts of leverage (e.g., mature firms such as utilities).
Although it‚Äôs tough to generalize, let me offer some rough benchmarks
for evaluating firms‚Äô ROEs. In general, any nonfinancial firm that can gener-
ate consistent ROEs above Ôú±Ôú∞percent without excessive leverage is at least
worth investigating. As of mid-Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, only about one-tenth of the nonfinan-
cial firms in Morningstar‚Äôs database were able to post an ROE above Ôú±Ôú∞per-
cent for each of the past five years, so you can see how tough it is to post this
ÔúπÔú∞
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
kind of performance. And if you can find a company with the potential for
consistent ROEs over Ôú≤Ôú∞percent, there‚Äôs a good chance you‚Äôre really on to
something.
Two caveats when you‚Äôre using ROE to evaluate firms: First, banks always
have enormous financial leverage ratios, so don‚Äôt be scared off by a leverage
ratio that looks high relative to a nonbank. (We go over how to evaluate
banks‚Äô financial health in Chapter Ôú±Ôú∑.) In addition, because banks‚Äô leverage is
always so high, you want to raise the bar for financial firms‚Äîlook for consis-
tent ROEs above Ôú±Ôú≤percent or so.
The second caveat concerns firms with ROEs that look too good to be
true because they‚Äôre usually just that. ROEs above Ôú¥Ôú∞percent or so are often
meaningless because they‚Äôve probably been distorted by the firm‚Äôs financial
structure. Firms that have been recently spun off from parent firms, com-
panies that have bought back many of their shares, and companies that have
taken massive charges often have very skewed ROEs because their equity base
is depressed. If you see an ROE over Ôú¥Ôú∞percent, check to see if the company
has any of these characteristics.
Free Cash Flow
In the previous chapter, I introduced you to cash flow from operations
(CFO), which measures how much cash a company generates. As useful as
CFO is, it doesn‚Äôt take into account the money that a firm has to spend on
maintaining and expanding its business. To do this, we need to subtract cap-
ital expenditures, which is money used to buy fixed assets. The result is free
cash flow:
Free cash flow = Cash flow from operations ‚àíCapital spending
Thinking back to our hot dog stand example, suppose Mike was so suc-
cessful that he decided to use the cash he‚Äôd generated to build a second hot
dog stand. The cost of building that stand would be posted to ‚Äúcapital spend-
ing,‚Äù and subtracted from free cash flow.
Why? We need to be able to separate out businesses that are net users of
capital‚Äîones that spend more than they take in‚Äîfrom businesses that are
net producers of capital because it‚Äôs only that excess cash that really belongs
to us as shareholders. You may sometimes see free cash flow referred to as
Ôù∞Ôù≤ÔùØÔù¶Ôù©Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¢Ôù©Ôù¨Ôù©Ôù¥Ôùπ
ÔúπÔú±
‚Äúowner earnings,‚Äù because that‚Äôs exactly what it is: the amount of money the
owner of a company could withdraw from the treasury without harming the
company‚Äôs ongoing business.
A firm that generates a great deal of free cash flow can do all sorts of
things with the money‚Äîsave it for future investment opportunities, use it
for acquisitions, buy back shares, and so forth. Free cash flow gives financial
flexibility because the firm isn‚Äôt relying on the capital markets to fund its ex-
pansion. Firms that have negative free cash flow have to take out loans or sell
additional shares to keep things going, and that can become a risky proposi-
tion if the market becomes unsettled at a critical time for the company.
As with ROE, it‚Äôs tough to generalize about how much free cash flow is
enough. However, I think it‚Äôs reasonable to say that any firm that‚Äôs able to con-
vert more than Ôúµpercent of sales to free cash flow‚Äîjust divide free cash flow by
sales to get this percentage‚Äîis doing a solid job at generating excess cash.
Putting Return on Equity and Free Cash Flow Together
One good way to think about the returns a company is generating is to use the
profitability matrix, which looks at a company‚Äôs ROE relative to the amount
of free cash flow it‚Äôs generating. Figure Ôú∂.Ôúµshows free cash flow along one side
and ROE on the other side, and this matrix can tell you a great deal about the
kind of company you‚Äôre analyzing.
Companies such as Microsoft, Pfizer, and First Data all have very high
ROEs. People write books about how to manage a business as well as these
companies do, and it‚Äôs easy to see why‚Äîthey‚Äôre all money machines. In-
vestors pour new money into them, and large amounts of extra money get
spit out. Their managements are very, very good at earning a high return on
shareholders‚Äô money.
If you follow any of these companies at all, you‚Äôll notice they have an-
other thing in common besides high ROEs‚Äîtheir stocks all had valuations
that were high during the bull market of the Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s. Again, it‚Äôs easy to see why:
A company that can earn a high return on its shareholders‚Äô money is worth
more to those same shareholders.
Looking at the other axis, we see that these companies are also very good
at generating free cash flow. Pfizer, for example, generated more than Ôú§Ôú∏bil-
lion in free cash flow in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤. That‚Äôs Ôú§Ôú∏billion Pfizer made after spending
whatever it needed to invest in its business. Pfizer could have chosen to pay

that Ôú§Ôú∏billion‚Äîwhich worked out to about Ôú§Ôú±.Ôú≥Ôú±per share‚Äîout to share-
holders. In fact, that‚Äôs exactly what older, more mature companies often do
with their free cash flow. Their businesses aren‚Äôt growing very fast, and they
figure that shareholders can earn a better return on the free cash flow than
they can. So, they return it to shareholders in the form of dividends. (This is
why slow-growth firms often have such high yields.)
Pfizer, on the other hand, thinks that it can figure out a way to invest that
Ôú§Ôú∏billion more profitably than its shareholders. Because the company is in a
relatively fast-growing area of the economy‚Äîhealth care‚Äîand has a solid

track record of turning out profitable new drugs, it may very well be able to

Microsoft has over the past several years, we‚Äôd probably conclude that the
company doesn‚Äôt have many profitable avenues for reinvesting its excess prof-
its. In this case, we‚Äôd want the firm to pay a dividend or buy back shares. Al-
though Microsoft‚Äôs dividend isn‚Äôt very large at this writing, at least the
company has one, which means that it has recognized that internal reinvest-
ment opportunities are diminishing.
On the bottom half of the matrix, we have companies such as Amazon
.com, JetBlue, Comcast, and Lowe‚Äôs, which generate low or negative free cash
flow. Companies like these aren‚Äôt generating much free cash because they‚Äôre using
all the cash their businesses generate‚Äîand then some‚Äîto invest in expansion.
They‚Äôre investing heavily because they hope that those expansion efforts will pay
off in the form of fat profits in the future. Amazon, for example, is spending
heavily on building a brand and expanding its Web site, while JetBlue is spending
heavily on new airplanes so that it can expand its service to new cities.
JetBlue and Amazon are like young entrepreneurs. They‚Äôve taken out
loans and maxed out their credit cards, and they‚Äôre plowing every cent they
have into building and expanding their businesses. Although they‚Äôre not
earning much in the way of profits right now, folks are investing in their busi-
nesses because they expect these companies to be very profitable sometime in
the future, which is when investors will be rewarded. Pfizer, on the other
hand, is more like a successful, middle-age businessman. He‚Äôs already proven
he can earn a good return on shareholders‚Äô money, so folks line up outside his
door for the privilege of investing in his ventures.
You‚Äôd be taking a lot less risk investing with the older businessman than
you would with the young entrepreneur‚Äîthough that entrepreneur might
just pay you back many, many times over. Just remember that for every Jeff
Bezos or Steve Jobs, there are literally hundreds of entrepreneurs who never
paid their investors a dime.
There‚Äôs nothing wrong with investing in the entrepreneurs of the world,
as long as you know what you‚Äôre getting into. A profitability matrix can help
you separate your long shots from your core holdings.
Think of the profitability matrix like this: That upper right-hand corner,
where Pfizer sits, is the sweet spot‚Äîexcess cash and the ability to earn a high
ÔúπÔú¥
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
return on it. Companies in this square tend to be the cream of the crop and
have a low level of business risk. (They might be very risky stocks, though, if
they‚Äôre trading at high valuations.)
Moving down to the bottom right, where Lowe‚Äôs is, you see companies
that are reinvesting all of their cash in expansion but are still able to generate
a high return on shareholders‚Äô money. If these firms still have profitable rein-
vestment opportunities, they should be spending all the cash they generate on
expansion. For example, Starbucks and Home Depot posted high ROEs and
negative free cash flow all through the Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s because they were plowing every
cent they earned into building more stores.
In the bottom left are young companies growing like weeds, but which
haven‚Äôt yet proven that they can earn a decent ROE‚Äîthey‚Äôre spending tons
of money, but they‚Äôre not yet making it pay off very well. This is where the
most speculative companies hang out. These companies are generally long
shots because it‚Äôs still unclear whether all of that heavy investment will ever
generate an attractive return.
Return on Invested Capital (ROIC)
Return on invested capital is a sophisticated way of analyzing return on
capital that adjusts for some peculiarities of ROA and ROE. Although you
needn‚Äôt worry about calculating it for yourself‚Äîit‚Äôs pretty complicated‚Äîit‚Äôs
worth knowing how to interpret it because it‚Äôs overall a better measure of
profitability than ROA and ROE. (If you are interested in how to calculate
it, see the ROIC box.)
Essentially, ROIC improves on ROA and ROE because it puts debt and
equity financing on an equal footing: It removes the debt-related distortion
that can make highly leveraged companies look very profitable when using
ROE. It also uses a different definition of profits than ROE and ROA, both
of which use net income. ROIC uses operating profits after taxes, but before
interest expenses. Again, the goal is to remove any effects caused by a com-
pany‚Äôs financing decisions‚Äîdoes it use debt or equity?‚Äîso that we can focus
as closely as possible on the profitability of the core business.
What does all this mean to you if you hear someone talking about ROIC?
Simply that you should interpret ROIC just as you would ROA and ROE‚Äî
a higher return on invested capital is preferable to a lower one.
Ôù∞Ôù≤ÔùØÔù¶Ôù©Ôù¥Ôù°Ôù¢Ôù©Ôù¨Ôù©Ôù¥Ôùπ
ÔúπÔúµ
Return on Invested Capital (ROIC)
Warning: The material in this box drifts dangerously close to finance-geek territory. If
hard-core finance puts you to sleep, ignore this box and don‚Äôt worry about the inner
workings of ROIC and WACC.
The true operating performance of a firm is best measured by return on invested capi-
tal (ROIC), which measures the return on all capital invested in the firm regardless of
the source of the capital. The formula for ROIC is deceptively simple:
The numerator of this equation is easy: profits after taxes, but before interest costs.
The denominator is a bit trickier, and although there are many different ways to calcu-
late it, you‚Äôll do just fine if you use this version:
You may also want to subtract goodwill if it‚Äôs a large percentage of assets.
Let‚Äôs run through an example: At the end of fiscal 2002, Wal-Mart‚Äôs total assets were
worth $94.7 billion. Subtract accounts payable, accrued liabilities, and accrued income
taxes, and $67.7 billion remains, which is Wal-Mart‚Äôs invested capital. Looking at the
firm‚Äôs income statement, we see that operating profit was $13.6 billion. If we multiply
this by the firm‚Äôs 36 percent tax rate, we get a rough idea of what taxes would have
been without any interest income or interest expense (remember, interest is tax
deductible for corporations), and we find that NOPAT equals $13.6 ‚àí($13.6 √ó 0.36) =
$8.7 billion.
Next, divide NOPAT by invested capital, or $8.7 billion by $67.7 billion, and ROIC is
12.9 percent‚Äîpretty respectable for a firm as large and mature as Wal-Mart.

Financial Health
Once we‚Äôve figured out how fast (and why) a company has grown and how
profitable it is, we need to look at its financial health. Even the most beauti-
ful home needs a solid foundation, after all.
The bottom line about financial health is that when a company increases
its debt, it increases its fixed costs as a percentage of total costs. In years when
business is good, a company with high fixed costs can be extremely profitable
because once those costs are covered, any additional sales the company makes
fall straight to the bottom line. When business is bad, however, the fixed
costs of debt push earnings even lower.
Look at what debt does to the earnings volatility of the creatively named
Acme (see Figure Ôú∂.Ôú∂). With more debt, Acme‚Äôs earnings fluctuate a lot:
They‚Äôre up more in good times and down more in bad times.
A common measure of leverage is simply the financial leverage ratio that
we used in calculating ROE, equal to assets divided by equity. Think of fi-
nancial leverage like a mortgage‚Äîa homebuyer who puts Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∞,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞down on a
Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞house has a financial leverage ratio of Ôúµ. For every dollar in equity,
the buyer has Ôú§Ôúµin assets.
The same holds true for companies. In Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤, home improvement retailer
Lowe‚Äôs had a financial leverage ratio of Ôú≤.Ôú±, meaning that for every dollar in
equity, the firm had Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôú±Ôú∞in total assets. (It borrowed the other Ôú§Ôú±.Ôú±Ôú∞.) A finan-
cial leverage ratio of Ôú≤.Ôú±is fairly conservative, even for a fast-growing retailer. It‚Äôs
when we see ratios of Ôú¥, Ôúµ, or more that companies start to get really risky.
In addition to financial leverage, make sure to examine a few other key
metrics when assessing a company‚Äôs financial health.
Debt to Equity
This is just what it sounds like‚Äîlong-term debt divided by shareholder‚Äôs
equity. It‚Äôs a little like the financial leverage ratio, except that it‚Äôs more nar-
rowly focused on how much long-term debt the firm has per dollar of equity.
Times Interest Earned
This one requires a little more work to calculate, but it‚Äôs worth it. Look up
pretax earnings, and add back interest expense‚Äîthis gives earnings before
Ôù¶Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨ Ôù®Ôù•Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù¥Ôù®
ÔúπÔú∑
interest and taxes (EBIT). Divide EBIT by interest expense, and you‚Äôll know
how many times (hence the name) the company could have paid the interest
expense on its debt. The more times that the company can pay its interest ex-
pense, the less likely that it will run into difficulty if earnings should fall un-
expectedly.
For home improvement retailer Lowe‚Äôs, for example, we add Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∏Ôú≤million
in interest expenses to Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôú≥Ôú∂billion in pretax earnings to get Ôú§Ôú≤.ÔúµÔú¥million in
EBIT, and we divide Ôú§Ôú≤.ÔúµÔú¥million by the Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∏Ôú≤million in interest expense to
get times interest earned of Ôú±Ôú¥. In other words, Lowe‚Äôs earned enough money
in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥to cover its interest obligation Ôú±Ôú¥times over, which is a pretty safe
margin (see Figure Ôú∂.Ôú∑).

It‚Äôs tough to say precisely how low this metric can go before you should
be concerned‚Äîbut higher is definitely better. You want to see higher times
interest earned for a company with a more volatile business than for a firm in
a more stable industry. Be sure to look at the trend in times interest earned
over time, as well. Calculate the ratio for the past five years, and you‚Äôll be able
to see whether the company is becoming riskier‚Äîtimes interest earned is
falling‚Äîor whether its financial health is improving.
Current and Quick Ratios
The current ratio (current assets divided by current liabilities) simply tells
you how much liquidity a firm has‚Äîin other words, how much cash it
could raise if it absolutely had to pay off its liabilities all at once. A low ratio
means the company may not be able to source enough cash to meet near-
term liabilities, which would force it to seek outside financing or to divert
