ratio of Ôú±.Ôúµor more means the firm should be able to meet operating needs
without much trouble.
Unfortunately, some current assets‚Äîsuch as inventories‚Äîmay be worth
less than their value on the balance sheet. (Imagine trying to sell old PCs or
last year‚Äôs fashions to generate cash‚Äîyou‚Äôd be unlikely to receive anything
close to what you paid for them.) So there‚Äôs an even more conservative test of
a company‚Äôs liquidity, the quick ratio, which is simply current assets less in-
ventories divided by current liabilities. This ratio is especially useful for man-
ufacturing firms and for retailers because both of these types of firms tend to
have a lot of their cash tied up in inventories. In general, a quick ratio higher
than Ôú±.Ôú∞puts a company in fine shape, but always look at other firms in the
same industry to be sure.
The Bear Case
After you‚Äôve assessed growth, profitability, and financial health, your next
task is to look at the bear case for the stock you‚Äôre analyzing. Start by listing
all of the potential negatives, from the most obvious to the least likely. What
could go wrong with your investment thesis? Why might someone prefer to
be a seller of the stock than a buyer? Constructing a convincing bear case is
especially important for those who like to buy high-quality companies that
have hit temporary speed bumps, because what looks like a speed bump may
very well be a roadblock on closer inspection.
Equally important, your bear case will be a great reference point even if
you do decide to buy the stock. You‚Äôll know in advance what signs of trouble
to watch for, which will help you make better decisions when bad news
comes down the pike in the future. Having already investigated the negatives,
you‚Äôll have the confidence to hang on to the stock during a temporary rough
patch as well as the savvy to know when the rough patch might really be a
serious turn for the worse.
I‚Äôll admit to a painful example from my own history that shows the im-
portance of developing a bear case before buying a stock. In the mid-Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s, I
purchased some shares of a small firm called Ballantyne of Omaha, which
was one of the world‚Äôs largest manufacturer of motion picture projectors (for
movie theaters) in the world at the time. (The big ones in movie theaters‚Äî
Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
not the ones your teacher used in elementary school.) The firm was growing
at a decent clip, had few competitors, and management seemed to have their
heads on straight. The stock was selling for what I thought was a pretty rea-
sonable price, as well.
Unfortunately, the movie theater industry‚ÄîBallantyne‚Äôs main customer‚Äî
was in the midst of a huge debt-fueled building boom at the time. Remember
all of those Ôú±Ôú∞- and Ôú≤Ôú∞-screen suburban theaters that got thrown up during the
Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s? That‚Äôs what was pumping up Ballantyne‚Äôs sales, and the expansion
wasn‚Äôt sustainable. Many of the big theater chains had liquidity crunches, and
a few even went belly up. Needless to say, this did not do wonders for movie-
projector demand, and Ballantyne‚Äôs financial results (and stock price) went
into a fast slide.
The lesson? I should have constructed a convincing bear case for Ballan-
tyne before I bought it. If I‚Äôd looked deeper into the financial health of Bal-
lantyne‚Äôs customers, I might have asked myself what would happen if the
multiplex boom slowed down. I didn‚Äôt and, as a result, I made a poor invest-
ment that could have been avoided.
Conclusion
To this point, we‚Äôve investigated the financial performance of companies.
We‚Äôve assessed their competitive strengths by investigating their economic
moats, and we‚Äôve analyzed their financial statements.
In Chapter Ôú∑, I‚Äôll discuss how to assess the quality of management. In
Chapter Ôú∏, I‚Äôll give you some tips on how to spot aggressive accounting, after
which we‚Äôll turn to perhaps the toughest task faced by any serious investor:
Deciding on a fair price for the stock. How can we avoid paying too much for
excellent companies, while not being so cheap that we let wonderful opportu-
nities pass us by? In Chapters Ôúπand Ôú±Ôú∞, I‚Äôll show you how to value stocks.
Investor‚Äôs Checklist: Analyzing a Company‚ÄîThe Basics
3 When you‚Äôre evaluating a company‚Äôs growth rate, don‚Äôt get swept away by
heady historical growth. Make sure you understand where the growth is
coming from and how it can be sustained over time.
3 Be wary of companies that have relied on acquisitions to boost growth.
Most acquisitions fail to produce positive returns for shareholders of the
Ôù©ÔùÆÔù∂Ôù•Ôù≥Ôù¥ÔùØÔù≤‚ÄôÔù≥ Ôù£Ôù®Ôù•Ôù£Ôù´Ôù¨Ôù©Ôù≥Ôù¥
Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú±
acquiring firm, and they make it tough to evaluate a company‚Äôs true
growth rate.
3 If earnings growth outstrips sales growth over a long period, this might be
a sign of manufactured growth. Make sure to dig into the numbers to see
how the company keeps squeezing out more profits from stagnant sales.
3 Return on assets measures the amount of profits that a company is able to
generate per dollar of assets. Companies with high ROAs are better at
translating assets into profits.
3 ROE is a good measure of profitability because it measures how the com-
pany is at earning a return on shareholders‚Äô money. But because companies
can boost their ROEs by taking on more debt, don‚Äôt take it as gospel. For a
nonfinancial company, look for an ROE of at least Ôú±Ôú∞percent, without ex-
cessive leverage.
3 Free cash flow gives financial flexibility because the firm isn‚Äôt relying on
the capital markets to fund its expansion. Firms with negative free cash
flow have to take out loans or sell additional shares to keep things going,
and that can become a risky proposition if the market becomes unsettled
at a critical time for the company.
3 Be wary of companies with too much financial leverage. Because debt is a
fixed cost, it magnifies earnings volatility and leads to more risk.
3 Before you buy a stock, think through all the potential negatives. This
can help you make better decisions if bad news does come down the pike.
Ôú∑
Analyzing a Company‚Äî
Management
EÔù∏Ôù£Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥ Ôù≠Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°ÔùßÔù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥ Ôù£Ôù°ÔùÆmake the difference between a mediocre
business and an outstanding one, and poor management can run even a great
business into the ground. Your goal is to find management teams that think
like shareholders‚Äîexecutives that treat the business as if they owned a piece
of it, rather than as hired hands. Unfortunately, managers like this are more
rare than you might think.
People buy stocks all the time without checking out the folks in the exec-
utive suite, and the excuse I hear most often is that it‚Äôs tough to assess man-
agement without meeting them face-to-face. Hogwash. There are many,
many ways to get a feel for the folks running a company that have nothing to
do with looking the CEO in the eye. No one can run a public company with-
out leaving a trail of pretty strong objective evidence behind. My advice is to
divide the management-assessment process into three parts: compensation,
character, and operations.
Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú¥
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù≠Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°ÔùßÔù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥
Compensation
Compensation is the easiest of the three areas to assess because the bulk of the
information is contained in a single document, usually called the proxy state-
ment. This is the form that companies mail shareholders around the time of the
annual meeting, and it details how much executives are paid and what perks
they get. (You can find this form online at www.sec.gov or www.freeedgar.com.
Look for form DEF14A.)
Here‚Äôs what to look for in a company‚Äôs compensation plan. First and most
important, how much does management pay itself? (This is detailed in the
aptly named ‚Äúsummary compensation table.‚Äù) Generally, I prefer big bonuses
to big base salaries and restricted stock grants to generous option packages.
Bonuses mean that a good portion of the pay is at least theoretically at risk,
and restricted stock means the executive loses money if the share price de-
clines. That‚Äôs just the tip of the iceberg, though.
First, look at the raw level of cash compensation to see if it‚Äôs reasonable.
There‚Äôs not necessarily a strict limit here, though I personally think an Ôú§Ôú∏mil-
lion cash bonus is silly no matter how well the company has done. (For refer-
ence, the average CEO of a large U.S. firm currently earns about ÔúµÔú∞Ôú∞times as
much as the average employee. Twenty years ago, this multiple was just Ôú¥Ôú∞.)
In any case, use your own judgment‚Äîif the amount that executives earn
makes you cringe, it‚Äôs probably too much. Also look at competing firms to
see what their CEOs are paid, so you can see how the boss that you‚Äôre inves-
tigating stacks up. In general, the larger the firm and the better its financial
performance, the more an executive should be paid. But some executives
think they have a license to print money just because they manage a huge
company, no matter how poor a job they‚Äôre doing, which is why you need to
determine whether their pay is tied to the firm‚Äôs operational performance.
(Case in point: After retiring as chairman and co‚ÄìCEO of Verizon, Charles
Lee became a ‚Äúconsultant‚Äù to the firm at the exorbitant rate of Ôú§Ôú≤ÔúµÔú∞,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞per
month. To me, that‚Äôs a cringe-worthy retirement package.)
Pay for Performance
What‚Äôs even more important is whether executives‚Äô pay is truly tied to the
company‚Äôs performance. At many companies, so-called ‚Äúperformance tar-
gets‚Äù are set by a subcommittee of the board of directors, which can often
Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù•ÔùÆÔù≥Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ
Ôú±Ôú∞Ôúµ
rewrite the rules of the game if the CEO appears to be losing. In Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú±, for
example, Coca-Cola‚Äôs board reduced CEO Douglas Daft‚Äôs goal of Ôú±Ôúµpercent
earnings growth over five years to Ôú±Ôú±percent. Moving the goalposts like this
can be justified in one sense‚Äîafter all, if the performance target isn‚Äôt achiev-
able, it‚Äôs hardly going to motivate the CEO‚Äîbut you can also fault the
board for not setting more realistic goals in the first place. In any case, I
think it‚Äôs a negative sign when the targets are changed but the potential re-
ward stays the same, because it indicates that the board is unwilling to stand
up to the CEO and punish him or her by slashing the bonus when perfor-
mance slumps.
At least Coke‚Äôs shareholders knew what the target was, though. According
to the Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú±proxy, Walt Disney‚Äôs compensation gurus decided that bonuses:
may be based on one or more of the following business criteria, or on any
combination thereof, on a consolidated basis: net income (or adjusted net
income), return on equity (or adjusted return on equity), return on assets (or
adjusted return on assets), earnings per share (diluted) (or adjusted earnings
per share [diluted]).
In other words, Disney‚Äôs CEO was going to get paid no matter what. To
add insult to injury, the gang at Disney wrote that ‚Äú[we believe] that the spe-
cific target constitutes confidential business information the disclosure of
which could adversely affect the Company.‚Äù More likely, it would have ad-
versely affected Disney management because the board wouldn‚Äôt have been
able to move the goalposts in the middle of the game. This kind of nondis-
closure on the compensation front is a bad sign when you‚Äôre looking for good
managers.
Another sign of poor compensation procedures is paying managers for
actions that make the company bigger, but not better. In Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú±, for example,
Disney wrote the following in its proxy statement:
As permitted by the plan, special bonuses were paid outside the plan to three
executives . . . Peter Murphy, Thomas Staggs and Louis Meisinger‚Äîfor ex-
traordinary services to the Company unrelated to the plan‚Äôs performance tar-
gets, including, in the case of Messrs. Murphy and Staggs, services related to
the Company‚Äôs acquisition of Fox Family Worldwide, Inc.
Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∂
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù≠Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°ÔùßÔù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥
Rewarding management for consummating an acquisition is an ab-
solutely terrible idea‚Äîpaying big bonuses just for getting a deal done simply
encourages executives to go out and do more dubious deals. A better idea,
though not one I‚Äôve ever seen implemented, would be to wait a couple of
years and pay a bonus only if an acquisition has provided an adequate‚Äîand
predetermined‚Äîreturn on the investment.
The bottom line is this: Executives‚Äô pay should rise and fall based on the
performance of the company. So, after reviewing a company‚Äôs historical fi-
nancials, read the past few years‚Äô proxies to see whether this has truly been the
case or whether some lackeys on the board of directors have cooked up justi-
fications for big bonuses even in bad times. Firms with good corporate gov-
ernance standards won‚Äôt hesitate to pay managers less in bad times and more
in good times, and that‚Äôs the kind of pattern you want to see as a shareholder.
Other Red Flags
Aside from the big-picture question of whether executives‚Äô pay truly is linked
to company performance, keep an eye out for the following issues when
you‚Äôre assessing executive compensation.
Were Executives Given ‚ÄúLoans‚Äù that Were Subsequently Forgiven? This was a
common‚Äîand disgusting‚Äîpractice before the Sarbanes-Oxley Act banned
it in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤. Companies would give loans to senior managers at below-market
rates of interest and then often quietly forgive the loans a few years later. In
my book, a loan that‚Äôs not repaid is a bonus, and companies that tried to
fudge executive pay in this fashion weren‚Äôt treating shareholders with re-
spect. If the executive needed a loan, he should have asked his bank, not 
his employer. Don‚Äôt tolerate this kind of behavior from companies you‚Äôre 
investigating‚Äîeven though it‚Äôs not legal any longer, companies that did
this sort of thing in past should be viewed with skepticism. After all, would
they have stopped unless they were forced to? (Loans of this sort are usually
disclosed in the ‚Äúother compensation‚Äù column of the executive compensa-
tion table in the footnotes.)
Do Executives Get Perks Paid for by the Company that They Should Really Be
Paying for Themselves? It‚Äôs a sure sign of corporate excess when execs get coun-
try club memberships and other frippery paid for by shareholders. After all,
Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù•ÔùÆÔù≥Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ
Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∑
when you‚Äôre paying someone several hundred thousand dollars per year, mak-
ing shareholders foot the bill for their greens fees seems rather silly. More im-
portantly, such behavior at the top sets a poor example for everyone else in the
firm. Managers who enjoy ridiculous perks are acting like latter-day royalty
rather than prudent custodians of shareholders‚Äô money. Conversely, thrifty
CEOs are a plus: Managers who do things such as paying for their own park-
ing and eschewing pricey perks set a good tone for the rest of the organization.
Does Management Hog Most of the Stock Options Granted in a Given Year, or Do
Rank-and-File Employees Share in the Wealth? Generally, firms with more
equitable distribution schemes perform better over the long run. Most firms
break out the percentage of options granted to executives relative to the total
granted in the proxy statement.
Does Management Use Stock Options Excessively? Even if they‚Äôre distributed be-
yond the executive suite, giving out too many options dilutes existing share-
holders‚Äô equity. If a company gives out more than Ôú±or Ôú≤percent of the
outstanding shares each year, they‚Äôre giving away too much of the firm‚Äôs equity
every year. Conversely, it‚Äôs a great sign if the firm issues restricted stock instead
of options. Restricted stock has to be counted as an expense on the income
statement (options don‚Äôt, as of this writing), and restricted stock also forces the
recipient to participate on the downside if the stock falls.
If a Founder or Large Owner Is Still Involved in the Company, Does He or She Also
Get a Big Stock Option Grant Each Year? This makes me queasy. After all, it‚Äôs
hard to argue that, for example, CEO Larry Ellison of Oracle needs additional
options to motivate him when he already owns Ôú≤Ôúµpercent of the firm.
Do Executives Have Some Skin in the Game? That is, do they have substantial
holdings of company stock, or do they tend to sell shares right after they exer-
cise options? As a shareholder, I want management to have meaningful equity
in the company. After all, selling shares in the name of ‚Äúdiversification‚Äù means
not being exposed if the company goes downhill. Generally, I‚Äôm happier own-
ing companies where executives own stock right alongside me because large
unexercised option positions are cold comfort. You can find this information
in the footnotes of the proxy. Companies indicate executives‚Äô percentage
Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∏
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù≠Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°ÔùßÔù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥
ownership including options prominently in a table labeled ‚Äúsecurity owner-
ship of certain beneficial owners,‚Äù but they declare only how many actual
shares are owned in the footnotes. In Peoplesoft‚Äôs Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥proxy, for example,
CEO Craig Conway was listed as owning Ôú≥.Ôú∏million shares, or Ôú±.Ôú≤percent of
the company. But if you looked at the footnote, you‚Äôd have seen that he
owned only Ôú∂Ôú≤Ôú∂,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞shares, Ôú∂Ôú≤Ôúµ,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞of which weren‚Äôt even vested yet.
Character
Compensation by itself is often a good litmus test for character‚Äîanecdotally,
there‚Äôs a pretty strong relationship between management teams that are in it
for the money and management teams that treat shareholders poorly. How-
ever, there are some other important questions you should ask to get a handle
on whether a firm‚Äôs management deserves your trust.
Does Management Use Its Position to Enrich Friends and Relatives?
In a company‚Äôs annual Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´filing, look for a section called ‚Äúrelated-party
transactions.‚Äù If a friend or relative of a company officer has substantial busi-
ness dealings with the firm, you‚Äôll read about it here. Often, this stuff is pretty
innocuous‚Äîan ex-officer or director is paid some nominal amount each year
for consulting services. As long as the firm isn‚Äôt paying out hundreds of thou-
sands of dollars, this kind of thing doesn‚Äôt get my hackles up.
But when the firm pays substantial amounts of money to, for example, an
interior-design firm run by the CEO‚Äôs wife or to a law firm in which the
CFO‚Äôs son-in-law is a partner, I sit up and take notice. The key here is to
make sure that (Ôú±) the firm isn‚Äôt sending a great amount of business in the di-
rection of related parties and (Ôú≤) there‚Äôs not an egregious pattern of abuse.
One or two small related-party transactions aren‚Äôt a big deal, but they do
cause me to raise an eyebrow because they may be signs of a deeper problem.
And if it looks as though virtually all family members of the company officers
have their hands in the till, you have a big character issue.
Is the Board of Directors Stacked with Management‚Äôs Family Members 
or Former Managers?
Look at the biographies of the board, which are also in the proxy statement.
If many of them are closely related to top management‚Äîor are former
Ôù£Ôù®Ôù°Ôù≤Ôù°Ôù£Ôù¥Ôù•Ôù≤
Ôú±Ôú∞Ôúπ
managers themselves, it‚Äôs a good bet the board isn‚Äôt going to be as hard-nosed
when questioning management‚Äôs actions as it could be. And because the
board is shareholders‚Äô last line of defense against a CEO running amok, that‚Äôs
hardly a good thing.
Is Management Candid about Its Mistakes?
Not even the smartest executives get everything right, and it‚Äôs important that
a management team be able to honestly discuss poor decisions and why they
were made. CEOs who bury mistakes might be burying other things as well.
Look for this type of candor in annual reports and in quarterly conference
calls. I especially like to look at the letter to shareholders in the annual re-
port. Is it a candid assessment of the past year‚Äôs successes and failures or a
fluff piece?
How Promotional Is Management?
Although a certain amount of rallying the troops is the job of a CEO, watch
out for company officers who cross the line and begin blindly pumping up
the stock or themselves. This is a red flag because management‚Äôs job is to
worry about running the company‚Äîif executives get that right, the stock
price will take care of itself over time. Executives who complain about how
undervalued their firm‚Äôs shares are or who opine about its true worth are
probably more concerned with the value of their options than with making
solid, long-term business decisions. Self-promotional managers, meanwhile,
are not likely to make decisions that are in the best interests of long-term
shareholders. If you read a number of glowing media articles in which a CEO
paints himself or herself as a latter-day savior, watch out. The cult of the
CEO-as-hero is dangerous.
Can the CEO Retain High-Quality Talent?
This one is subtler, but it can tell you a great deal about the firm you‚Äôre ana-
lyzing. Some firms judge the quality of managers by the turnover rate of their
subordinates because turnover is seen as the ultimate acid test of the working
environment and employees‚Äô views of a company‚Äôs potential. Extend this
view to the executive team: How often do officers turn over? What is the
tenure of key officers? Is executive hiring done from the outside? (All of this
Ôú±Ôú±Ôú∞
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù≠Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°ÔùßÔù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥
can be uncovered by reviewing several years‚Äô worth of proxy statements.)
Long tenure is a great signal of intrinsic motivation and confidence in the
business, whereas a CEO who keeps forcing out immediate subordinates is
likely spending too much time on internal power struggles and not enough
on running the business.
Does Management Make Tough Decisions that Hurt Results but Give 
a More Honest Picture of the Company?
If a management team makes decisions that actually hurt reported results,
you‚Äôre in luck. Management teams that use restricted stock grants instead of
options‚Äîbecause the former has to be expensed, while the latter doesn‚Äôt‚Äîor
who expense rather than capitalize items such as research and development or
software costs are the kinds of folks who are more interested in running the
business than playing numbers games. And those are precisely the kind of
people you want running the companies you own.
Running the Business
In addition to managers who are paid reasonably and are honest, you also
want folks who can run the business well.
Performance
The first stop is simply the financial performance of the company during the
tenure of the current management team. Look for high and increasing ROEs
and ROAs‚Äîbut don‚Äôt forget to check whether increasing ROE was driven by
higher leverage, as opposed to improved profitability or asset efficiency.
Are there any big jumps in revenue? If so, the firm probably did an ac-
quisition, and you should check to see whether management paid a reason-
able price and whether the acquired firm wound up adding to shareholder
value. Most acquisitions do not pay off, so digging into past merger and ac-
quisition (M&A) activity is definitely worth your while.
Finally, look at the share count over a long period of time. If the number
of shares outstanding has increased substantially because of aggressive op-
tions programs or frequent equity issuance, the firm is essentially giving away
part of your stake without asking you. That‚Äôs not a great recipe for long-term
share performance.
Ôù≤ÔùµÔùÆÔùÆÔù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢ÔùµÔù≥Ôù©ÔùÆÔù•Ôù≥Ôù≥
Ôú±Ôú±Ôú±
Follow-Through
When management identifies a problem and promises a solution, does it ac-
tually implement the plan, or does it hope you forget about it? The same goes
for any ‚Äúnew strategic initiatives‚Äù that are announced: Does the firm go ahead
with a new plan once it‚Äôs announced, or does every year see the firm an-
nouncing a grand new strategy that‚Äôs never realized? One way to vet this is to
look at past annual reports and see what new initiatives were discussed three
to seven years ago. It‚Äôs fine if they weren‚Äôt all successful, but where are they
now? Has management kept shareholders up to date on them, or do the ini-
tiatives just disappear from the radar screen? In the latter case, management is
probably spending more time hiring consultants with grandiose plans than
actually figuring out what‚Äôs broken.
Candor
Does the firm provide enough information to properly analyze the business,
or does it clam up about certain issues? Generally, management teams will talk
ad nauseam about parts of the business that are hitting on all cylinders, but
questions about a problem area are sometimes met with evasion or a straight,
‚ÄúWe don‚Äôt disclose that.‚Äù
For example, Merck changed the way it reported its drug sales figures in
Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú±, but it wouldn‚Äôt provide comparable data for past periods‚Äîwhich made
it difficult to get a clear picture of what was going on. Lucent was another 
offender in this department because it grouped together high-growth and low-
growth product lines when it broke out segment data. It‚Äôs entirely proper for
firms not to report certain things, but selective reticence about problem areas
is never a good sign.
Self-Confidence
I generally applaud firms that do something markedly different from their
peers or from conventional opinion. It‚Äôs tough to take a pass on a hot 
consulting fad when all of your golfing buddies are singing its praises or to
buy a beaten-down competitor when your industry is in the tank‚Äîbut
these are both moves that can pay off in spades. Maintaining research and
development spending during an industry downturn is another good 
example of self-confidence that shows management is more concerned with
Ôú±Ôú±Ôú≤
Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°Ôù¨ÔùπÔù∫Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù° Ôù£ÔùØÔù≠Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùÆÔùπ‚ÄîÔù≠Ôù°ÔùÆÔù°ÔùßÔù•Ôù≠Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥
beating competitors over the long haul than beating its quarterly earnings
guidance.
Flexibility
Has management made decisions that will give the firm flexibility in the fu-
ture? These include simple decisions such as not taking on too much debt
and controlling fixed expenses (even in good years), as well as more strategic
decisions such as issuing equity when the stock is high. Attaching call options
to debt, retiring high-rate debt when the opportunity presents itself, and
buying back stock only when the price is low are also good examples of capi-
tal allocation decisions that evidence a solid operational hand on the tiller.
Investor‚Äôs Checklist: Analyzing a Company‚ÄîManagement
3 Evaluating management is a critical component of analyzing a stock. Look
for management teams that act like shareholders, rather than hired hands.
3 Get compensation information from the proxy statement‚ÄîSEC form
DEF-14A. Check out how much management is paid and whether pay
varies with the company‚Äôs performance. If a pay package makes you cringe,
it‚Äôs too high.
3 Avoid companies that give loans to executives, have many related-party
transactions, or give out too many stock options. Look for executives that
have substantial stock ownership positions.
3 Make sure the company discloses enough information for you to properly
analyze the business. ‚ÄúWe don‚Äôt disclose that‚Äù is often code for, ‚ÄúThe news
is bad, so we‚Äôd rather not say.‚Äù
Ôú∏
Avoiding Financial Fakery
AÔù≥ ÔùπÔùØÔùµ‚ÄôÔù∂Ôù• Ôù¨Ôù©Ôù´Ôù•Ôù¨Ôùπfigured out by now, picking great stocks is not a black-
and-white process. Unfortunately, neither is accounting. There are literally
dozens of techniques that are perfectly legal and aboveboard, but which have
the effect of fooling an observer into thinking that a firm has posted true op-
erational improvements when all it has really done is moved some numbers
around. You need to know how to identify what‚Äôs known as aggressive ac-
counting so you can avoid the companies that practice it.
Even worse than aggressive accounting, of course, is outright fraud‚Äîthe
hucksters of the world are naturally attracted to the stock market because it‚Äôs
the perfect arena for profiting from the greed and carelessness of others.
Knowing the signs of potential fraud can save you a lot of financial pain.
It‚Äôs not that hard, either. Although you might need a CPA to understand
exactly how an aggressive or fraudulent firm is exaggerating its results, you
don‚Äôt need to be an expert to recognize the warning signs of accounting chi-
canery. As long as you avoid the companies that bristle with red flags, you
won‚Äôt be caught owning them when the SEC starts investigating.
Ôú±Ôú±Ôú¥
Ôù°Ôù∂ÔùØÔù©Ôù§Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù¶Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨ Ôù¶Ôù°Ôù´Ôù•Ôù≤Ôùπ
Six Red Flags
When you‚Äôre giving a company the once-over, there are six major red flags to
watch out for. Though some of these issues can pop up for innocent reasons,
be sure you thoroughly investigate them before giving the firm a clean bill of
health‚Äîor your money.
Declining Cash Flow
Even if accounting gobbledygook makes your head spin, there is one very
simple thing you can do: Watch cash flow. Over time, increases in a com-
pany‚Äôs cash flow from operations should roughly track increases in net in-
come. If you see cash from operations decline even as net income keeps
marching upward‚Äîor if cash from operations increases much more slowly
than net income‚Äîwatch out. This usually means that the company is gener-
ating sales without necessarily collecting the cash, and that‚Äôs a very good
recipe for a blowup down the road.
My favorite example of this is former high-flyer Lucent. Between Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∑and
Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔúπ, Lucent‚Äôs net income soared from Ôú§Ôú¥Ôú¥Ôúπmillion, to just over Ôú§Ôú±billion, to
almost Ôú§Ôú≥.Ôúµbillion‚Äîan incredible growth rate for such a large company. At the
same time, however, cash flow from operations was plunging precipitously,
from Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôú±billion in Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∑to Ôú§Ôú±.Ôúπbillion in Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∏, to negative Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∑Ôú∂million in
Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔúπ. Why? It‚Äôs a long and sordid story, but it boiled down to three big reasons:
Ôú±. Lucent was extending credit to anyone who could spell photon, which
meant it was booking many sales without receiving the cash. (Think back
to our hot dog stand example in Chapter Ôú¥‚Äîas long as a firm ships a
product and has reasonable certainty that it will eventually collect the
cash, it can post the sale.) Accounts receivable (A/R) ballooned from Ôú≤Ôú∞
percent of sales to Ôú≤Ôú∑percent of sales between Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∑and Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔúπ, a clear sign
that the firm was having trouble collecting the money it was owed.
Ôú≤. Lucent kept building more gear than it could ship, which sent inventories
upward. (Remember, manufacturing something and then storing it in a
warehouse uses cash.) This effect showed up in the ‚Äúincrease in invento-
ries‚Äù line item in the firm‚Äôs statement of cash flows.
Ôú≥. Lucent‚Äôs pension plan was pumping up net income with noncash gains.
If you do nothing else, watch cash flow like a hawk.
Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù∏ Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù§ Ôù¶Ôù¨Ôù°ÔùßÔù≥
Ôú±Ôú±Ôúµ
Serial Chargers
Be wary of firms that take frequent one-time charges and write-downs. This
practice makes the historical financials muddier because every charge has a
long explanation and usually has various components that affect different
accounts‚Äîall of which need to be adjusted if you want to look at compara-
ble year-to-year financial results.
More importantly, frequent charges are an open invitation to accounting
hanky-panky because firms can bury bad decisions in a single restructuring
charge. Usually, the rationale for a charge is pretty vague, which means there‚Äôs
a fair amount of leeway for management.
When a firm takes a big restructuring charge, it‚Äôs essentially improving fu-
ture results by pulling future expenses into the present. In other words, poor
decisions that might need to be paid for in future quarters‚Äîan unsuccessful
product that may need to be terminated or a bloated division that will need to
pay severance payments to redundant employees‚Äîall get rolled into a single
one-time charge in the current quarter, which improves future results.
If you run across a firm that has frequent restructuring charges, don‚Äôt ig-
nore them, despite the firm‚Äôs blandishments about what earnings would be
after excluding the charge. After all, if a firm dug itself into a deep enough
hole that it needs quarter after quarter of charges to make things right, those
charges are a normal cost of improving the business.
Serial Acquirers
As I mentioned earlier in the book, firms that make numerous acquisitions
can be problematic‚Äîtheir financials have been restated and rejiggered so
many times that it‚Äôs tough to know which end is up. Aside from muddying
the waters, acquisitions increase the risk that the firm will report a nasty sur-
prise some time in the future, because acquisitive firms that want to beat
their competitors to the punch often don‚Äôt spend as much time checking out
their targets as they should.
The Chief Financial Officer or Auditors Leave the Company
Quis custodiet ipsos custodes? is Latin for ‚Äúwho watches the watchmen?‚Äù And
when it comes to financial reporting, those watchmen are the chief financial
officer (CFO) and the corporate auditors. If a CFO leaves the company for
reasons that seem at all strange‚Äîor inexplicable‚Äîyou should be on your
Ôú±Ôú±Ôú∂
Ôù°Ôù∂ÔùØÔù©Ôù§Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù¶Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨ Ôù¶Ôù°Ôù´Ôù•Ôù≤Ôùπ
guard. It‚Äôs normal for CFOs to move around just like other executives, but if
you see a CFO leave a company that‚Äôs already under suspicion for accounting
issues, you should think very hard about whether there might be more going
on than meets the eye. The same applies to corporate auditors. If a company
changes auditors frequently or fires its auditors after some potentially damag-
ing accounting issue has come to light, watch out. This one may not be a big
deal by itself, but it‚Äôs definitely something to watch with firms that have al-
ready displayed other warning signs.
The Bills Aren‚Äôt Being Paid
There are few things Wall Street loves more than growth, and companies go
to great lengths to keep their top line increasing as rapidly as possible. One of
the sneakier ways for a company to pump up its growth rate is to loosen cus-
tomers‚Äô credit terms, which induces them to buy more products or services.
(Companies can also ship out more products than their customers ask for‚Äî
known as ‚Äústuffing the channel‚Äù‚Äîbut this is less common.)
The trick here is that even though the company has recorded a sale‚Äî
which increases revenues‚Äîthe customer has not yet paid for the product. If
enough customers don‚Äôt pay‚Äîand those looser credit terms are probably at-
tracting financially shakier customers‚Äîthe pumped-up growth rate will
eventually come back to bite the company in the form of a nasty write-down
or charge against earnings.
You should track how fast A/R are increasing relative to sales‚Äîthe two
should roughly track each other. But if sales increase by, for example, Ôú±Ôúµper-
cent, while A/R increases Ôú≤Ôúµpercent, the company is booking sales faster
than it‚Äôs receiving cash from its customers. (Remember, A/R measures
goods that are sold, but not yet paid for.) As a general rule, it‚Äôs simply not
possible for A/R to increase faster than sales for a long time‚Äîthe company
is paying out more money (as finished goods) than it‚Äôs taking in (through
cash payments).
On the credit front, watch the ‚Äúallowance for doubtful accounts,‚Äù which
is essentially the company‚Äôs estimate of how much money it won‚Äôt be able to
collect from deadbeat customers. If this amount doesn‚Äôt move up in sync with
A/R, the company may be artificially boosting its results by being overly op-
timistic about how many of its new customers will pay their bills.
Ôù≥Ôù•Ôù∂Ôù•ÔùÆ ÔùØÔù¥Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤ Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù¥Ôù¶Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù≥ Ôù¥ÔùØ Ôù∑Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù£Ôù® ÔùØÔùµÔù¥ Ôù¶ÔùØÔù≤
Ôú±Ôú±Ôú∑
Changes in Credit Terms and Accounts Receivable
Finally, check the company‚Äôs Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù±filing for any mentions of changes in credit
terms for customers, as well as for any explanation by management as to why
A/R has jumped. (Look in the management‚Äôs discussion and analysis section
for the latter and in the accounting footnotes for the former.)
Seven Other Pitfalls to Watch Out For
Watching for these six warning signs will help you avoid maybe two-thirds of
potential accounting-related blowups. Unfortunately, though, there are many
other ways that firms can embellish their financial results. When you‚Äôre read-
ing a Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´or annual report, watch for the following pitfalls.
Gains from Investments
It‚Äôs reasonably common for large firms‚Äîespecially in the technology sec-
tor‚Äîto make small investments in other companies. Occasionally, these in-
vestments work out well, the owner sells some of the shares, and records the
capital gain as income. This is no different from the way you or I would re-
port a capital gain as income when we‚Äôre doing our taxes every year, and it‚Äôs
perfectly legal and aboveboard.
An honest company breaks out these sales, however, and reports them
below the ‚Äúoperating income‚Äù line on its income statement. The problem
arises when companies try to boost their operating results‚Äîin other words,
the performance of their core business‚Äîby shoehorning investment income
into other parts of their financial statements.
The most blatant means of using investment income to boost results is to
include it as part of revenue, though this isn‚Äôt very common. Companies can
also record the income ‚Äúabove the line‚Äù so that it is included as part of operat-
ing income and thus boosts their operating margins. This is manifestly a no-no
because accounting rules require any piece of one-time income to be separated
from income that comes from normal operations. Finally, companies can hide
investment gains in their expense accounts by using them to reduce operating
expenses, which makes the firm look more efficient than it really is.
If the firm you‚Äôre analyzing is using investment gains or asset sales to
boost operating income or reduce expenses, you know you‚Äôre dealing with a
company that might be less than forthcoming in other areas as well.
Ôú±Ôú±Ôú∏
Ôù°Ôù∂ÔùØÔù©Ôù§Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù¶Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨ Ôù¶Ôù°Ôù´Ôù•Ôù≤Ôùπ
Pension Pitfalls
Pensions can be a big ball-and-chain for companies with many retirees be-
cause if the assets in the pension plan don‚Äôt increase quickly enough, the firm
has to divert profits to prop up the pension. To fund pension payments to fu-
ture retirees, companies shovel money into pension plans that then are in-
vested in stocks, bonds, real estate, and so forth. If a company winds up with
fewer pension assets than pension liabilities, it has an underfunded plan, and if
the company has more than enough pension assets to meet its projected obli-
gations to retirees, it has an overfunded plan. (More on overfunded plans in
the next section of this chapter.)
To see whether the company has an over- or underfunded pension plan,
go to the footnotes of a Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´filing and look for the note labeled ‚Äúpension and
other postretirement benefits,‚Äù ‚Äúemployee retirement benefits,‚Äù or some vari-
ation. Then look at the line labeled ‚Äúprojected benefit obligation.‚Äù This is the
first key number. It‚Äôs the estimated amount the company will owe to employ-
ees after they retire, and it‚Äôs based on assumptions about how long retirees
will live, the rate that salary levels at the company will grow over time, and
the interest rate that the company uses to discount its future obligations to
their present value. Compare this with the line labeled ‚Äúfair value of plan as-
sets at end of year,‚Äù which is the second key number.
If the benefit obligation exceeds the plan assets, the company has an un-
derfunded pension plan and is likely to have to shovel in more money in the
future, reducing profits. This can be a huge number for a large company
with many retirees‚ÄîGeneral Motors, for example, had pension obligations
of Ôú§Ôú∏Ôú∞billion at year-end Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤versus pension assets of Ôú§Ôú∂Ôú±billion. One way
or another, GM will need to make up the Ôú§Ôú±Ôúπbillion difference. (As recently
as Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞, GM had a Ôú§Ôú±.Ôú∑billion surplus in its pension plan. When Wall Street
goes south, so do pension plans‚Äîbut the obligation keeps growing as em-
ployees earn benefits.)
What did this mean for GM? In Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤, the firm chucked in a whopping
Ôú§Ôú¥.Ôúπbillion to the plan. (You can see this in the pension footnote under ‚Äúem-
ployer contributions.‚Äù) That‚Äôs almost Ôú§Ôúµbillion in cold, hard cash that an un-
wary shareholder might have been expecting to increase the firm‚Äôs value, but
which actually went straight to GM retirees.
So, always check out the pension footnote. There‚Äôs a lot of account-
ing gobbledygook there, but you can also find out whether the firm you‚Äôre
Ôù≥Ôù•Ôù∂Ôù•ÔùÆ ÔùØÔù¥Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤ Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù¥Ôù¶Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù≥ Ôù¥ÔùØ Ôù∑Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù£Ôù® ÔùØÔùµÔù¥ Ôù¶ÔùØÔù≤
Ôú±Ôú±Ôúπ
looking at is going to wind up owing its retirees more money than it actu-
ally has. And if that‚Äôs the case, they‚Äôll get the money before you will as a
shareholder.
Pension Padding
Pensions can be a boon as well as a burden. When stocks and bonds do really
well, as they did in the Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s, pension plans go gangbusters. And if those an-
nual returns exceed the annual pension costs, the excess can be counted as
profit. Flowing gains from an overfunded pension plan through the income
statement is a perfectly legal practice that pumped up earnings at General
Electric for years and boosted earnings at many defense companies by Ôú≥Ôú∞per-
cent to Ôú¥Ôú∞percent during the Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s.
However, this pension-related income is a strange kind of profit. It‚Äôs not
available to pay out to shareholders‚Äîit belongs to the pension plan. And the
only way to unlock that excess is to terminate the plan, which is highly un-
usual. But the excess does benefit shareholders: It should mean the company
will have to contribute less to the pension plan in the future to keep it sol-
vent. As a shareholder, you‚Äôd much rather have an overfunded pension plan
than an underfunded one. But this income is completely dependent on the
stock market, so it‚Äôs not money you want to rely on in the future. You should
subtract it from net income when trying to figure out just how profitable a
company really is.
To find out how much profits decreased because of pension costs or in-
creased because of pension gains, go to the line in the pension footnote labeled
either ‚Äúnet pension/postretirement expense,‚Äù ‚Äúnet pension credit/loss,‚Äù ‚Äúnet pe-
riodic pension cost,‚Äù or some variation. Companies usually break out the con-
tribution of pension costs to profits for the trailing three years; therefore, you
can see not only the absolute level of pension profit or loss, but also the trend.
You won‚Äôt see these numbers in the income statement‚Äîthey get lumped in
with other categories there.
Vanishing Cash Flow
There‚Äôs one important caveat to the general rule that cash flow is a number
to be trusted: You can‚Äôt count on cash flow generated by employees exercis-
ing options. I mentioned this wrinkle in Chapter Ôúµ‚Äîthe amount is labeled
‚Äútax benefits from employee stock plans,‚Äù or ‚Äútax benefit of stock options
Ôú±Ôú≤Ôú∞
Ôù°Ôù∂ÔùØÔù©Ôù§Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù¶Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨ Ôù¶Ôù°Ôù´Ôù•Ôù≤Ôùπ
exercised‚Äù on the statement of cash flows. Here‚Äôs why you don‚Äôt want to
count on this cash flow.
When employees exercise their stock options, the amount of cash taxes
that their employer has to pay declines. Let‚Äôs say your employer gives you Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞
options with an exercise price of Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞. A few years later, the stock is trading for
Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞, and you decide to cash in. You pay taxes on the Ôú§Ôú≤,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞difference (the
Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞market price less the Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞exercise price), and your employer gets to take a
tax deduction of Ôú§Ôú≤,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞against its corporate income because taxable em-
ployee compensation is tax deductible for employers. In other words, your
employer reduced its tax bill by Ôú§Ôú∑Ôú∞Ôú∞‚Äîassuming a Ôú≥Ôúµpercent tax rate‚Äîjust
because you exercised your Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞options.
As long as the firm‚Äôs stock keeps going up and it keeps giving out options,
this process continues. More options are exercised, tax deductions are taken,
and the firm saves cash by lowering its tax bill. But what happens if the stock
takes a tumble? Many people‚Äôs options will be worthless‚Äîtheir exercise prices
will be higher than the market price‚Äîand, consequently, fewer options will be
exercised. Fewer options are now exercised, the company‚Äôs tax deduction gets
smaller, and it has to pay more taxes than before, which means lower cash flow.
Therefore, when the stock price declines, the firm generates less cash than
it did when the stock was flying. Sun Microsystems, for example, reported
about Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôú±billion in cash flow from operations in fiscal Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú±, Ôú§Ôú∏Ôú±Ôú∂million of
which resulted from this lovely tax benefit. In other words, Sun would have
generated Ôú¥Ôú∞percent less cash in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú±if its employees hadn‚Äôt exercised tons of
options. But the next year, when Sun‚Äôs shares plunged to below Ôú§Ôúµ, this portion
of cash flow dried up very fast as fewer options were exercised. In Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤, Sun‚Äôs
option-related tax benefits dropped by almost ÔúπÔú∞percent, to only Ôú§ÔúπÔú∏million.
If you‚Äôre analyzing a company with great cash flow that also has a high-
flying stock, check to see how much of that cash flow growth is coming from
options-related tax benefits. Unless you think you can predict the stock mar-
ket, that‚Äôs not cash you want to count on in years to come.
Overstuffed Warehouses
When inventories rise faster than sales, there‚Äôs likely to be trouble on the
horizon. Sometimes the buildup is just temporary as a company prepares for
a new product launch, but that‚Äôs usually more the exception than the rule.
Ôù≥Ôù•Ôù∂Ôù•ÔùÆ ÔùØÔù¥Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤ Ôù∞Ôù©Ôù¥Ôù¶Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù¨Ôù≥ Ôù¥ÔùØ Ôù∑Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù£Ôù® ÔùØÔùµÔù¥ Ôù¶ÔùØÔù≤
Ôú±Ôú≤Ôú±
When a company produces more than it‚Äôs selling, either demand has dried up
or the company has been overly ambitious in forecasting demand. In any
case, the unsold goods will have to get sold eventually‚Äîprobably at a dis-
count‚Äîor written off, which would result in a big charge to earnings.
This is what happened in late Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞to virtually every company selling
communications-related gear‚Äîinventories started to balloon as demand
from telecom carriers started to slow. At the time, many analysts (including,
I‚Äôm humbled to say, me) thought that the gear-makers had simply overesti-
mated demand temporarily and that the situation would correct itself once
telecom carriers used up the gear they‚Äôd already ordered. But it turned out
that demand was not just pausing‚Äîit was falling off a cliff. Therefore, inven-
tories continued to pile up until sales started to slow as well, and companies
such as Cisco had to write off billions in unsold goods.
Change Is Bad
Another way firms can make themselves look better is by changing any one
of a number of assumptions in their financial statements. As a very general
rule, you should look skeptically on any optional change‚Äîsome accounting
changes are mandated by rule makers‚Äîthat improves reported results. Odds
are good that the motivation for making the change wasn‚Äôt altruistic.
One item that can be altered is a firm‚Äôs depreciation expense. If a firm is
assuming that an asset‚Äîsuch as a building or factory‚Äîwill wear out in Ôú±Ôú∞
years, it subtracts (or depreciates) one-tenth of the building‚Äôs value from its
earnings each year. As you can imagine, the longer the depreciation period,
the smaller the annual hit to earnings. Therefore, if a firm suddenly decides
that an asset has a longer useful life and stretches out the depreciation period,
it‚Äôs essentially pushing costs out into the future and inflating current earnings.
Firms can also change their allowance for doubtful accounts. If the allowance
for doubtful accounts doesn‚Äôt increase at the same rate as accounts receivable,
a firm is essentially saying that its new customers are much more creditworthy
than the previous ones‚Äîwhich is pretty unlikely. If the allowance actually de-
clines as accounts receivable rise, the company is stretching the truth even fur-
ther. In either case, if more customers wind up not paying than the firm
estimated, the firm will have to take a charge to earnings at some point in the
future, which means current results are overstated.
Ôú±Ôú≤Ôú≤
Ôù°Ôù∂ÔùØÔù©Ôù§Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù¶Ôù©ÔùÆÔù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù©Ôù°Ôù¨ Ôù¶Ôù°Ôù´Ôù•Ôù≤Ôùπ
Firms can also change things as basic as how expenses are recorded and
when revenue is recognized (one of those gray accounting areas). You‚Äôll gen-
erally find this kind of information in the ‚Äúsummary of significant account-
ing policies‚Äù section of the Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´‚Äîand if a firm chooses to make changes that
materially reduce expenses or increase revenue, watch out. Unless these
moves were required by the accounting rule makers, the firm is probably try-
ing to cover up deteriorating results.
To Expense or Not to Expense
Companies can also fiddle with their costs by capitalizing them. As I dis-
cussed in Chapter Ôú¥, the basis of accrual accounting is that benefits have to be
matched with expenses on the income statement. Operating costs‚Äîsuch as
office supplies, office rents, and so forth‚Äîare expensed because they produce
a short-term benefit. (You pay the rent only one month or year at a time, and
the benefit you receive expires at the end of that period unless you make an-
other payment.) On the other hand, costs such as a new piece of machinery
are capitalized‚Äîthat is, their value is recorded as an asset that slowly declines
in value over time‚Äîbecause they produce long-term benefits. (A machine
that you buy today will still be cranking out products three years from now,
just at a lower rate because of wear and tear.)
The tricky part is that certain types of costs, such as marketing and
some kinds of software development, can be treated either way. As you can
imagine, a company that wants to inflate profits can easily do so by capital-
izing Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞in marketing costs and spreading the expense out over several
years, rather than expensing the entire Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞in the current period. This is
exactly what AOL did in the mid-Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s‚Äîthe firm argued that the sub-
scribers it was acquiring were likely to produce long-term benefits, so it
should be allowed to capitalize its marketing costs. The SEC disagreed, and
AOL had to reverse course.
Finding this kind of information will take some digging around in the
footnotes of a company‚Äôs Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´form, but it‚Äôs time well spent. Any time you see
expenses being capitalized, ask some hard questions about just how long that
‚Äúasset‚Äù will generate an economic benefit. Looking at the useful life assump-
tion will generally do the trick‚Äîa building might be useful for Ôú¥Ôú∞years, but
a piece of office furniture or a chunk of software won‚Äôt.
Ôù©ÔùÆÔù∂Ôù•Ôù≥Ôù¥ÔùØÔù≤‚ÄôÔù≥ Ôù£Ôù®Ôù•Ôù£Ôù´Ôù¨Ôù©Ôù≥Ôù¥
Ôú±Ôú≤Ôú≥
Investor‚Äôs Checklist: Avoiding Financial Fakery
3 The simplest way to detect aggressive accounting is to compare the trend
of net income with the trend in cash flow from operations. If net income is
growing quickly while cash flow is flat or declining, there‚Äôs a good chance
of trouble lurking.
3 Companies that make numerous acquisitions or take many one-time
charges are more likely to have aggressive accounting. Be wary if a firm‚Äôs
chief financial officer leaves or if the firm changes auditors.
3 Watch the trend of accounts receivable relative to sales. If accounts re-
ceivable is growing much faster than sales, the company may be having
trouble collecting cash from its customers.
3 Pension income and gains from investments can boost reported net in-
come, but don‚Äôt confuse them with solid results from the company‚Äôs core
operations.

Valuation‚ÄîThe Basics
SÔùØ Ôù¶Ôù°Ôù≤, Ôù∑Ôù•‚ÄôÔù∂Ôù•spent all of our time analyzing companies. If the investment
process were as simple as identifying great companies with shareholder-
friendly management teams and wide economic moats, we‚Äôd be finished‚Äî
and investing would be much easier. But even the most wonderful business is
a poor investment if purchased for too high a price. To invest successfully
means you need to buy great companies at attractive prices.
This is an idea that lost credence during the bull market of the Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s and
was thrown completely out the window during the tech bubble. Valuations
mattered less and less because investors were always willing to pay more and
more‚Äîin fact, one popular investment commentary service stated baldly
that business quality was Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞times more important than valuation.
For a while, this strategy‚Äîrelying on a greater fool to take an asset off
your hands at a higher price‚Äîwas lucrative and made many people rich, at
least on paper. The trouble was that no one knew when the music would
stop. When it did, investors who bought overpriced assets hoping to sell
them at even more inflated prices were sorely disappointed.
This is the difference between investors and speculators. Investors pur-
chase an asset for less than their estimate of its value and receive a return
Ôú±Ôú≤Ôú∂
Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
more or less in line with the financial performance of that asset. Specula-
tors, by contrast, purchase an asset not because they believe it‚Äôs actually
worth more, but because they think another investor will pay more for it at
some point. The return that investors receive on assets depends largely on
the accuracy of their analysis, whereas a speculator‚Äôs return depends on the
gullibility of others.
Over time, the stock market‚Äôs returns come from two key components:
investment return and speculative return. As Vanguard founder John Bogle
has pointed out, the investment return is the appreciation of a stock because
of its dividend yield and subsequent earnings growth, whereas the speculative
return comes from the impact of changes in the price-to-earnings (P/E) ratio.
Over the entire twentieth century, Bogle found that the Ôú±Ôú∞.Ôú¥percent average
annual return of U.S. equities broke down into Ôúµpercentage points from div-
idends, Ôú¥.Ôú∏percentage points from earnings growth, and just Ôú∞.Ôú∂percentage
points from P/E changes. In other words, over a long time span, the impact
of investment returns trump the impact of speculative returns.Ôú±
However, the picture is much different when we look at it over shorter
time frames. From Ôú±ÔúπÔú∏Ôú∞through Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞, for example, the market‚Äôs approxi-
mately Ôú±Ôú∑percent annual return was composed of Ôú¥percentage points divi-
dends, Ôú∂percentage points earnings growth‚Äîand a whopping Ôú∑percentage
points per year from the increase in the P/E ratio.
During the horrific bear market of the Ôú±ÔúπÔú∑Ôú∞s, the market‚Äôs investment return
was a solid Ôú±Ôú≥.Ôú¥percent per year, but as the aftermath of the early-Ôú±ÔúπÔú∑Ôú∞s Nifty
Fifty craze dragged the market‚Äôs P/E ratio down from Ôú±Ôú∂times to Ôú∑times, the
market‚Äôs speculative return was a crushing ‚àíÔú∑.Ôúµpercentage points per year. The
market returned an average of only Ôúµ.Ôúπpercent per year during the Ôú±ÔúπÔú∑Ôú∞s, not
because earnings and dividends refused to cooperate, but because the average
investor paid less for the average stock in Ôú±ÔúπÔú∏Ôú∞than in Ôú±ÔúπÔú∑Ôú∞.
Paying Up Rarely Pays Off
What does all this have to do with picking solid stocks? By paying close atten-
tion to the price you pay for a stock, you minimize your speculative risk, which
Ôú±John C. Bogle, ‚ÄúAfter the Fall: What Lies Ahead for Capitalism and the Financial Markets?‚Äù
(address given at the University of Missouri, Columbia, October Ôú≤Ôú≤, Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤).
Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùπÔù©ÔùÆÔùß ÔùµÔù∞ Ôù≤Ôù°Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù¨Ôùπ Ôù∞Ôù°ÔùπÔù≥ ÔùØÔù¶Ôù¶
Ôú±Ôú≤Ôú∑
helps maximize your total return. No one knows what a stock‚Äôs speculative
return will be over the next year‚Äîor even Ôú±Ôú∞years‚Äîbut we can make some
pretty good educated guesses about the investment return. If you find great
companies, value them carefully, and purchase them only at a discount to a
reasonable valuation estimate, you‚Äôll be fairly well insulated against the vicis-
situdes of market emotion.
For example, let‚Äôs take a stock that trades for Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞per share, earns Ôú§Ôú±.ÔúµÔú∞per
share, and pays a Ôú§Ôú±.Ôú∞Ôú∞annual dividend. Assume that earnings and dividends
grow at Ôú∂percent per year, and the initial P/E ratio of Ôú≤Ôú∞doesn‚Äôt change.
After five years, earnings will be Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôú∞Ôú±, so our shares would theoretically
trade for Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôú∞Ôú±√ó Ôú≤Ôú∞= Ôú§Ôú¥Ôú∞.Ôú≤Ôú∞. We‚Äôve also received Ôú§Ôú∂.ÔúµÔúπin dividends, which
means we have Ôú§Ôú¥Ôú∂.Ôú∑Ôúπafter five years. That works out to an annualized return
of Ôúπ.Ôú≥percent, which is our investment return. Because the P/E remained at
Ôú≤Ôú∞, we didn‚Äôt receive any speculative return.
However, if earnings and dividends grow at the same rate, but the P/E
ratio decreases from its starting point of Ôú≤Ôú∞to Ôú±Ôúµ, our returns change dramat-
ically. Although we still have Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôú∞Ôú±in earnings after five years, our shares are
worth only Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôú∞Ôú±√ó Ôú±Ôúµ= Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞.Ôú±Ôúµ. Add in Ôú§Ôú∂.Ôú∑Ôúπin dividends, and annualized re-
turn shrinks to just Ôú¥.Ôú±percent‚Äîour Ôúπ.Ôú≥percent investment return was dam-
aged by a ‚àíÔúµ.Ôú≤percent speculative return. Conversely, a rise in the P/E ratio
from Ôú≤Ôú∞to Ôú≤Ôúµwould yield a fat Ôú±Ôú≥.Ôú∂percent annual return because the specu-
lative return builds on our investment return.
Thus, a change in the market‚Äôs mood can reduce our solid Ôúπ.Ôú≥percent re-
turn to a paltry Ôú¥.Ôú±percent or boost it to a wonderful Ôú±Ôú≥.Ôú∂percent. You can
buy an excellent company that kicks out earnings and dividends like clock-
work, but the negative effects of a sharp decline in the stock‚Äôs valuation can
wipe out even the most robust investment return‚Äîand a P/E decline from
Ôú≤Ôú∞to Ôú±Ôúµis hardly a worst-case scenario. In fact, declines in valuation are usu-
ally coupled with deteriorating corporate fundamentals‚Äîslowing earnings
growth or some similar setback. When this happens, the investor gets socked
with a decline in the speculative return as the valuation shrinks and a decline
in the investment return as earnings growth slows down.
Careful attention to valuation lessens the risk that something truly un-
known‚Äîwhat other investors will pay for our asset in the future‚Äîwill hurt
the return of our portfolio. As investors, we can diligently work to identify
Ôú±Ôú≤Ôú∏
Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
wonderful businesses, but we can‚Äôt predict how other market participants will
value stocks, so we shouldn‚Äôt try.
Being picky about valuation isn‚Äôt fun. It means letting many pitches go by
and watching many stocks run‚Äîstocks that never met your strict valuation
criteria. But when it‚Äôs done properly, disciplined valuation also greatly in-
creases your batting average‚Äîthe number of stocks you pick that do well ver-
sus the number that do poorly‚Äîand it also limits the odds of a real blow-up
damaging your portfolio.
Using Price Multiples Wisely
Our first stop in learning how to value stocks is traditional measures such as the
price-to-sales (P/S) or P/E ratios. Although these measures do have some ad-
vantages‚Äîfor example, they‚Äôre very easy to compute and use‚Äîthey also have
some significant pitfalls that can lead the unwary investor to fuzzy conclusions.
Price-to-Sales
The most basic ratio of all is the P/S ratio, which is the current price of the
stock divided by sales per share. The nice thing about the P/S ratio is that sales
are typically cleaner than reported earnings because companies that use ac-
counting tricks usually seek to boost earnings. (Firms can use accounting tricks
to boost sales, but it‚Äôs much less frequent and it‚Äôs easier to catch.) In addition,
sales are not as volatile as earnings‚Äîone-time charges can depress earnings
temporarily, and the bottom line of economically cyclical companies can vary
significantly from year to year.
This relative smoothness of sales makes the P/S ratio useful for quickly
valuing companies with highly variable earnings, by comparing the current
P/S ratio with historical P/S ratios. Motorola, for example, takes special
charges so often that they‚Äôre no longer very special, and, as a result, it had neg-
ative net income in three of the five years between Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∏and Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤. With such
spotty earnings, a P/E ratio isn‚Äôt going to help us very much. But over that
same time period, sales haven‚Äôt jumped around nearly as much, which makes
the P/S ratio useful. In mid-Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, Motorola‚Äôs P/S ratio was about Ôú±.Ôú∞, close to
a five-year low, which made the stock look relatively inexpensive compared
with where it traded in the past (see Figure Ôúπ.Ôú±).
However, the P/S ratio has one big flaw: Sales may be worth a little or a
lot, depending on a company‚Äôs profitability. If a company is posting billions
ÔùµÔù≥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù∞Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù£Ôù• Ôù≠ÔùµÔù¨Ôù¥Ôù©Ôù∞Ôù¨Ôù•Ôù≥ Ôù∑Ôù©Ôù≥Ôù•Ôù¨Ôùπ
Ôú±Ôú≤Ôúπ
in sales, but it‚Äôs losing money on every transaction, we‚Äôd have a hard time pin-
ning an appropriate P/S ratio on the shares because we have no idea what
level (if any) profits the company will generate. We can see the drawbacks of
using sales as a proxy for value in the marketplace every day.
Retailers, which typically have very low net margins‚Äîthat is, they convert
a relatively small percentage of every dollar of sales into profits‚Äîtend to have
very low P/S ratios. The average grocery store, for example, had a P/S ratio of
Ôú∞.Ôú¥in mid-Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, whereas the average medical device firm had a P/S of around
Ôú¥.Ôú≥. The reason for this huge difference isn‚Äôt that grocery stores happened to be
dirt cheap‚Äîit was that the average grocery store had a net margin of Ôú≤.Ôúµper-
cent, whereas the average medical device firm had a net margin of Ôú±Ôú±percent.
A grocer with a P/S of just Ôú±.Ôú∞would look ridiculously overvalued, but a med-
ical device manufacturer with the same P/S ratio would be an absolute steal.
Therefore, although the P/S ratio might be useful if you‚Äôre looking at a
firm with highly variable earnings‚Äîbecause you can compare today‚Äôs P/S
with a historical P/S ratio‚Äîit‚Äôs not something you want to rely on very much.
In particular, don‚Äôt compare companies in different industries on a price-to-
sales basis, unless the two industries have very similar levels of profitability.
Price-to-Book
Another common valuation measure is price-to-book (P/B), which compares a
stock‚Äôs market value with the book value (also known as shareholder‚Äôs equity or
net worth) on the company‚Äôs most recent balance sheet. The idea here is that
future earnings or cash flows are ephemeral, and all we can really count on is
the net value of a firm‚Äôs tangible assets in the here-and-now. Legendary value
investor Benjamin Graham, one of Warren Buffett‚Äôs mentors, was a big advo-
cate of book value and P/B in valuing stocks.
Although P/B still has some utility today, the world has changed since
Ben Graham‚Äôs day. When the market was dominated by capital-intensive
