Ôú±Ôú≥Ôú∞
Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
firms that owned factories, land, rail track, and inventory‚Äîall of which had
some objective tangible worth‚Äîit made sense to value firms based on their ac-
counting book value. After all, not only would those hard assets have value 
in a liquidation, but also they were the source of many firms‚Äô cash flow. But
now, many companies are creating wealth through intangible assets such as
processes, brand names, and databases, most of which are not directly included
in book value.
For service firms, in particular, P/B has little meaning. If you used P/B to
value eBay, for example, you wouldn‚Äôt be according a shred of worth to the firm‚Äôs
dominant market position, which is the single biggest factor that has made the
firm so successful. Price-to-book can also lead you astray for a manufacturing
firm such as Ôú≥M, which derives much of its value from its brand name and inno-
vative products, not from the size of its factories or the quantity of its inventory.
Another item to be wary of when using P/B to value stocks is goodwill,
which can inflate book value to the point that even the most expensive firm
looks like a value. When one company buys another, the difference between
the target firm‚Äôs tangible book value and the purchase price is called goodwill,
and it‚Äôs supposed to represent the value of all the intangible assets‚Äîsmart em-
ployees, strong customer relationships, efficient internal processes‚Äîthat made
the target firm worth buying.
Unfortunately, goodwill often represents little else but the desperation of
the acquiring firm to buy the target before someone else did, because acquir-
ing firms often overpay for target companies. Be highly skeptical of firms for
which goodwill makes up a sizable portion of their book value. The P/B may
be low, but the bulk of the B could disappear in a hurry if the firm declares
the goodwill as ‚Äúimpaired‚Äù (in other words, the firm admits that it grossly
overpaid for a past acquisition) and writes down its value.
Price-to-book is also tied to return on equity (equal to net income divided
by book value) in the same way that price-to-sales is tied to net margin (equal
to net income divided by sales). Given two companies that are otherwise
equal, the one with a higher ROE will have a higher P/B ratio. For example,
over the past five years, Nokia‚Äôs P/B ratio has averaged about Ôú±Ôú¥, whereas rival
mobile-phone giant Motorola‚Äôs has averaged Ôú≥.Ôú±. One major reason for this
difference is that Nokia‚Äôs average ROE of Ôú≤Ôúπpercent over the same period left
Motorola‚Äôs average ROE of Ôú≥percent in the dust. The reason is clear‚Äîa firm
ÔùµÔù≥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù∞Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù£Ôù• Ôù≠ÔùµÔù¨Ôù¥Ôù©Ôù∞Ôù¨Ôù•Ôù≥ Ôù∑Ôù©Ôù≥Ôù•Ôù¨Ôùπ
Ôú±Ôú≥Ôú±
that can compound book equity at a much higher rate is worth far more be-
cause book value will increase more quickly.
Therefore, when you‚Äôre looking at P/B, make sure you relate it to ROE. A
firm with a low P/B relative to its peers or to the market and a high ROE
might be a potential bargain, but you‚Äôll want to do some digging before mak-
ing that assessment based solely on the P/B.
A caveat: Although P/B isn‚Äôt terribly useful for service firms, it‚Äôs very good
for valuing financial services firms because most financial firms have consider-
able liquid assets on their balance sheets. The nice thing about financial firms
is that many of the assets included in their book value are marked-to-market‚Äî
in other words, they‚Äôre revalued every quarter to reflect shifts in the market-
place, which means that book value is reasonably current. (A factory or piece of
land, by contrast, is recorded on the balance sheet at whatever value the firm
paid for it, which is often very different from the asset‚Äôs current value.)
As long as you make sure that the firm doesn‚Äôt have a large number of bad
loans on its books‚Äîsee Chapter Ôú±Ôú∑for more on banks and bad loans‚ÄîP/B
can be a solid way to screen for undervalued financial firms. Just remember
that financial firms trading below book value (a P/B lower than Ôú±.Ôú∞) are often
experiencing some kind of trouble, so you‚Äôll want to investigate just how
solid that book value is before investing.
Price-to-Earnings: The Benefits
Now we come to the most popular valuation ratio, which can take you pretty
far as long as you‚Äôre aware of its limitations. The nice thing about P/E is that
accounting earnings are a much better proxy for cash flow than sales, and
they‚Äôre more up-to-date than book value. Moreover, earnings per share results
and estimates are easily available from just about any financial data source
imaginable, so it‚Äôs an easy ratio to calculate.
The easiest way to use a P/E ratio is to compare it to a benchmark, such
as another company in the same industry, the entire market, or the same
company at a different point in time. Each of these approaches has some
value, as long as you know the limitations. A company that‚Äôs trading at a
lower P/E than its industry peers could be a good value, but remember that
even firms in the same industry can have very different capital structures, risk
levels, and growth rates, all of which affect the P/E ratio. All else equal, it
Ôú±Ôú≥Ôú≤
Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
makes sense to pay a higher P/E for a firm that‚Äôs growing faster, has less debt,
and has lower capital reinvestment needs.
You can also compare a stock‚Äôs P/E to the average P/E of the entire market.
However, the same limitations of industry comparisons apply to this process
as well. The stock you‚Äôre investigating might be growing faster (or slower)
than the average stock, or it might be riskier (or less risky). In general, com-
paring a company‚Äôs P/E with industry peers or with the market has some
value, but these aren‚Äôt approaches that you should rely on to make a final buy
or sell decision.
However, comparing a stock‚Äôs current P/E with its historical P/E ratios
can be useful, especially for stable firms that haven‚Äôt undergone major shifts
in their business. If you see a solid company that‚Äôs growing at roughly the
same rate with roughly the same business prospects as in the past, but it‚Äôs
trading at a lower P/E than its long-term average, you should start getting in-
terested. It‚Äôs entirely possible that the company‚Äôs risk level or business outlook
has changed, in which case a lower P/E is warranted, but it‚Äôs also possible that
the market is simply pricing the shares at an irrationally low level.
This method generally works better with more stable, established firms
than with young companies with more uncertain business prospects. Firms
that are growing rapidly are changing a great deal from year to year, which
means their current P/Es are less comparable to their historical P/Es.
Price-to-Earnings: The Drawbacks
Relative P/Es have one huge drawback: A P/E of Ôú±Ôú≤, for example, is neither
good nor bad in a vacuum. Using P/E ratios only on a relative basis means
that your analysis can be skewed by the benchmark you‚Äôre using.
So, let‚Äôs try to look at the P/E ratio on an absolute level. What factors
would cause a firm to deserve a higher P/E ratio? Because risk, growth, and
capital needs are all fundamental determinants of a stock‚Äôs P/E ratio, higher
growth firms should have higher P/E ratios, higher risk firms should have lower
P/E ratios, and firms with higher capital needs should have lower P/E ratios.
We can see why this is true intuitively, without breaking out any equa-
tions, by thinking about the basics of valuation‚Äîthe three big factors that af-
fect value are the amount, timing, and riskiness of a firm‚Äôs future cash flows.
Firms that have to shovel in large amounts of capital to generate their
earnings run the risk of needing to tap additional funding, either through
ÔùµÔù≥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù∞Ôù≤Ôù©Ôù£Ôù• Ôù≠ÔùµÔù¨Ôù¥Ôù©Ôù∞Ôù¨Ôù•Ôù≥ Ôù∑Ôù©Ôù≥Ôù•Ôù¨Ôùπ
Ôú±Ôú≥Ôú≥
debt (which increases the risk level of the company) or through additional
equity offerings (which may dilute the value of current shareholders‚Äô stake).
Either way, it‚Äôs rational to pay less for firms with high reinvestment needs
because each dollar of earnings requires more of shareholders‚Äô capital to
produce it.
Meanwhile, a firm that‚Äôs expected to grow quickly will likely have a larger
stream of future cash flows than one that‚Äôs growing slowly, so all else equal,
it‚Äôs rational to pay more for the shares (thus, the higher P/E ratio). On the
flip side, a firm that‚Äôs riskier‚Äîmaybe it has high debt, maybe it‚Äôs highly cycli-
cal, or maybe it‚Äôs still developing its first product‚Äîhas a good chance of hav-
ing lower future cash flows than we originally expected, so it‚Äôs rational to pay
less for the stock.
When you‚Äôre using the P/E ratio, remember that firms with an abun-
dance of free cash flow are likely to have low reinvestment needs, which
means that a reasonable P/E ratio will be somewhat higher than for a run-of-
the-mill company. The same goes for firms with higher growth rates, as long
as that growth isn‚Äôt being generated using too much risk.
A few other things can distort a P/E ratio. Keep these questions in the
back of your mind when looking at P/E ratios, and you‚Äôll be less likely to mis-
use them.
Has the Firm Sold a Business or an Asset Recently? When you‚Äôre looking at a
P/E ratio, you must be sure that the E makes sense. If a firm has recently sold
off a business or perhaps a stake in another firm, it‚Äôs going to have an artifi-
cially inflated E, and thus a lower P/E. Because you don‚Äôt want to value the
firm based on one-time gains such as this, you need to strip out the proceeds
from the sale before calculating the P/E. In late Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞, it looked as though
Oracle had a ridiculously low P/E based on the past four quarters‚Äô earnings‚Äî
until you dug into the numbers and saw that the company had booked a Ôú§Ôú∑
billion gain by selling part of its stake in Oracle Japan. Based on operating
earnings, the stock wasn‚Äôt all that cheap.
Has the Firm Taken a Big Charge Recently? If a firm is restructuring or clos-
ing down plants, earnings could be artificially depressed, which would push
the P/E up. For valuation purposes, it‚Äôs useful to add back the charge to get a
sense of the firm‚Äôs normalized P/E.
Ôú±Ôú≥Ôú¥
Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
Is the Firm Cyclical? Firms that go through boom and bust cycles‚Äîsemicon-
ductor companies and auto manufacturers are good examples‚Äîrequire a bit
more care. Although you‚Äôd typically think of a firm with a very low trailing P/E
as cheap, this is precisely the wrong time to buy a cyclical firm because it
means earnings have been very high in the recent past, which in turn means
they‚Äôre likely to fall off soon. For cyclical stocks, your best bet is to look at the
most recent cyclical peak, make a judgment whether the next peak is likely to
be lower or higher than the last one, and calculate a P/E based on the current
price relative to what you think earnings per share will be at the next peak.
Does the Firm Capitalize or Expense Its Cash Flow-Generating Assets? A firm
that makes money by building factories and making products gets to spread
the expense of those factories over many years by depreciating them bit by
bit. On the other hand, a firm that makes money by inventing new prod-
ucts‚Äîdrug firms are the classic example‚Äîhas to expense all of its spending
on research and development every year. Arguably, it‚Äôs that spending on
R&D that‚Äôs really creating value for shareholders. Therefore, the firm that ex-
penses assets will have lower earnings‚Äîand thus a higher P/E‚Äîin any given
year than a firm that capitalizes assets.
Is the E Real or Imagined? There are two kinds of P/Es‚Äîa trailing P/E, which
uses the past four quarters‚Äô worth of earnings to calculate the ratio, and a for-
ward P/E, which uses analysts‚Äô estimates of next year‚Äôs earnings to calculate the
ratio. Because most companies are increasing earnings from year to year, the
forward P/E is almost always lower than the trailing P/E, sometimes markedly
so for firms that are increasing earnings at a very rapid clip. Unfortunately, es-
timates of future earnings by Wall Street analysts‚Äîthe consensus numbers that
you often read about‚Äîare consistently too optimistic. As a result, buying a
stock because its forward P/E is low means counting on that future E to mate-
rialize in its entirety, and that‚Äôs usually not the case.
Price-to-Earnings Growth (PEG)
The PEG is an offshoot of the P/E ratio that‚Äôs calculated by dividing a 
company‚Äôs P/E by its growth rate. The PEG is extremely popular with 
some investors because it seeks to relate the P/E to a piece of fundamental 
Ôù≥Ôù°Ôùπ ÔùπÔù•Ôù≥ Ôù¥ÔùØ ÔùπÔù©Ôù•Ôù¨Ôù§
Ôú±Ôú≥Ôúµ
information‚Äîa company‚Äôs growth rate. On the surface, this makes sense be-
cause a firm that‚Äôs growing faster will be worth more in the future, all else equal.
The problem is that risk and growth often go hand in glove‚Äîfast-growing
firms tend to be riskier than average. This conflation of risk and growth is why
the PEG is so frequently misused. When you use a PEG ratio, you‚Äôre assuming
that all growth is equal, generated with the same amount of capital and the
same amount of risk.
But firms that are able to generate growth with less capital should be
more valuable, as should firms that take less risk. If you look at a stock that‚Äôs
expected to grow at Ôú±Ôúµpercent trading at Ôú±Ôúµtimes earnings and another one
that‚Äôs expected to grow at Ôú±Ôúµpercent trading at Ôú≤Ôúµtimes earnings, don‚Äôt just
plunk your money down on the one with the lower PEG ratio. Look at the
capital that needs to be invested to generate the expected growth, as well as
the likelihood that those expectations will actually materialize, and you may
very well wind up making a very different decision.
Say Yes to Yield
In addition to multiple-based measures, you can also use yield-based measures
to value stocks. For example, if we invert the P/E and divide a firm‚Äôs earnings
per share by its market price, we get an earnings yield. If a stock sells for Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∞
per share and has Ôú§Ôú±in earnings, it has a P/E of Ôú≤Ôú∞(Ôú≤Ôú∞‚ÅÑÔú±) but an earnings yield of
Ôúµpercent (Ôú±‚ÅÑÔú≤Ôú∞). The nice thing about yields, as opposed to P/Es, is that we can
compare them with alternative investments, such as bonds, to see what kind
of a return we can expect from each investment. (The difference is that earn-
ings generally grow over time, whereas bond payments are fixed.)
In late-Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, for example, I could get a risk-free return from Uncle Sam
of about Ôú¥.Ôúµpercent by buying a Ôú±Ôú∞-year treasury bond. Therefore, I‚Äôd want to
demand a higher rate of return from my stocks because they‚Äôre riskier than
treasuries. A stock with a P/E of Ôú≤Ôú∞would have an earnings yield of Ôúµpercent,
which is a bit better than treasuries, but not much considering the additional
risk I‚Äôm taking. A stock with a P/E of Ôú±Ôú≤, however, would have an earnings
yield of Ôú∏.Ôú≥percent (Ôú±‚ÅÑÔú±Ôú≤), which is much better than those poky treasuries.
Thus, I might be induced to take the additional risk.
The best yield-based valuation measure is a relatively little-known metric
called cash return. In many ways, it‚Äôs actually a more useful tool than the P/E.
Ôú±Ôú≥Ôú∂
Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ‚ÄîÔù¥Ôù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≥
To calculate a cash return, divide free cash flow by enterprise value. (Enterprise
value is simply a stock‚Äôs market capitalization plus its long-term debt minus its
cash.) The goal of the cash return is to measure how efficiently the business is
using its capital‚Äîboth equity and debt‚Äîto generate free cash flow.
Essentially, cash return tells you how much free cash flow a company gen-
erates as a percentage of how much it would cost an investor to buy the whole
shebang, including the debt burden. An investor buying the whole company
would not only need to buy all the shares at market value, but also would be
taking on the burden of any debt (net of cash) the company has.
Let‚Äôs use household-products giant Clorox as an example of how to use
cash return to find reasonably valued investments. In late Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, Clorox had a
market cap of about Ôú§Ôúπ.Ôú∏billion and carried Ôú§Ôú¥ÔúπÔúµmillion in long-term debt
and Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∑Ôú≤million in cash on its balance sheet. Its enterprise value was Ôú§Ôúπ,Ôú∏Ôú∞Ôú∞
+ Ôú§Ôú¥ÔúπÔúµ‚àíÔú§Ôú±Ôú∑Ôú≤, or Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞.Ôú±billion. That‚Äôs half of our ratio.
The other half is free cash flow. Figure Ôúπ.Ôú≤shows Clorox‚Äôs free cash flow
over the past decade; the firm generated about Ôú§Ôú∂Ôú∞Ôú∞million in free cash flow
in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥. So, our cash return on Clorox will be Ôú§Ôú∂Ôú∞Ôú∞million/Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞,Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞million,
or Ôúµ.Ôúπpercent.
With Ôú±Ôú∞-year treasuries yielding just Ôú¥.Ôúµpercent in late Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥and corporate
bonds yielding a higher (but still relatively paltry) Ôú¥.Ôúπpercent, that Ôúµ.Ôúπpercent
cash return from Clorox looks pretty good. Throw in the fact that Clorox‚Äôs
free cash flow is likely to grow over time, whereas those bond payments are
fixed, and Clorox starts to look like a pretty solid value.
Cash return is a great first step to finding cash cows trading at reasonable
prices, but don‚Äôt use cash return for financials or foreign stocks. As I discuss in
Chapter Ôú±Ôú∑, cash flow isn‚Äôt terribly meaningful for banks and other firms that
earn money via their balance sheets. And because definitions of cash flow can

vary widely in other countries, a foreign stock that looks cheap based on its

Investor‚Äôs Checklist: Valuation‚ÄîThe Basics
3 Be picky about valuation. You‚Äôll do well over the long haul by buying
companies that are undervalued relative to their earnings potential.
3 Don‚Äôt rely on any single valuation metric because no individual ratio tells
the whole story. Apply a number of different valuation tools when you‚Äôre
assessing a stock.
3 If the firm is cyclical or has a spotty earnings history, use the price-to-sales
ratio. Companies with P/S ratios lower than their historical average can
sometimes be bargains.
3 The price-to-book ratio is most useful for financial firms and firms with
numerous tangible assets, and it‚Äôs least useful for service-oriented firms.
In addition, firms with higher ROEs will typically be worth a higher
P/B ratio.
3 You can compare a company‚Äôs P/E with the market, with a similar firm, or
with the company‚Äôs historical P/E. In each case, you‚Äôll want the company‚Äôs
P/E to be lower than the benchmark, but make sure you‚Äôre aware of any
differences in risk or growth rates between the company you‚Äôre valuing
and the benchmark. The most reliable benchmark is likely to be the com-
pany‚Äôs own historical valuations, assuming the company hasn‚Äôt changed
very much over time.
3 Use the PEG with caution because fast-growing firms also tend to be
riskier. Don‚Äôt overpay for expected growth that may never materialize.
3 The lowest P/E isn‚Äôt always the best. Firms with high growth, low risk,
and low capital reinvestment needs should have higher P/E ratios. You‚Äôll
likely be better off in the long run paying more for a low-risk firm that‚Äôs
generating large amounts of cash than paying less for a cyclical company
that‚Äôs very capital intensive.
3 Check the earnings yield and cash return, and compare them with the
rates available on bonds. An earnings yield or cash return above current
bond rates can indicate an undervalued stock.
Ôú±Ôú∞
Valuation:‚ÄîIntrinsic Value
TÔù®Ôù• Ôù¢Ôù©Ôùß Ôù§Ôù≤Ôù°Ôù∑Ôù¢Ôù°Ôù£Ôù´of the ratios we discussed in the previous chapter is
that they‚Äôre all based on price‚Äîthey compare what investors are currently pay-
ing for one stock to what they‚Äôre paying for another stock. Ratios do not, how-
ever, tell you anything about value, which is what a stock is actually worth.
Without knowing what a stock is worth, how can you know how much
you should pay for it? At Morningstar, we‚Äôre firm believers that stocks should
be purchased because they‚Äôre trading at some discount to their intrinsic value,
not simply because they‚Äôre priced at a higher or lower point than similar com-
panies. Comparing ratios across companies and across time can help us un-
derstand whether our valuation estimate is close to or far from the mark, but
estimating the intrinsic value of a company gives us a better target.
Having an intrinsic value estimate keeps you focused on the value of the
business, rather than the price of the stock‚Äîand that‚Äôs what you want be-
cause, as an investor, you‚Äôre buying a small piece of a business. Intrinsic valua-
tion also forces you to think about the cash flows that a business is generating
today and the cash it could generate in the future, as well as the returns on
capital that the firm creates. It makes you ask yourself: If I could buy the
whole company, what would I pay?
Ôú±Ôú¥Ôú∞
Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ‚ÄîÔù©ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©ÔùÆÔù≥Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù•
Second, having an intrinsic value gives you a stronger basis for making
investment decisions. Without looking at the true determinants of value,
such as cash flow and return on capital, we have no way of assessing whether
a P/E of, for example, Ôú±Ôúµor Ôú≤Ôú∞is too low, too high, or right on target. After
all, the company with the P/E of Ôú≤Ôú∞might have much lower capital needs
and a less risky business than the company with the P/E of Ôú±Ôúµ, in which case
it might actually be the better investment.
In this chapter, I‚Äôm going to walk you through a simplified version of
how we estimate intrinsic values at Morningstar. Even if you choose not to go
through the entire exercise yourself for every stock you think about buying‚Äî
and you very well may not‚Äîknowing the basic principles will help you make
better investment decisions.
A warning: This can be some difficult stuff, so don‚Äôt be discouraged if
you‚Äôre a bit confused. It does get easier with practice, though‚ÄîI promise!
Cash Flow, Present Value, and Discount Rates
Our first step is answering a basic question‚Äîwhat‚Äôs a stock worth? Luckily,
we can stand on the shoulders of giants such as economists Irving Fisher and
John Burr Williams, who answered this question for us more than Ôú∂Ôú∞years
ago: The value of a stock is equal to the present value of its future cash flows.
No more and no less.
Let‚Äôs take this idea apart carefully, because understanding it is crucial to
properly valuing stocks. Companies create economic value by investing capi-
tal and generating a return. Some of that return pays operating expenses, some
gets reinvested in the business, and the rest is free cash flow.
Remember, we care about free cash flow because that‚Äôs the amount of
money that could be taken out of the business each year without harming its
operations. A firm can use free cash flow to benefit shareholders in a number
of ways. It can pay a dividend, which essentially converts a portion of each
investor‚Äôs interest in the firm to cash. It can buy back stock, which reduces
the number of shares outstanding and thus increases the percentage owner-
ship of each shareholder. Or, the firm can retain the free cash flow and rein-
vest it in the business.
These free cash flows are what give the firm its investment value. A pres-
ent value calculation simply adjusts those future cash flows to reflect the fact
Ôù£Ôù°Ôù≥Ôù® Ôù¶Ôù¨ÔùØÔù∑, Ôù∞Ôù≤Ôù•Ôù≥Ôù•ÔùÆÔù¥ Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù•, Ôù°ÔùÆÔù§ Ôù§Ôù©Ôù≥Ôù£ÔùØÔùµÔùÆÔù¥ Ôù≤Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù•Ôù≥
Ôú±Ôú¥Ôú±
that money we plan to receive in the future is worth less than money we re-
ceive today.
Why are future cash flows worth less than current ones? First, money that
we receive today can be invested to generate some kind of return, whereas we
can‚Äôt invest future cash flows until we receive them. This is the time value of
money. Second, there‚Äôs a chance we may never receive those future cash flows,
and we need to be compensated for that risk, called the ‚Äúrisk premium.‚Äù
The time value of money is essentially the opportunity cost of receiving
money in the future versus receiving it today, and it‚Äôs often represented by the
interest rates being paid on government bonds. It‚Äôs pretty certain that the
U.S. government will be around to pay us our interest in a few years.
Of course, not many cash flows are as certain as those from the feds, so we
need to tack on an additional premium to compensate us for the risk that we
may never receive the money that we‚Äôve been promised. Add the government
bond rate to the risk premium, and you have what‚Äôs known as a discount rate.
The oddly named discount rate makes more sense when you think about
it in these terms: What rate of return would you need to make you indifferent
between receiving some quantity of money right now versus at some time in
the future? The old saying that a bird in the hand is worth two in the bush ex-
presses the same concept in a different way: We know we have the bird in the
hand, so we need two birds in the bush to make us indifferent between the
two options. In a similar vein, money to be received in the future is worth less
to us because we don‚Äôt know if we‚Äôll get it, and if we get it today, we could in-
vest it to earn a return.
For example, if you were about to take a one-week vacation, but your
boss asked you at the last minute to postpone it for a year, you might ask your
boss for an extra day off to compensate for the delay. In other words, six days
off next year is equivalent to five days off right now, because you‚Äôd rather take
the trip now and because something else could crop up over the next year
that would force you to delay the trip again.
So, your mental discount rate for vacation time is Ôú≤Ôú∞percent. That‚Äôs the
rate at which five days becomes six days in a year (6 days ‚àíÔúµdays/Ôúµdays).
And if you thought your boss might get fired‚Äîwhich would make it less cer-
tain that her promise of an extra day would be honored‚Äîyou might ask for
two extra days off next year. In that case, your mental discount rate would be
Ôú±Ôú¥Ôú≤
Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ‚ÄîÔù©ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©ÔùÆÔù≥Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù•
Ôú¥Ôú∞percent, which is the rate at which five days becomes seven days in a year
(Ôú∑days ‚àíÔúµdays/Ôúµdays).
Now you can start to see why stocks with stable, predictable earnings often
have such high valuations‚Äîinvestors discount their future cash flows at a
lower rate, because they believe that there‚Äôs a lower risk attached to the likeli-
hood that those future cash flows will actually show up. Conversely, a business
with an extremely uncertain future should logically have a lower valuation be-
cause there‚Äôs a substantial risk that the potential future cash flows will never
materialize.
You can see why a rational investor should be willing to pay more for a
company that‚Äôs profitable now relative to one that promises profitability only
at some point in the future. Not only does the latter carry higher risk (and
thus a higher discount rate), but the promised cash flows won‚Äôt arrive until
some years in the future, diminishing their value still further.
This can be a tough concept, so let‚Äôs look at an example with some real
numbers. Figure Ôú±Ôú∞.Ôú±illustrates the difference that changing discount rates and

is about Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú≤,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞.

percent some years and shrinks in some years, so investors perceive it as a
riskier investment and use a higher discount rate when they‚Äôre valuing its
shares. As a result, the present value of the discounted cash flows is lower.
The difference in the present value of the cash flows is even more
acute when you look at RiskCorp, which is worth almost Ôú§Ôú∏,Ôú≥Ôú∞Ôú∞less than
StableCorp. Not only are the bulk of RiskCorp‚Äôs cash flows far off in the fu-
ture, but also, we‚Äôre less certain that they‚Äôll come to pass, so we assign an
even higher discount rate.
Believe it or not, you now know the basic principles behind a discounted
cash flow model. Value is determined by the amount, timing, and riskiness of a
firm‚Äôs future cash flows, and these are the three items you should always be
thinking about when deciding how much to pay for a stock. That‚Äôs all it re-
ally boils down to.
Calculating Present Value
Now that you know the theory behind an intrinsic value calculation, here‚Äôs
how you can do it in practice. To find the present value of a Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞future cash
flow, divide that future cash flow by Ôú±.Ôú∞plus the discount rate. Using a Ôú±Ôú∞per-
cent discount rate, for example, a cash flow of Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞one year in the future is
worth Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞/Ôú±.Ôú±Ôú∞, or Ôú§ÔúπÔú∞.ÔúπÔú±. A Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞cash flow two years in the future is worth
Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞/(Ôú±.Ôú±Ôú∞)2, or Ôú§Ôú∏Ôú≤.Ôú∂Ôú¥. In other words, Ôú§Ôú∏Ôú≤.Ôú∂Ôú¥invested at Ôú±Ôú∞percent becomes
Ôú§ÔúπÔú∞.ÔúπÔú±in a year and Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∞Ôú∞in two years. Discount rates are really just interest
rates that go backwards through time instead of forwards.
Generalizing the previous formula, if we represent the discount rate as R,
the present value of a future cash flow in year N equals CFn/(Ôú±+ R)n. Sup-

Fun with Discount Rates
Now that we have the formula down, we need to figure out what factors de-
termine discount rates. How do we know whether to use Ôú∑percent or Ôú±Ôú∞per-
cent? From our previous example of the delayed vacation, we know that
opportunity cost‚Äîor time value‚Äîis one factor and that the other big deter-
minant of our discount rate is risk.
Unfortunately, there is no precise way to calculate the exact discount rate
that you should use in a discounted cash flow (DCF) model, and academics
have filled entire journal issues with nothing but discussions about the right
way to estimate discount rates‚Äîbut trust me, it‚Äôs not a discussion in which
you want to be involved.
Here‚Äôs what you need to know for practical purposes: As interest rates in-
crease, so will discount rates. As a firm‚Äôs risk level increases, so will its dis-
count rate. Let‚Äôs put these two together. For interest rates, you can use a
long-term average of Treasury rates as a reasonable proxy. (Remember, we use

Ôú±Ôú¥Ôúµ
the interest rate on treasuries to represent opportunity costs because we‚Äôre
pretty certain that the government will pay us our promised interest.) In mid-
Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, the average yield of the Ôú±Ôú∞-year bond over the past decade was about Ôúµ.Ôúµ
percent, so we‚Äôll use that. Because this isn‚Äôt an exact science, you may want to
use Ôúµpercent or Ôú∂percent.
Now for risk, which is an even less exact factor to measure. According to
standard finance theorists, risk is the same thing as volatility, and the risk level
of a company can be estimated simply by looking at how much its shares have
bounced around relative to how much the market has bounced around. Thus,
if a firm‚Äôs shares suddenly drop from Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞to Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∞, this theory holds that the
stock has just become much riskier.
We‚Äôre not big fans of this definition of risk at Morningstar because we
think stocks that are cheap are generally less risky than stocks with high price
tags. (This assumes that nothing dramatic has changed with the underlying
business, which isn‚Äôt always the case when a stock drops.) We think it‚Äôs better
to assess risk by looking at the company, rather than by looking at the stock,
and that a firm‚Äôs riskiness is determined by the likelihood that it will or won‚Äôt
generate the cash flows that we‚Äôre forecasting.
Why? Because what the share price has done in the past may have little
bearing on what cash flows the company generates in the future. We think it
makes more sense to define risk as the chance of permanent capital impair-
ment‚Äîin other words, the likelihood that our investment will be worth
much less when we go to sell it than it is today. Here are some factors we
think should be taken into account when estimating discount rates.
Size
Smaller firms are generally riskier than larger firms because they‚Äôre more vul-
nerable to adverse events. They also usually have less diversified product lines
and customer bases.
Financial Leverage
Firms with more debt are generally riskier than firms with less debt because
they have a higher proportion of fixed expenses (debt payments) relative to
other expenses. Earnings will be better in good times, but worse in bad times,
with an increased risk of financial distress. (Financial distress means that the
Ôú±Ôú¥Ôú∂
Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ‚ÄîÔù©ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©ÔùÆÔù≥Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù•
firm is having trouble paying its debts.) Look at a firm‚Äôs debt-to-equity ratio,
interest coverage, and a few other factors to determine the degree of a com-
pany‚Äôs risk from financial leverage.
Cyclicality
Is the firm in a cyclical industry (such as appliances or semiconductors) or a
stable industry (such as breakfast cereal or beer)? Because the cash flows of
cyclical firms are much tougher to forecast than stable firms, their level of risk
increases.
Management/Corporate Governance
This factor boils down to a simple question: How much do you trust the
folks running the shop? Although it‚Äôs rarely black or white, firms with pro-
motional managers, managers who draw egregious salaries, or who exhibit
any of the other red flags covered in Chapter Ôú∑are definitely riskier than
companies with managers who don‚Äôt display these traits.
Economic Moat
Does the firm have a wide moat, a narrow moat, or no economic moat? The
stronger a firm‚Äôs competitive advantage‚Äîthat is, the wider its moat‚Äîthe
more likely it will be able to keep competitors at bay and generate a reliable
stream of cash flows.
Complexity
The essence of risk is uncertainty, and it‚Äôs tough to value what you can‚Äôt see.
Firms with extremely complex businesses or financial structures are riskier than
simple, easy-to-understand firms because there‚Äôs a greater chance that some-
thing unpleasant is hiding in a footnote that you missed. Even if you think
management is as honest as the day is long and that the firm does a great job
running its operations, it‚Äôs wise to incorporate a complexity discount into your
mental assessment of risk‚Äîunless you really want to memorize all Ôú∑Ôú∞Ôú∞pages of
the last Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´filing.
How should you incorporate all of these risk factors into a discount rate?
As I said earlier, there‚Äôs no right answer. At Morningstar, we use Ôú±Ôú∞.Ôúµpercent as
the discount rate for an average company based on the factors in the preceding
Ôù£Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù£ÔùµÔù¨Ôù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù∞Ôù•Ôù≤Ôù∞Ôù•Ôù¥ÔùµÔù©Ôù¥Ôùπ Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù•Ôù≥
Ôú±Ôú¥Ôú∑
list, and we create a distribution of discount rates based on whether firms are
riskier or less risky than the average. As of mid-Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, firms such as Johnson &
Johnson, Colgate, and Wal-Mart fall at the bottom of the range, at around Ôúπ
percent, whereas riskier firms‚Äîsuch as Micron Technology, JetBlue Airways,
and E*Trade‚Äîtop out at Ôú±Ôú≥percent to Ôú±Ôúµpercent.
The key is to pick a discount rate you‚Äôre comfortable with. Don‚Äôt worry
about being exact‚Äîjust think about whether the company you‚Äôre evaluating is
riskier or less risky than the average firm, along with how much riskier or less
risky it is, and you‚Äôll be fine. In addition, remember that assigning discount
rates is an inexact science‚Äîthere is no ‚Äúright‚Äù discount rate for a company.
Calculating Perpetuity Values
We‚Äôre almost there‚Äîwe have cash flow estimates, and we have a discount
rate. We need only one more element, called a perpetuity value, and we‚Äôre
ready to put the whole thing together. We need a perpetuity because it‚Äôs not
feasible to project a company‚Äôs future cash flows out to infinity, year-by-year,
and because companies have theoretically infinite lives.
The most common way to calculate a perpetuity is to take the last cash
flow (CF) that you estimate, increase it by the rate at which you expect cash
flows to grow over the very long term (g), and divide the result by the dis-
count rate (R) minus the expected long-term growth rate. In formula terms,
this equals:
The result of this calculation then must be discounted back to the pres-
ent, using the method I discussed previously. Let‚Äôs run through an example to
show you what I mean.
For example, suppose we‚Äôre using a Ôú±Ôú∞-year DCF model for a company
with an Ôú±Ôú±percent discount rate. We estimate that the company‚Äôs cash flow in
year Ôú±Ôú∞will be Ôú§Ôú±billion and its cash flows will grow at a steady Ôú≥percent an-
nual rate after that. (Three percent is generally a good number to use as
your long-run growth rate because it‚Äôs roughly the average rate of U.S. gross

Plugging in the numbers:

Simple 10-Year Valuation Model
Step 1 
Forecast free cash flow (FCF) for the next 10 years.
Step 2 
Discount these FCFs to reflect the present value:
3 Discounted FCF = FCF for that year : (1 + R)
N
(where R = discount rate and N = year being discounted) 
Step 3
Calculate the perpetuity value and discount it to the present:
3 Perpetuity Value = FCF 10 " (1 + g) : (R - g)
3 Discounted Perpetuity Value = Perpetuity Value : (1 + R) 10
Step 4 
Calculate total equity value by adding the discounted perpetuity value 
to the sum of the 10 discounted cash flows (calculated in step 2):
3 Total Equity Value = Discounted Perpetuity Value + 10 Discounted Cash Flows
Step 5 
Calculate per share value by dividing total equity value by shares outstanding:
3 Per Share Value = Total Equity Value : Shares Outstanding

This is a very simple DCF model‚Äîthe one we use at Morningstar has
about a dozen Excel tabs, adjusts for complicated items such as pensions and
operating leases, and explicitly models competitive advantage periods, among
many other things. But a model doesn‚Äôt need to be super complex to get you
most of the way there and help you clarify your thinking. For example, our
valuation of Clorox‚Äîwhich pegs the value of the stock at about Ôú±Ôúµpercent
higher than the stock price in late Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥‚Äîhas the company generating free
cash flow of about Ôú§Ôú∏Ôú∞Ôú∞million per year Ôú±Ôú∞years from now.
How realistic is this? As of late Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, only Ôú±Ôú≤Ôúµcompanies in Morningstar‚Äôs
database of more than Ôú∂,ÔúµÔú∞Ôú∞firms were able to accomplish such a feat, so it‚Äôs
certainly a high hurdle. However, given Clorox‚Äôs portfolio of strong brands
and solid track record of product innovation it‚Äôs not unreasonable. Moreover,
our Ôúµpercent estimated annual growth rate in free cash flow is a good deal
lower than the firm‚Äôs past growth rate, which makes the model somewhat
conservative. After all, Clorox has so many strong brands‚Äîincluding the
eponymous bleach, Pine-Sol, and Formula Ôú¥Ôú∞Ôúπ‚Äîthat it may be able to hold
the line when it negotiates with big retailers like Wal-Mart.
The important thing is that we forced ourselves to think through these
kinds of issues, which we wouldn‚Äôt have if we‚Äôd just looked at Clorox‚Äôs stock
chart or if we‚Äôd just said, ‚ÄúSixteen times earnings seems reasonable.‚Äù By
thinking about the business, we arrived at a better valuation in which we
have more confidence.
Ôú±ÔúµÔú≤
Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ‚ÄîÔù©ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©ÔùÆÔù≥Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù•
Margin of Safety
We‚Äôve analyzed a company, we‚Äôve valued it‚Äînow we need to know when to
buy it. If you really want to succeed as an investor, you should seek to buy
companies at a discount to your estimate of their intrinsic value. Any valua-
tion and any analysis is subject to error, and we can minimize the effect of
these errors by buying stocks only at a significant discount to our estimated
intrinsic value. This discount is called the margin of safety, a term first popu-
larized by investing great Benjamin Graham.
Here‚Äôs how it works. Let‚Äôs say we think Clorox is worth Ôú§ÔúµÔú¥, and the stock
is trading at Ôú§Ôú¥Ôúµ. If we buy the stock and we‚Äôre exactly right about our analysis,
the return we receive should be the difference between Ôú§Ôú¥Ôúµand Ôú§ÔúµÔú¥(Ôú≤Ôú∞per-
cent) plus the discount rate of about Ôúπpercent. (The discount rate for a stock
is sometimes called the required return for precisely this reason.) That would be
Ôú≤Ôúπpercent, which is a pretty darn good return, all things considered.
But what if we‚Äôre wrong? What if Clorox grows even more slowly than
we‚Äôd anticipated‚Äîmaybe a competitor takes market share‚Äîor the firm‚Äôs
pricing power erodes faster than we‚Äôd thought? If that‚Äôs the case, then Clorox‚Äôs
fair value might actually be Ôú§Ôú¥Ôú∞, which means we would have overpaid for the
stock by buying it at Ôú§Ôú¥Ôúµ.
Having a margin of safety is like an insurance policy that helps prevent us
from overpaying‚Äîit mitigates the damage caused by overoptimistic esti-
mates. If, for example, we‚Äôd required a margin of safety of Ôú≤Ôú∞percent before
buying Clorox, we wouldn‚Äôt have purchased the stock until it fell to Ôú§Ôú¥Ôú≥. In
that case, even if our initial analysis had been wrong and the fair value had re-
ally been Ôú§Ôú¥Ôú∞, the damage to our portfolio wouldn‚Äôt have been as severe.
Because all stocks aren‚Äôt created equal, not all margins of safety should be
the same. It‚Äôs much easier to forecast the cash flows of, for example, Anheuser-
Busch over the next five years than the cash flows of Boeing. One company
has tons of pricing power, dominant market share, and relatively stable de-
mand, whereas the other has relatively little pricing power, equal market
share, and highly cyclical demand. Because I‚Äôm less confident about my fore-
casts for Boeing, I‚Äôll want a larger margin of safety before I buy the shares.
There‚Äôs simply a greater chance that something might go wrong and that my
forecasts will be too optimistic.
Ôù£ÔùØÔùÆÔù£Ôù¨ÔùµÔù≥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ
Ôú±ÔúµÔú≥
Paying more for better businesses makes sense, within reason. The price
you pay for a stock should be closely tied to the quality of the company, and
great businesses are worth buying at smaller discounts to fair value. Why? Be-
cause high-quality businesses‚Äîthose that have wide economic moats‚Äîare
more likely to increase in value over time, and it‚Äôs better to pay a fair price for
a great business than a great price for a fair business.
How large should your margin of safety be? At Morningstar, it ranges all
the way from just Ôú≤Ôú∞percent for very stable firms with wide economic moats
to Ôú∂Ôú∞percent for high-risk stocks with no competitive advantages. On aver-
age, we require a Ôú≥Ôú∞percent to Ôú¥Ôú∞percent margin of safety for most firms.
Having a margin of safety is critical to being a disciplined investor be-
cause it acknowledges that as humans, we‚Äôre flawed. Simply investing in the
stock market requires some degree of optimism about the future, which is
one of the biggest reasons that buyers of stocks are too optimistic far more
often than they‚Äôre too pessimistic. Once we know this, we can correct for it
by requiring a margin of safety for all of our share purchases.
Conclusion
Every approach to equity investing has its own warts. Being disciplined about
valuation may mean that you‚Äôll miss out on some great opportunities because
some companies wind up performing better for longer periods of time than al-
most anyone would have anticipated. Companies such as Microsoft and Star-
bucks, for example, looked very pricey back in their heyday, and it‚Äôs unlikely
that many investors who were very strict about valuation would have bought
them early in their corporate lives. Why? Because both firms managed to fend
off their competitors for very long periods of time‚Äîmuch longer than a con-
servative estimate would have given them credit for.
Being disciplined about valuation would have meant missing these op-
portunities, but it also would have kept you out of many investments that
were priced like the next Microsoft, but which wound up disappointing in-
vestors in a big way. Think about how many software firms have fallen by the
wayside over the past decade, for example, or when bagel stocks were priced
as if they were going to all become the next McDonald‚Äôs back in the early
Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s. Although we acknowledge that some high-potential companies are
Ôú±ÔúµÔú¥
Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù°Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùØÔùÆ‚ÄîÔù©ÔùÆÔù¥Ôù≤Ôù©ÔùÆÔù≥Ôù©Ôù£ Ôù∂Ôù°Ôù¨ÔùµÔù•
worth a leap of faith and a high valuation, on balance, we think it‚Äôs better to
miss a solid investment because you‚Äôre too cautious in your initial valuation
than it is to buy stocks at prices that turn out to be too high.
After all, the real cost of losing money is much worse than the opportunity
cost of missing out on gains. That‚Äôs why the price you pay is just as important
as the company you buy.
Investor‚Äôs Checklist: Valuation‚ÄîIntrinsic Value
3 Estimating an intrinsic value keeps you focused on the value of the busi-
ness, rather than on the price of the stock.
3 Stocks are worth the present value of their future cash flows, and that value
is determined by the amount, timing, and riskiness of the cash flows.
3 A discount rate is equal to the time value of money plus a risk premium.
3 The risk premium is tied to factors like the size, financial health, cyclical-
ity, and competitive position of the firm you‚Äôre evaluating.
3 To calculate an intrinsic value, follow these five steps: Estimate cash flows
for the next year, forecast a growth rate, estimate a discount rate, estimate
a long-run growth rate, and add the discounted cash flows to the perpe-
tuity value.
Ôú±Ôú±
Putting It All Together
NÔùØÔù∑, Ôù©Ôù¥‚ÄôÔù≥ Ôù¥Ôù©Ôù≠Ôù•for the fun part. You now have an investment philosophy,
you know what economic moats are, and you know how to read financial
statements and evaluate companies. Let‚Äôs put all this knowledge to work by
analyzing two real-world companies: chipmaker Advanced Micro Devices
(AMD) and Biomet, a medical device firm.
(Note: All of the financial data in this chapter is available free on
Morningstar.com in the same format that you see it here.)
Advanced Micro Devices
At first, AMD might look like an attractive investment. It‚Äôs one of only two
companies that manufacture microprocessors‚Äîthe brains in a PC or server‚Äî
and computers are pretty ubiquitous devices. AMD also makes flash memory
chips that are used in a variety of devices that should have solid long-term de-
mand, such as mobile phones and network routers. In the microprocessor
market, AMD has caught up to archrival Intel on the technological front over
the past several years, and for a brief spell in the late Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s, the firm was sell-
ing chips that were arguably faster and better than those being sold by Intel.
Ôú±ÔúµÔú∂
Ôù∞ÔùµÔù¥Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù©Ôù¥ Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù¨ Ôù¥ÔùØÔùßÔù•Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤
In addition, AMD has been working on a powerful next-generation chip that
may be better than anything Intel has to offer.
This is the AMD story that you might know if you followed the news a
bit and casually flipped through the company‚Äôs Web site and its recent annual
reports. However, it‚Äôs not enough information to make a sound investment
decision, so let‚Äôs approach the firm systematically to see just how solid an in-
vestment AMD really is.
Economic Moat
First, look for evidence of an economic moat. As we discussed in Chapter Ôú≥,
we can do this by examining how profitable AMD has been in the past by an-
alyzing free cash flow, margins, return on equity, and return on assets (see
Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú±).
It looks like AMD has had a pretty spotty history of generating free cash
flow. After a few good years in the early Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s, free cash flow turned negative as
the firm heavily increased capital spending, and AMD didn‚Äôt throw off any free
cash again until the technology boom in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞. That‚Äôs not a good sign that the
firm has much of an economic moat. However, some rapidly growing firms
spend years plowing all of their money back into capital spending, which
means their free cash flow is negative because they‚Äôre still building their eco-
nomic moat. (Starbucks was a great example of this during the Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s.) Even
these kinds of firms should have solid profits, however, so let‚Äôs turn to AMD‚Äôs
margins and returns on capital (see Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú≤).
Looking at operating and net margins, we see that although AMD scores
better on this front than it does on free cash flow, it still lost money for Ôú∂of
the past Ôú±Ôú∞years. The trend doesn‚Äôt indicate that these were simply cyclical

Ôù°Ôù§Ôù∂Ôù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù•Ôù§ Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≤ÔùØ Ôù§Ôù•Ôù∂Ôù©Ôù£Ôù•Ôù≥
Ôú±ÔúµÔú∑
losses‚Äîthe firm lost money for four straight years between Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∂and Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔúπ,
even though the U.S. economy was going gangbusters.
ROE and ROA tell a similar story. After some salad days in the early
Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s‚ÄîROEs in the high teens are pretty good‚ÄîAMD‚Äôs performance went
into a big slump until the tech boom in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞. We can also see that asset
turnover has declined, which means that AMD has become less efficient and
financial leverage has gradually edged upward, indicating that AMD has prob-
ably taken on more debt.
AMD has had one great year recently‚ÄîROE, free cash flow, and margins
were all excellent in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞‚Äîand it‚Äôs worth our time to find out why so we can
determine whether it was an aberration or a sign of better times to come.
After reading through some recent annual reports, we find that AMD made
money in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞the old-fashioned way: It rolled out a chip that was faster and
cheaper than anything Intel had at the moment. This made the company a
ton of money until Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú±, when the demand for PCs slowed down and Intel
rolled out a competing chip.
So, AMD‚Äôs one big recent success was due to a product with superior
technology, which we know from Chapter Ôú≥is the least sustainable source of
an economic moat. It shouldn‚Äôt come as a surprise to us that Intel quickly
used its superior size and cash hoard to accelerate the rollout of a competing
chip and regain the market share it briefly lost to AMD.
Overall, there‚Äôs not much evidence of a sustainable economic moat in
AMD‚Äôs historical financials. It‚Äôs not difficult to understand why: Intel domi-
nates the microprocessor market, and AMD‚Äôs market share has typically been
around Ôú±Ôúµpercent for the past several years. Intel‚Äôs size lets it spend four times

as much on research and development as AMD does, which is a big advan-
tage when you consider how fast semiconductor technology changes. Intel‚Äôs
size has also allowed it to attain much greater economies of scale, because it
can spread the fixed costs of its manufacturing plants across a much larger
volume of chips.
AMD‚Äôs lack of an economic moat means we‚Äôd want a big margin of safety
if we wanted to buy the stock, but even no-moat companies can sometimes
be decent investments if the fundamentals aren‚Äôt too shaky and the stock is
cheap enough. Let‚Äôs complete our analysis by looking at the five areas I dis-
cussed in Chapter Ôú∂‚Äîgrowth, profitability, financial health, risks, and man-
agement‚Äîand then by doing a rough valuation of the stock.
Growth
Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú≥shows that revenue growth has been somewhat volatile for AMD
over the past decade. Overall, it‚Äôs not been terrible, but a Ôú∂percent average
annual growth rate over a decade when PC demand was pretty hot is nothing
to crow about.
It‚Äôs tough to say much that‚Äôs meaningful about growth in profits because
AMD had so many money-losing years during the Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s. About the best we
can do is note that AMD made a lot more money in its most recent profitable
year (Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞) than it did in the previous profitable year (Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔúµ), but it also lost
much more money in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤than it ever had before‚Äîa spotty track record, at
best (see Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú¥).
Profitability
We looked at free cash flow and returns on capital when we were assessing
whether AMD had an economic moat, and the verdict was pretty negative.
Let‚Äôs dig a bit deeper to see what else we can understand about how AMD

we saw on the statement of cash flows‚ÄîAMD was spending boatloads of
money on expanding its manufacturing capabilities.

much more efficient in terms of overhead spending, and having to increase
research spending in a big way. Couple these trends with highly variable
sales‚Äîsee the revenue growth line in Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú≥‚Äîand you have a pretty dis-
mal profitability picture.

Financial Health
Unfortunately, things don‚Äôt look much better when we examine AMD‚Äôs fi-
nancial health.
At the end of Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤, the firm had Ôú§Ôú±.Ôúπbillion in debt and Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôúµbillion in
shareholders‚Äô equity. The resulting debt-to-equity ratio of Ôú∞.Ôú∑isn‚Äôt terribly
high relative to the market, but it‚Äôs not great for a firm that has as much trou-
ble generating profits as AMD does. AMD‚Äôs current ratio‚Äîwhich, remem-
ber, is simply current assets divided by current liabilities‚Äîwas about Ôú±.Ôúµ.
Again, not terrible, but not comforting either, given AMD‚Äôs second-tier in-
dustry position and spotty history of profitability.
Finally, we learn by digging into AMD‚Äôs Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´filing that the firm has
some big loans outstanding with banks in Germany, relating to a large man-
ufacturing facility that the firm is building there. Adding these loans to other
contractual obligations, we see that AMD will need to pay out more than
Ôú§ÔúπÔúµÔú∞million to various parties between Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú¥and Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∂. That‚Äôs a huge
amount of money for a company that has generated only Ôú§Ôú≤ÔúµÔú∞million in net
operating income since Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú≥and has bled about Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôú∑billion in free cash flow
over the same period.
(You can calculate the cumulative operating income and free cash flow
amounts yourself from the previous figures.) I‚Äôd say AMD doesn‚Äôt score very
well on the financial health front.
The Bear Case
Although I normally recommend developing a strong bear case for any com-
pany you analyze, we‚Äôve uncovered many more negatives than positives about
AMD so far, so let‚Äôs move on.
Management
Let‚Äôs look at some of AMD‚Äôs proxy statements to see what we can find out
about management. For one thing, they‚Äôre paid pretty well: Outgoing CEO
Jerry Sanders pulled in about Ôú§Ôú±million in salary every year between Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∑and
Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤, an additional Ôú§Ôú¥Ôú∞Ôú∞,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞in ‚Äúdeferred retirement compensation‚Äù for each
of those years, and bonuses ranging from zero to Ôú§Ôúµ.Ôú±million. He also re-
ceived a good-sized amount of additional ‚Äúin-kind‚Äù compensation from use
Ôù°Ôù§Ôù∂Ôù°ÔùÆÔù£Ôù•Ôù§ Ôù≠Ôù©Ôù£Ôù≤ÔùØ Ôù§Ôù•Ôù∂Ôù©Ôù£Ôù•Ôù≥
Ôú±Ôú∂Ôú±
of company vehicles and the company plane. In Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤, for example, he re-
ceived about Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∏Ôú¥,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞worth of company-provided vehicle services. That‚Äôs
either an expensive car or a well-paid chauffeur.
Other top executives also did well in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤, with the top five individuals re-
ceiving salaries of between Ôú§Ôú¥ÔúµÔú∞,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞and Ôú§ÔúπÔú∞Ôú∞,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞, and three of the five re-
ceiving hefty bonuses despite AMD‚Äôs Ôú§Ôú±.Ôú≤billion loss that year. Incoming CEO
Hector Ruiz didn‚Äôt get a cash bonus, but don‚Äôt feel too sorry for him‚Äîhe re-
ceived Ôú±.Ôú≤million options (about Ôú±Ôú∞percent of the total granted that year) in-
stead. Depending on the assumptions used, Ruiz‚Äôs options will be worth
between Ôú§Ôú±Ôú≤and Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞million over the next decade.
Speaking of options, it looks to me as though AMD has been giving away
the store to its employees and diluting shareholder value as a result. From
Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞through Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, AMD issued about Ôú¥Ôú∂million stock options to its em-
ployees and officers, increasing the number of shares outstanding by Ôú±Ôúµper-
cent. (You can find the total number of options granted each year in that
year‚Äôs proxy statement.) Therefore, anyone who bought AMD shares in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞
and held them through Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥saw their stake in the company shrink by Ôú±Ôúµper-
cent after three years, simply because the firm gave away so many options to
its employees. This kind of egregious options granting tells me that manage-
ment cares little for outside shareholders.
Overall, I‚Äôd say that AMD‚Äôs management is overpaid, and they are not the
type of folks to whom I‚Äôd entrust my money.
Valuation
Valuation is tough, because AMD has lost money for the past two years. On
a price-to-sales basis, AMD was trading at about Ôú±.Ôúµtimes sales in September,
Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, which was much cheaper than the seven times sales valuation of the
chip industry average and in line with AMD‚Äôs own Ôú±.Ôú∂times sales average val-
uation over the past five years‚Äînot too bad.
We can‚Äôt use a P/E because AMD was forecasted to lose about Ôú§Ôú∞.Ôú≥Ôú∞per
share in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú¥. In any event, it would be tough to have much confidence in
earnings estimates for a company that‚Äôs been as volatile as AMD‚Äîfor example,
Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú¥earnings estimates for AMD ranged from a low of ‚àíÔú§Ôú∞.Ôú∏Ôúµper share to a
high of Ôú§Ôú∞.Ôú≤Ôú∞per share in September, Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥. In other words, no one has the
Ôú±Ôú∂Ôú≤
Ôù∞ÔùµÔù¥Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù©Ôù¥ Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù¨ Ôù¥ÔùØÔùßÔù•Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤
faintest clue how much money AMD will be making (if any) in the near fu-
ture, which tells us that we should demand a big margin of safety from our val-
uation we arrive at.
Because AMD is highly cyclical, we could also attempt to predict its earn-
ings at the peak of the next cycle and value the company based on the result-
ing P/E ratio. Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú∂shows that AMD‚Äôs last peak earnings per share was
almost Ôú§Ôú≥in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞. However, we‚Äôd be wise to take this figure with a grain of
salt because the earnings occurred during a technology boom that‚Äôs unlikely
to repeat and during a rare time when AMD was able to catch Intel napping.
Still, even if we assume that AMD will earn only Ôú§Ôú±per share during its next
cyclical peak, the stock doesn‚Äôt look too expensive‚Äîit was trading at Ôú§Ôú±Ôú≤in
September Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≥, which is Ôú±Ôú≤times peak earnings.
The problem is that we have no idea when (or if) AMD‚Äôs next cyclical
peak will arrive. After all, the company has a weak competitive position and
a troublesome balance sheet. Thus, I don‚Äôt think valuing it on peak earnings
makes much sense.
Finally, we can attempt to forecast cash flows and use a DCF approach.
This is also tough because AMD has generated positive annual free cash flow
only three times in the past decade. Let‚Äôs give it a whirl, though.
If we conservatively estimate that AMD returns to positive free cash flow
in Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôúµ, generates Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞million that year, and increases free cash flow at Ôúµper-
cent annually for the next Ôú±Ôú∞years, it will generate about Ôú§Ôú≤.Ôú≤billion in free
cash over the next decade. Discounted back to the present at Ôú±Ôú¥percent, that‚Äôs
about Ôú§Ôú±.Ôú∞billion. (I used Ôú±Ôú¥percent because AMD had a poor track record of
profitability, had considerable balance sheet risk, and operated from a weak
competitive position in a cyclical industry.) Add in our perpetuity value of
Ôú§Ôú∑ÔúµÔú∞million, and AMD is worth about Ôú§Ôú±.Ôú∏billion, or about Ôú§Ôúµper share (see
Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú∑).

With the stock trading at Ôú§Ôú±Ôú≤as of this writing, I‚Äôd take a pass. With such
an uncertain future, this is a stock that I would buy only at a big discount to a
conservative estimate of intrinsic value‚Äîand perhaps not even then, given
how many strikes the firm has against it. The intrinsic value could be much
lower if AMD fails to generate free cash flow in the near future or runs into liq-
uidity troubles, or it could be much higher if AMD gains some kind of com-
petitive edge against Intel. In particular, the next-generation ‚ÄúHammer‚Äù family
of chips AMD has rolled out could give the firm a boost. Given the firm‚Äôs poor
track record of holding its own against Intel, though, that‚Äôs not a bet I‚Äôd want
to make. The stock could move higher if the market gets enthusiastic about
AMD‚Äôs prospects‚Äîit tends to be pretty volatile‚Äîbut it seems like a poor long-
term investment.
Biomet
Medical-device firm Biomet, which will hopefully be more promising than
AMD, makes artificial joints‚Äîmainly hips and knees‚Äîas well as a variety of
other products used in orthopedic surgery, such as pins and screws for setting
broken bones. The firm has been around for about Ôú≤Ôúµyears, is still run by one
of the founders, and competes with a relatively small group of firms. (The
top five firms in Biomet‚Äôs main market control about Ôú∏Ôúµpercent of sales.)
The orthopedic device industry is pretty attractive. Developed-country
populations are aging, and better health care means that people are staying
active longer‚Äîwhich increases the demand for artificial joints. Moreover, ar-
tificial joints became a mass-market product only during the Ôú±ÔúπÔú∏Ôú∞s and Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔú∞s.
Because joints typically have a Ôú±Ôú∞- to Ôú±Ôú≤-year life span, the number of revision
procedures, which replace or repair a worn-out artificial joint, is just now
starting to climb. Add these revisions to the demand for first-time proce-
dures, and the market is growing at Ôú∑percent to Ôú±Ôú∞percent. And because an-
nual price hikes of Ôú≥percent to Ôú¥percent are pretty usual in the orthopedic
industry, sales growth of Ôú±Ôú∞percent to Ôú±Ôú¥percent looks pretty doable for the
average orthopedic device company. Companies that gradually gain market
share or compete in the high-end segment of the market might be able to
grow a touch faster, as well.
Margins are fat because the industry has high barriers to entry and high
switching costs. Surgeons prefer products with long clinical track records,
Ôù¢Ôù©ÔùØÔù≠Ôù•Ôù¥
Ôú±Ôú∂Ôúµ
which means it would be tough for an upstart to quickly gain market share.
In addition, this is a heavily research-dependent business, so having decades
of product development experience and expertise helps keep incumbents
ahead of potential new entrants. Finally, each company‚Äôs products are slightly
different, which means that orthopedic surgeons develop product preferences
and are reluctant to take time off from surgery to get retrained on a competi-
tor‚Äôs product unless it offers a large potential benefit. Because artificial joint
innovations tend to be incremental, rather than revolutionary, this isn‚Äôt terri-
bly likely, so market share in the industry stays fairly stable.
You can learn all of this information by reading Biomet‚Äôs Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´and those of
a few of its competitors, as well as surfing a few industry-oriented Web sites.
Let‚Äôs see if the financials bear out what seems to be an attractive company.
Economic Moat
First, we need to look for evidence that the firm has an economic moat‚Äîwhat
sounds good in words may not always show up in the numbers. As with AMD
earlier in the chapter, we can do this by examining the trends in Biomet‚Äôs free
cash flow, margins, return on equity, and return on assets (see Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú∏).
These are the kinds of consistently excellent financial results that serious
stock investors dream about‚Äîconsistent and constantly increasing free cash
flow, a free cash-to-sales ratio well above Ôúµpercent (and usually above Ôú±Ôú∞per-
cent), and very consistent operating and net margins. There‚Äôs little to quibble
with here‚Äîany company that can convert more than Ôú±Ôú∞percent of sales into
free cash flow for a decade is doing something right.
Trends in ROA and ROE also look stellar, with high margins, decent
asset turns, and modest financial leverage (see Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôúπ). Asset efficiency

mental note to pay extra-special attention to Biomet‚Äôs balance sheet to make
sure it has the resources to pay any more judgments that might come along.

Overall, it looks as though Biomet has a pretty sizeable economic moat.
Returns on capital and free cash flow are consistently high, and there‚Äôs not
much year-to-year variation. Although we‚Äôll want to make sure we dig further
into Biomet‚Äôs competition‚Äîto make sure that these solid financial results are
likely to persist in the future‚ÄîBiomet has the financial hallmarks of a solid
investment so far.
Growth
Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú±Ôú±indicates that revenue growth has been a little volatile, ranging
from Ôú∏percent to Ôú≤Ôú∞percent, but a sales growth rate in the mid teens is about
average. That‚Äôs pretty good, and it‚Äôs right in line with the industry growth
rates we examined earlier in this section. That means Biomet is likely holding
its own in terms of market share.
Now, let‚Äôs see whether earnings growth is similarly solid (see Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú±Ôú≤).
Aside from Ôú±ÔúπÔúπÔúπ, which was affected by that Ôú§ÔúµÔúµmillion legal charge that we
mentioned earlier, earnings growth has been excellent. It‚Äôs tough to increase
the bottom line at Ôú±Ôúµpercent annually for a decade, but that‚Äôs what Biomet
has done. In addition, it looks as though long-term earnings growth of Ôú±Ôúµ.Ôú∑
percent is right in line with long-term sales growth of Ôú±Ôúµ.Ôú∏percent, which
means Biomet probably hasn‚Äôt had to play any accounting games to generate
such solid results. The firm increased earnings the way a great company
should‚Äîby selling more products, year in and year out.

Profitability
We‚Äôve already given very high marks to Biomet for its solid free cash flow and
high returns on capital, but as we did with AMD, let‚Äôs dig deeper to find out
what was driving the firm‚Äôs profitability. Again, we use a common size in-
come statement as our tool (see Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú±Ôú≥).
These are some very solid, steady results. Gross margins of Ôú∑Ôú∞percent are
high, and it looks as if they‚Äôve been very gradually increasing over time, which
means the firm has been able to maintain pricing power of the goods it sells
and control costs of the materials used to make its products. Overhead
(SG&A) costs have been very steady as a percentage of sales, which means Bio-
met isn‚Äôt becoming more efficient as it grows. This is all right, though, because
a big chunk of those SG&A costs are Biomet‚Äôs payments to its salesforce‚Äî
when salespeople sell more, they get paid more, which is as it should be.
Finally, it looks as though research and development has been declining as
a percentage of sales. Although this could be positive, because it means higher
overall margins as Biomet spreads R&D costs over a larger sales base, we need
to be sure that Biomet hasn‚Äôt pared back too far on research. Innovation is the
lifeblood of a company such as Biomet, so we want to do some digging to
make sure the company has plenty of new products in the pipeline. We can
probably determine this by going through the company‚Äôs recent press releases
and annual report, and we can also compare how much Biomet is spending
on research with its competitors to make sure it‚Äôs in line.
Overall, though, there are few quibbles here. Biomet passes the prof-
itability test with flying colors, and the results are as clean as can be, with very

few one-time charges. Most important, the charges that the company has

Financial Health
Biomet has no long-term debt, so that‚Äôs one thing we won‚Äôt have to worry
about. The current ratio is around Ôú¥, which is high for a company with no
debt to worry about, and the firm has consistently kept around Ôú±Ôúµpercent of
total assets in cash.
One thing you want to watch with super-profitable companies such as
Biomet is that they don‚Äôt let too much cash pile up on the balance sheet. It‚Äôs
fine for a company to build a temporary war chest if it anticipates big invest-
ments sometime in the next few years, but cash that sits around on the bal-
ance sheet for a long time isn‚Äôt being used efficiently. If you see cash as a
percentage of total assets rising year after year for a firm that‚Äôs already in fine
financial health, try to find out why management isn‚Äôt buying back stock,
paying a dividend, or reinvesting it in the business. Any of the three are
preferable to letting the cash account balloon.
The Bear Case
Creating a convincing bear case is especially crucial when evaluating com-
panies like Biomet that score well on just about every front. Things that look
too good to be true usually are, and every company has some warts that need
to be taken into account.
First, we need to think about litigation risks. We mentioned earlier a re-
cent Ôú§ÔúµÔúµmillion charge related to an adverse legal settlement, and it‚Äôs likely
that the firm will be embroiled in other disputes from time to time. There-
fore, we want to make sure that the firm has adequate insurance and that it‚Äôs
good about disclosing the status of whatever litigation it‚Äôs party to. (The
‚Äúcommitments and contingencies‚Äù section of the Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´will help us do this.)
Legal risks are largely unpredictable, so we should be prepared for some po-
tential headline risk from Biomet.
Another area of concern is that Biomet‚Äôs foreign operations aren‚Äôt in
nearly as good a shape as its U.S. operations. If we look at the ‚Äúsegment data‚Äù
section of Biomet‚Äôs Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤Ôú±Ôú∞-Ôù´, we find that the firm does about Ôú≤Ôúµpercent of
its sales outside the United States, but only Ôú±Ôú≤percent of the firm‚Äôs operating
Ôú±Ôú∑Ôú∞
Ôù∞ÔùµÔù¥Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù©Ôù¥ Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù¨ Ôù¥ÔùØÔùßÔù•Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤
income stems from its foreign units. Because the foreign segment is a big por-
tion of the overall company and it‚Äôs only half as profitable‚Äîand it‚Äôs not
growing as quickly‚Äîwe want to find out what Biomet‚Äôs plans are for its for-
eign operations. Why are they so much less profitable and growing so much
slower? How does Biomet plan to fix them? If Biomet can‚Äôt get its foreign op-
erations on track, does it plan to exit them altogether, or is there some larger
strategic reason that the firm needs an international presence? We want to try
to get answers to these questions so we know just how big a risk the firm‚Äôs for-
eign operations are likely to be.
Biomet‚Äôs size is also an issue, because digging into the orthopedic devices
industry reveals that Biomet is not as large as some of its competitors. Biomet
has about Ôú∑percent of the worldwide orthopedic market, whereas its com-
petitor Stryker has Ôú±Ôúµpercent and the DePuy division of Johnson & Johnson
has about Ôú±Ôú¥percent. Firms with more market share can sometimes gain
greater economies of scale‚Äîwhich would allow them to price their products
lower‚Äîand may also be able to outmuscle smaller competitors by offering
customers a more diverse product lineup. Because Biomet has been growing
nicely for some time and there‚Äôs no evidence yet of margin pressure, it looks as
though the firm is holding its own against its larger peers. However, we want
to keep in mind the risk that a larger competitor will try to squeeze Biomet.
Finally, we want to look into some big-picture industry risks. Biomet‚Äôs
large gross margins suggest that the firm has been able to charge premium
prices for its products, and highly profitable health care industries tend to at-
tract political pressure from time to time‚Äîjust ask the large drug firms. A
change in the rules for Medicare reimbursements or some other arcane regula-
tory issue could have a big impact on the whole orthopedic device industry.
Like legal risks, regulatory changes are tough to predict, so we want to men-
tally prepare ourselves for a potentially unpleasant shock.
Management
As with AMD, Biomet‚Äôs proxy statement‚Äîwhich details executives‚Äô compen-
sation‚Äîis a good first stop in assessing management. They‚Äôre paid pretty rea-
sonably: President and CEO Dane Miller pulled in less than Ôú§ÔúµÔú∞Ôú∞,Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú∞in
salary and bonus, and other members of the executive team are in roughly the
same range. One vice president even had a higher base salary than Miller in
Ôù¢Ôù©ÔùØÔù≠Ôù•Ôù¥
Ôú±Ôú∑Ôú±
Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú≤. Salaries and bonuses have been moving up steadily over the past few
years, but so have Biomet‚Äôs profits. Finally, there‚Äôs none of the ‚Äúother compen-
sation‚Äù frippery that executives at so many firms receive‚Äîno mention of
loans, company cars, life insurance policies, special deferred retire-like-a-king
accounts, and so on. Management is paid a healthy cash salary, some receive
modest stock option grants, and that‚Äôs that.
On the stock option front, neither Miller nor Chairman of the Board
Niles Noblitt has received any stock options over the past three years, and
some further digging into the proxy reveals that Miller has never received a
stock option from the firm. Because Miller owns Ôú≥percent of the outstanding
shares and Noblitt owns Ôú±.Ôú∏percent, this is entirely appropriate‚Äîboth already
have substantial ownership stakes that motivate them to act in shareholders‚Äô
interests, and they see no need for greater ownership stakes. (Contrast this be-
havior with executives such as Larry Ellison of Oracle or Steve Jobs of Apple,
each of whom has received substantial options grants in recent years despite
already-large ownership stakes.)
For those executives who did receive option grants, the amounts look rea-
sonable‚Äîover the past few years, no executive has received more than Ôú±per-
cent of the total options granted in a given year, which means options are likely
being widely distributed throughout the firm. Moreover, the total amount
being granted each year is rarely above Ôú±percent of the total number of shares
outstanding, and the firm‚Äôs total number of shares has barely budged over the
past decade. That tells me Biomet is using options responsibly to motivate em-
ployees and executives without diluting shareholder value, which is exactly the
kind of behavior you want to see in the management team of a firm.
Valuation
For a firm as high quality as Biomet, valuation is likely to be the Achilles‚Äô heel
in the investment analysis process. Companies this good are rarely cheap, so
we need to tread carefully as we decide what a reasonable value would be for
Biomet‚Äôs shares. Even though the firm‚Äôs high growth rate and strong prof-
itability mean that we should be willing to pay up for the shares, we can‚Äôt pay
too much, or we‚Äôll be unlikely to receive a decent return on our investment.
Starting with the basic valuation multiples, we find that Biomet was trad-
ing at about Ôú≤Ôú∏times the past year‚Äôs earnings as of this writing. That‚Äôs
Ôú±Ôú∑Ôú≤
Ôù∞ÔùµÔù¥Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù©Ôù¥ Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù¨ Ôù¥ÔùØÔùßÔù•Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤
pricey‚Äîit‚Äôs way above the market‚Äôs price-to-earnings ratio of about Ôú≤Ôú∞‚Äîbut
it‚Äôs substantially lower than the firm‚Äôs average P/E of Ôú≥Ôú∏over the past five
years. Price-to-cash flow (P/CF) tells a similar story: The current P/CF ratio
of Ôú≥Ôúµwas higher than the market P/CF of Ôú±Ôú¥, but lower than Biomet‚Äôs histor-
ical average of Ôú¥Ôúµ.
Finally, Biomet‚Äôs earnings yield and cash return of Ôú≥.Ôú∞percent and Ôú≤.Ôú±per-
cent, respectively, didn‚Äôt exactly scream ‚Äúundervalued.‚Äù We could get better
returns in risk-free treasuries, and given Biomet‚Äôs higher risk than a T-bond,
we should demand a higher cash return and earnings yield from its shares.
However, bond payments are fixed, whereas Biomet‚Äôs earnings and cash
flow should grow substantially over time. Moreover, Biomet‚Äôs business has
been consistent over the past several years, which means we can forecast the
firm‚Äôs future with more confidence than we could, for example, AMD‚Äôs future.
Sounds to me like Biomet is a perfect candidate for a discounted cash flow
analysis.
Biomet‚Äôs free cash flow has increased pretty steadily over the past several
years, so let‚Äôs use Ôú§Ôú±Ôú∏Ôú∞million as our estimated amount for Ôú≤Ôú∞Ôú∞Ôú¥. (Biomet‚Äôs
historical free cash flows are shown in Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú∏.)
If we increase free cash flow at Ôú±Ôúµpercent over the next five years and con-
servatively assume that Biomet starts to lose market share and grow more
slowly after five years, we see that the present value of the free cash that Biomet
will generate over the next Ôú±Ôú∞years is about Ôú§Ôú≤billion. (I used a relatively low
discount rate of Ôúπpercent‚Äîversus a market average of Ôú±Ôú∞.Ôúµpercent‚Äîbecause
Biomet is a very financially stable company.) Add in our perpetuity value of
about Ôú§Ôú≥.Ôúµbillion, and Biomet is worth Ôú§Ôúµ.Ôúµbillion, or about Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú±per share (Fig-
ure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú±Ôú¥runs through these calculations for you).
With the stock trading at about Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∏as of this writing, Biomet doesn‚Äôt look
like much of a value under this set of assumptions. However, maybe we‚Äôre
being too conservative by forecasting that a competitor will start eating Bio-
met‚Äôs lunch in just five years. Moreover, it might not be reasonable to assume
that a firm in an industry as young and robust as orthopedic devices will be
growing at just Ôú≥percent after a decade. That‚Äôs a really low growth rate‚Äîin
line with the overall economy‚Äîand it‚Äôs entirely possible that Biomet will still
be growing at an above-average rate in Ôú±Ôú∞years‚Äô time. Therefore, let‚Äôs try an-
other scenario. We‚Äôll assume Biomet can grow faster starting in year six, and

Ôú±Ôú∑Ôú¥
Ôù∞ÔùµÔù¥Ôù¥Ôù©ÔùÆÔùß Ôù©Ôù¥ Ôù°Ôù¨Ôù¨ Ôù¥ÔùØÔùßÔù•Ôù¥Ôù®Ôù•Ôù≤
we‚Äôll push our forecast horizon out to Ôú±Ôúµyears, at which time we assume that
Biomet‚Äôs growth rate declines to a steady state of Ôú≥percent (see Figure Ôú±Ôú±.Ôú±Ôúµ).
This set of assumptions results in an estimated intrinsic value per share of
about Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞, which is right around where the shares are trading as of this writing.
Assuming this is a reasonable scenario, I‚Äôd start getting interested in the stock
at around Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú¥, which would be a Ôú≤Ôú∞percent discount to my estimated intrinsic
value. I‚Äôm not looking for much of a margin of safety because Biomet‚Äôs strong
balance sheet, excellent industry prospects, and solid profitability all make it
less likely that something will go horribly wrong with my assumptions.
However, this process has taught us something very important: For us to
believe that Biomet‚Äôs shares are worth Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞, rather than Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∞, we have to believe
that the firm can hold off its competition and grow at an above-average rate
for a long period of time. Companies that can increase free cash flow at an av-
erage annual rate of Ôú±Ôú≤percent for a lengthy period of time‚Äîwhich is what
our second scenario assumes‚Äîare few and far between, after all.
This is the key benefit of a discounted cash flow approach to valuation.
Having thought through a couple of possible scenarios for Biomet, we now
know exactly what assumptions are incorporated in our estimated intrinsic
value of Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞per share. Armed with that knowledge, we can make a more in-
formed investment decision‚Äîwe wouldn‚Äôt know as much about the assump-
tions needed to believe that the stock is reasonably valued if we‚Äôd just looked at
the current P/E relative to the historical average P/E. For example, we should
probably pay very close attention to Biomet‚Äôs competitive position relative to
other firms in the industry, as well as to any signs that the long-term demand
for orthopedic devices might be slowing, because those two factors are what
moved our value estimate from Ôú§Ôú≤Ôú∞to Ôú§Ôú≥Ôú∞.
